تَصْدِيرُ الطَّبْعَةِ الأولى
كانت الفكرة السائدة لدى الباحثين - شرقيين وأوروبيين - أن المنطق الارسططاليسي قوبل في العالم الإسلامي - حين ترجم وتوالت تراجمه - أحسن مقابلة . فسرعان ما اعتبرته المدارس الإسلامية - على اختلاف نزعاتها وتباين أغراضها - قانون العقل الذي لا يرد ، والمنهج العلمي الثابت . تعاريفه وحدوده ثابتة . وأحكامه وقضاياه مسلمة . وأقيسته منتجة لليقين وموصلة إلى العلم من حيث هو . ومن ثمة نشأت تلك الفكرة القائلة : إن المنطق الأرسططاليسي أميز مثال « للفتنة اليونانية » التي افتتن بها المسلمون ، والتي سيطرت على عقولهم حتى العصور القريبة . وبهذا حل الباحثون مشكلة المنهج في العالم الإسلامي . إن هذا المنهج كان أرسططاليسياً ، إن في كلياته وإن في تفصيلاته .
وقفت أمام هذه الفكرة - بعد دراسة عميقة للمنطق الارسططاليسي - موقف الشك والارتياب إن هذا المنطق هو منهج البحث في علوم اليونان الفكرية والفلسفية منها على الخصوص . يتصل بها أوثق اتصال وتختلط أبحاثه وتتشابك بأبحاثها . ثم إن هذا المنطق هو أدق تعبير عن الروح اليونانية في نظرتها إلى الكون وفي محاولتها إقامة مذاهبها في الوجود . وقد لفظ الإسلام علوم اليونان الفكرية لفظاً قاسياً . وحاربها أشد محاربة - كانت الروح
الإسلامية تستمد مقوماتها من بيئة مخالفة وجنس مخالف وتصور حضاري جديد . وتنأى أشد النأي عن النظر في العوالم اليونانية الفكرية من ميتافيزيقا وفيزيقا وغيرهما ـ فكان من المحتم أن يكون لها منهج في البحث مختلف أشد الاختلاف عن منهج اليونان تستمد مقوماته من حضارتها العلمية ، بحيث يكون طابع تلك الحضارة الاساسي وجوهرها الوحيد .
بدأت البحث في التراث المنهجي للعالم الإسلامي . وحاولت الكشف عن نتاج العبقرية الإسلامية في التوصل إلى المنهج لا في كتب من يدعون « فلاسفة الإسلام » وهم دوائر منفصلة منعزلة عن تيار الفكر الإسلامي العام . بل في كتب ممثلي الإسلام الحقيقيين من فقهاء وأصوليين ومتكلمين وغيرهم من مفكرين مسلمين ـ وقد توصلت خلال شذرات متفرقة أشد التفرق أحياناً ـ وخلال كتابات يعوزها التحليل والتركيب في الكتب العربية القديمة أحياناً أخرى إلى إقامة المنهج الإسلامي كمنهج علمي ثابت . ولقد أيقنت أنني أمام أعظم كشف عرفه العالم الأوروبي فيما بعد اكتشاف المنهج التجريبي في العالم الإسلامي في أكمل صوره . هذا علاوة على أبحاث أخرى هامة ، سبق المسلمون الأوروبيين المحدثين فيها أيضاً سبقاً تاماً .
وإن هذا البحث الذي أعرضه للقارىء هو تاريخ تلك الحركة الفكرية التي سادت العالم الإسلامي ، والتي أدت الى الكشف عن هذا المنهج . وقد كان هذا البحث موضوع رسالتي للحصول على الماجستير في مايو ١٩٤٢ وإني أقدمه للقارىء ـ كما هو بدون ما تغيير ولا تبديل .
وإني لأبعث ـ بمناسبة طبع بحثي هذا ـ إلى تلك الروح الممتازة الوديعة . . . روح أستاذي الجليل مصطفى عبد الرازق ـ آيات حبي وولائي . وأذكر مقدار تشجيعه لي في كتابة هذا البحث ـ أما وقد فصل الموت بيني وبينه ـ وكنت أقرب تلامذته اليه وأخلصهم ـ فإني سأذكر على الدوام تلك
Preface to the First Edition. The prevailing idea among researchers - Eastern and European - was that Aristotelian logic was received in the Islamic world - when it was translated and its translations multiplied - in the best reception. Soon Islamic schools - despite their different inclinations and diverse purposes - considered it the law of reason that cannot be rejected, and the fixed scientific method. Its definitions and limits are fixed. Its judgments and propositions are accepted. Its syllogisms are productive of certainty and lead to knowledge as such. Hence arose that idea: that Aristotelian logic is the finest example of the 'Greek seduction' that Muslims were seduced by, and which dominated their minds until recent times. Thus researchers solved the problem of method in the Islamic world. This method was Aristotelian, both in its universals and in its details. I stood before this idea - after a deep study of Aristotelian logic - in a position of doubt and suspicion. This logic is the method of research in the intellectual sciences of the Greeks, especially the philosophical ones. It is most closely connected to them and its researches are mixed and intertwined with theirs. Moreover, this logic is the most precise expression of the Greek spirit in its view of the universe and in its attempt to establish its doctrines about existence. Islam rejected the intellectual sciences of the Greeks harshly. It fought them most severely - the Islamic spirit derived its components from a different environment, a different race, and a new civilizational conception. It shunned most severely any consideration of the Greek intellectual worlds - metaphysics, physics, and others - so it was inevitable that it should have a method of research most different from the Greek method, deriving its components from its own scientific civilization, such that the fundamental character of that civilization and its sole essence would be its imprint. I began research into the methodological heritage of the Islamic world. I attempted to uncover the product of Islamic genius in arriving at the method, not in the books of those called the 'Philosophers of Islam' - who are separate isolated circles from the general current of Islamic thought - but in the books of the true representatives of Islam: jurists, usul scholars, theologians, and other Muslim thinkers. I arrived, through scattered fragments - sometimes most scattered - and through writings lacking analysis and synthesis in old Arabic books at other times, at establishing the Islamic methodology as a fixed scientific method. I became convinced that I was before the greatest discovery that the European world would later know: the discovery of the empirical method in the Islamic world in its most complete form. This is in addition to other important researches, in which Muslims completely preceded modern Europeans. This research that I present to the reader is the history of that intellectual movement that prevailed in the Islamic world, and which led to the discovery of this method. This research was the subject of my thesis for the Master's degree in May 1942, and I present it to the reader as it is, without any change or alteration. And I send, on the occasion of printing this research of mine, to that excellent gentle spirit... the spirit of my great teacher Mustafa Abd al-Raziq - verses of my love and loyalty. And I recall his encouragement to me in writing this research - but since death has separated me from him - and I was his closest and most sincere student - I will forever remember those
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage is the preface (tashdir) to the first edition of the book. The author begins by stating the prevailing scholarly view that Aristotelian logic was warmly received in the Islamic world, quickly adopted by Islamic schools as the unchallengeable law of reason and fixed scientific method. Its definitions, propositions, and syllogisms were taken as certain and productive of knowledge. This gave rise to the idea that Aristotelian logic is the prime example of the 'Greek seduction' that dominated Muslim minds until recent times. Scholars thus solved the problem of method in Islam by asserting it was Aristotelian in both universals and details.

The author then reveals his stance: after deep study, he stood in doubt and suspicion. He argues that this logic is inseparably tied to Greek intellectual and philosophical sciences, their spirit, and their worldview. Islam, however, harshly rejected Greek intellectual sciences because the Islamic spirit derived from a different environment, race, and civilizational vision. Therefore, Islam must have its own distinct method of research, sourced from its own scientific civilization, constituting its fundamental character.

The author describes his research journey into the methodological heritage of the Islamic world. He sought the Islamic method not in the works of the so-called 'Philosophers of Islam' (who were isolated from mainstream Islamic thought) but in the books of true representatives: jurists, usul scholars, theologians, and other Muslim thinkers. Through scattered fragments and writings lacking analysis, he arrived at establishing a fixed Islamic scientific method. He believed this was the greatest discovery later known to the European world as the empirical method in its complete form, and that Muslims preceded modern Europeans in other important researches.

Finally, he states that this book is the history of that intellectual movement leading to the discovery of this method, originally his Master's thesis in 1942, presented unchanged. He dedicates the edition to his teacher Mustafa Abd al-Raziq, expressing love and loyalty, and promises to always remember his encouragement.

Key Terms
المنطق الارسططاليسي
المنهج الإسلامي
المنهج التجريبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The author's central claim is that Islamic civilization developed its own distinct empirical methodology, later misattributed to Greek influence, and that this method is found in the works of jurists, theologians, and usul scholars.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Islamic Methodology vs Greek Thought
Author argues for an indigenous Islamic research methodology distinct from Greek philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of the author's argument for an indigenous Islamic research methodology and distinguish it from the Greek method.

Core Idea
Islamic methodology is not a copy of Greek logic but an original product of Islamic civilization.

In this passage, the author sets the stage for his entire work by declaring that Islamic civilization developed its own unique method of research, independent of Greek philosophy. He explains why this must be so, and he identifies the true sources of this method: not the so-called "philosophers of Islam," but the jurists, jurisprudents, and theologians. Your task is to extract his central claim and understand how he supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim
Read the first few lines: the author states that Islamic civilization has its own constituents—environment, race, vision—so it must have its own method. What is the core assertion about the relationship between Islamic and Greek methods?
2
Find the sources he rejects
The author explicitly rejects the works of those he calls 'the philosophers of Islam' (falasifat al-Islam). Why does he reject them? What does he say about their connection to mainstream Islamic thought?
3
Identify the sources he accepts
He turns instead to the 'true representatives of Islam': jurists, usuliyyun, mutakallimun. Why are these groups more reliable for discovering an Islamic method?
4
Assess his evidence
The author admits his evidence comes from 'scattered fragments' and writings lacking analysis. Does this weaken his argument? How does he overcome this limitation?
5
Evaluate the claim about empirical method
He claims the Islamic method is the 'greatest discovery' that Europe later knew—the empirical method. Is this a historical claim or a logical one? How would you test it?

الإسلامية تستمد مقوماتها من بيئة مخالفة وجنس مخالف وتصور حضاري جديد . وتنأى أشد النأي عن النظر في العوالم اليونانية الفكرية من ميتافيزيقا وفيزيقا وغيرهما ـ فكان من المحتم أن يكون لها منهج في البحث مختلف أشد الاختلاف عن منهج اليونان تستمد مقوماته من حضارتها العلمية ، بحيث يكون طابع تلك الحضارة الاساسي وجوهرها الوحيد . بدأت البحث في التراث المنهجي للعالم الإسلامي . وحاولت الكشف عن نتاج العبقرية الإسلامية في التوصل إلى المنهج لا في كتب من يدعون « فلاسفة الإسلام » وهم دوائر منفصلة منعزلة عن تيار الفكر الإسلامي العام . بل في كتب ممثلي الإسلام الحقيقيين من فقهاء وأصوليين ومتكلمين وغيرهم من مفكرين مسلمين ـ وقد توصلت خلال شذرات متفرقة أشد التفرق أحياناً ـ وخلال كتابات يعوزها التحليل والتركيب في الكتب العربية القديمة أحياناً أخرى إلى إقامة المنهج الإسلامي كمنهج علمي ثابت . ولقد أيقنت أنني أمام أعظم كشف عرفه العالم الأوروبي فيما بعد اكتشاف المنهج التجريبي في العالم الإسلامي في أكمل صوره . هذا علاوة على أبحاث أخرى هامة ، سبق المسلمون الأوروبيين المحدثين فيها أيضاً سبقاً تاماً . وإن هذا البحث الذي أعرضه للقارىء هو تاريخ تلك الحركة الفكرية التي سادت العالم الإسلامي ، والتي أدت الى الكشف عن هذا المنهج . وقد كان هذا البحث موضوع رسالتي للحصول على الماجستير في مايو ١٩٤٢ وإني أقدمه للقارىء ـ كما هو بدون ما تغيير ولا تبديل .
Islamic civilization derives its constituents from a different environment, a different race, and a new civilizational vision. It distances itself greatly from consideration of the Greek intellectual worlds—metaphysics, physics, and others—so it was inevitable that it would have a method of research vastly different from the Greek method, deriving its constituents from its own scientific civilization, such that it becomes the basic character and sole essence of that civilization. I began research in the methodological heritage of the Islamic world. I attempted to uncover the product of Islamic genius in arriving at the method, not in the books of those who claim to be 'the philosophers of Islam'—who are separate circles isolated from the general current of Islamic thought—but rather in the books of the true representatives of Islam: jurists, jurisprudents (usuliyyun), theologians (mutakallimun), and other Muslim thinkers. Through scattered fragments—sometimes extremely scattered—and through writings lacking analysis and synthesis in old Arabic books at other times, I arrived at establishing the Islamic method as a fixed scientific method. And I became certain that I was before the greatest discovery that the European world would later know: the discovery of the empirical method in the Islamic world in its most complete form. This is in addition to other important research in which Muslims also completely preceded modern Europeans. And this research that I present to the reader is the history of that intellectual movement that dominated the Islamic world and led to the discovery of this method. This research was the subject of my thesis for the master's degree in May 1942, and I present it to the reader as it is, without any change or alteration.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage serves as an introduction to the author's work, where he outlines his thesis: that Islamic civilization developed its own distinct research methodology, independent of Greek thought. The author begins by emphasizing the unique environment and worldview of Islamic civilization, which naturally led to a different method of inquiry. He distances Islamic methodology from Greek metaphysics and physics, arguing that the Islamic method is rooted in its own scientific heritage. The author then explains his research approach: he did not rely on the works of those labeled 'philosophers of Islam,' whom he dismisses as disconnected from mainstream Islamic thought. Instead, he turned to the writings of genuine Islamic scholars—jurists, usul al-fiqh experts, theologians, and others. Through scattered references and sometimes underdeveloped works, he reconstructed what he calls the 'Islamic method' as a coherent scientific method. He even claims that this method predates and surpasses the European empirical method. The passage concludes by identifying the present book as the historical chronicle of that intellectual movement and notes that it was originally his master's thesis, presented without modification.

Key Takeaway
The author asserts that Islamic civilization developed its own unique research methodology, independent of and prior to the European empirical method, and he locates this method in the works of jurists, theologians, and usul al-fiqh scholars rather than in the Greek-influenced philosophers.

Navigating the Table of Contents: Overview of Manahij al-Bahth
Author's dedication and full table of contents of the book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to navigate the table of contents of a modern scholarly work on Islamic logic and identify its major sections and topics.

Core Idea
A table of contents is a roadmap: it reveals the author's plan and the book's logical flow.

This lesson introduces the table of contents of 'Manahij al-Bahth', a modern academic work on Aristotelian logic in the Islamic world. The table of contents shows how the book progresses from the transmission of logic to the Islamic world, to the study of concepts (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat), and finally to reasoning methods. Understanding this structure will help you locate specific topics and see the big picture.

Guided Path
1
Examine the preface and dedication
The author dedicates the book to his teacher, expressing gratitude. What does this suggest about the author's intellectual lineage and the scholarly tradition he is part of?
2
Identify the major parts
The book is divided into an introduction and Part One. What is the introduction about? (Transmission of Aristotelian logic to the Islamic world). Part One covers Aristotelian logic as treated by Islamic commentators.
3
Break down Part One into chapters
Part One has three chapters: General issues of logic, Study of concepts (tasawwurat), Study of assents (tasdiqat). Under Concepts, note the subsections: expressions, meanings, categories.
4
Note the reasoning methods listed
Under Assents, the section on reasoning lists four methods: syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), analogy (tamthil), and Platonic division (qisma afalatuniyya). How do these relate to the Islamic logical tradition?
5
Relate the structure to Islamic sciences
This book is about the history of logic in the Islamic world. How does understanding the book's structure help in studying Islamic logic? For example, if you want to study syllogism, you know to look under 'Mabahith al-tasdiqat'.

Commentary
This passage contains the author's dedication and the book's table of contents. The dedication expresses gratitude to a teacher for his knowledge and character. The table of contents outlines the structure of the book, which is a modern academic work on the history of Aristotelian logic in the Islamic world. It begins with an introduction on the transmission of logic to Muslims. Part One covers Aristotelian logic as treated by Islamic commentators and summarizers, with chapters on general issues of logic, concepts (tasawwurat) subdivided into expressions, meanings, and categories, and assents (tasdiqat) subdivided into propositions and reasoning. The reasoning section includes syllogism, induction, analogy, and Platonic division. This table provides an overview of the topics to be studied, all rooted in the Islamic intellectual tradition of logic.

The Two Currents of Islamic Logic: Aristotelian and Usuli
Table of contents showing overall structure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the main topics of Aristotelian logic as presented in Part One from the Islamic legal reasoning methods in Part Two.

Core Idea
Islamic logic is not a single system; it includes both appropriated Greek logic and indigenous juristic reasoning.

The table of contents of Manahij al-Bahth reveals a clear structural contrast: Part One presents the Aristotelian logical apparatus as developed by Islamic commentators, while Part Two examines how Islamic legal theorists and theologians evaluated and transformed that apparatus. This lesson helps you navigate the overall landscape.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two parts
Find the headings 'الباب الأول' (Part One) and 'الباب الثاني' (Part Two) in the Arabic TOC. Notice how they frame the book.
The page numbers indicate the start of each part.
2
Scan Part One chapter headings
Identify the main branches of logic: Chapter Two on Concepts (التصورات) and Chapter Three on Assents (التصديقات). These are the two pillars of Aristotelian logic as taught in Islam.
3
Scan Part Two chapters
Notice the shift: chapters on the position of usulis and mutakallimun, then usuli definition, usuli qiyas, other Islamic methods, and finally critique of Aristotelian laws of thought.
The sequence shows an increasing independence from Greek logic.
4
Compare types of reasoning
Part One mentions syllogism (قياس), induction (استقراء), analogy (تمثيل). Part Two focuses on qiyas usuli (legal analogy based on 'illa) and alternative methods like 'analogy of the unseen to the witness'.
The term 'qiyas' in Arabic can refer to both Aristotelian syllogism and legal analogy, but the context distinguishes them.

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents from the book 'Manahij Bahth'. It presents the overall structure of the work in two parts. Part One covers Aristotelian logic as transmitted to and developed by Islamic commentators, with chapters on general issues, concepts (words, meanings, categories), and assents (propositions, inference including syllogism, induction, analogy, and Platonic division). Part Two focuses on the response of Islamic legal theorists (Usulis) and theologians (Mutakallimun) to Aristotelian logic up to the fifth century AH. It details chapters on the Usuli definition, the Usuli analogical reasoning (including discussions on cause, its conditions and paths), other Islamic methods of inference (such as analogy of the unseen to the witness, and imposition), and finally a critique of the Aristotelian laws of thought: non-contradiction and excluded middle. The page numbers indicate the location of each section within the book. This overview helps the reader understand the scope and sequence of arguments presented in the work.

Key Terms
المنطق الأرسططاليسي
القياس الأصولي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the table of contents, which part of the book contains chapters primarily on Islamic legal inference methods?
A) Part One
B) Part Two
C) General Introduction
D) Foreword

Key Takeaway
The book's structure mirrors the historical interaction: Islamic scholars first received and summarized Aristotelian logic (Part I), then critically engaged and developed alternative methods (Part II).

?

?

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
8 min
The Building Blocks of Aristotelian Logic in Islamic Commentaries
Part One structure of Aristotelian logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main subdivisions of Aristotelian logic as reflected in Part One of Manahij al-Bahth: concepts, propositions, and inference.

Core Idea
Islamic logicians divided Aristotelian logic into tasawwur and tasdiq, mirroring the Arabic translation tradition's structure.

Part One of the TOC outlines the standard arrangement of Aristotelian logic as taught in Islamic madrasas: general issues, then concepts (including words, meanings, categories), then assents (propositions and inference). Understanding this skeleton is crucial for reading any Islamic logical text.

Guided Path
1
Read Chapter Two heading
Find 'مبحث التصورات' (The Section on Concepts). Note its three subsections: words, meanings, categories.
These correspond to the logical study of terms, concepts, and the highest genera.
2
Read Chapter Three heading
Find 'مبحث التصديقات' (The Section on Assents). It has two subsections: propositions and inference.
Propositions are the building blocks of reasoning; inference is the process of deriving new knowledge.
3
List the four types of inference
Under inference, the TOC lists: syllogism (القياس), induction (الإستقراء), analogy (التمثيل), and Platonic division (القسمة الأفلاطونية).
Platonic division is an unusual inclusion; it was a method of classification.
4
Explain why categories are under concepts
Categories (المقولات) are the highest genera under which all concepts fall; they are part of conceptual analysis.
This follows Aristotle's Organon where Categories is the first text.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
12 min
Islamic Responses to Greek Logic: From Acceptance to Critique
Part Two structure showing Islamic engagement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the arc of the Islamic engagement with Aristotelian logic as shown in Part Two, from legal appropriation to theological critique.

Core Idea
Islamic scholars did not simply accept Greek logic; they adapted it for jurisprudence and eventually critiqued its metaphysical foundations.

Part Two of the TOC traces a dramatic narrative: first, usul al-fiqh scholars cautiously integrated logical methods (chapters 1-4), then they developed distinct Islamic inference methods (chapter 5), and finally theologians challenged the very laws of Aristotelian thought (chapter 6). This lesson shows how the TOC itself tells this story.

Guided Path
1
Note the sequence of chapters in Part Two
The chapters progress from positions of usulis/mutakallimun, to usuli definition, to usuli qiyas, to other Islamic methods, to critique of Aristotelian laws.
This reflects a movement from initial contact to creative development to critical evaluation.
2
Find the discussion of 'cause' ('illa)
In Chapter Four, the TOC lists 'Schools of Muslims on the Cause', 'Conditions of the Cause', and 'Paths of the Cause'. The cause is the central element of usuli qiyas.
The paths include sabr wa taqsim, tard, dawaran, tanqih al-manat – juristic methods for identifying the cause.
3
Identify alternative Islamic methods in Chapter Five
Methods like 'Analogy of the Unseen to the Witness' and 'Production of Premises to Conclusions' are distinct from Aristotelian syllogism.
These reflect reasoning patterns from the Quran and early legal practice, not from Aristotle.
4
Examine Chapter Six on critique
Chapter Six critiques the law of non-contradiction and law of excluded middle. The subsection 'Section on the State' (Mabhas al-Hal) hints at the ontological debate about intermediate states.
These critiques were part of the kalam tradition, particularly by the Mu'tazila and Ash'ariyya.

This passage is a table of contents from the book 'Manahij Bahth'. It presents the overall structure of the work in two parts. Part One covers Aristotelian logic as transmitted to and developed by Islamic commentators, with chapters on general issues, concepts (words, meanings, categories), and assents (propositions, inference including syllogism, induction, analogy, and Platonic division). Part Two focuses on the response of Islamic legal theorists (Usulis) and theologians (Mutakallimun) to Aristotelian logic up to the fifth century AH. It details chapters on the Usuli definition, the Usuli analogical reasoning (including discussions on cause, its conditions and paths), other Islamic methods of inference (such as analogy of the unseen to the witness, and imposition), and finally a critique of the Aristotelian laws of thought: non-contradiction and excluded middle. The page numbers indicate the location of each section within the book. This overview helps the reader understand the scope and sequence of arguments presented in the work.

Mapping the Islamic Engagement with Aristotelian Logic
Lists chapters and sections on Islamic scholars' stance towards Aristotelian logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main topics and historical progression of Islamic scholars' engagement with Aristotelian logic as outlined in the table of contents.

Core Idea
The table of contents reveals the trajectory from stances to constructive logic to critique.

This passage is a roadmap for the second part of a modern study examining how Islamic scholars (usuliyyun and mutakallimun) interacted with Aristotelian logic up to the fifth century AH. By parsing this table of contents, you will gain a structured overview of the key debates, constructive developments, and critiques that shaped Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scholarly groups
The passage mentions 'usuliyyun' (scholars of usul al-fiqh) and 'mutakallimun' (theologians). Note their distinct treatments in Chapters One and Two.
These groups had different relationships with Aristotelian logic.
2
Trace the sequence of topics
The chapters move from stances (chapters 1-2) to constructive topics (chapters 3-5: definition, inference, other Islamic methods) and then to critiques (chapters 6-7) and finally synthesis (chapter 8 on al-Ghazali).
3
Locate the chapters on Islamic logic
Chapters Three, Four, and Five focus on what the authors call 'logic of usuliyyun' and 'Islamic inference' and 'other Islamic methods'. These represent attempts to construct an alternative or parallel logical framework.
4
Identify the critical chapters
Chapters Six and Seven are explicitly critical: they deal with the mutakallimun's critique of Aristotelian laws of thought (non-contradiction, excluded middle) and the law of causality.
5
Find al-Ghazali's role
Chapter Eight discusses the blending of Aristotelian logic with Islamic sciences through al-Ghazali and his own stance. This chapter is the last, indicating a synthesis.

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents for the second major part of a modern academic work on the relationship between Islamic scholars and Aristotelian logic up to the fifth century AH. It outlines eight chapters covering the positions of usul al-fiqh scholars, mutakallimun, the development of an 'usuli logic' focusing on definition and inference, and critiques of Aristotelian logical principles such as non-contradiction, excluded middle, and causality. It also notes the pivotal role of al-Ghazali in synthesizing Greek logic with Islamic sciences. The structure shows a systematic historical and analytical approach, moving from stances to doctrinal critiques.

Key Terms
الأصوليون (al-usuliyyun): Scholars specialized in usul al-fiqh (principles of jurisprudence) who engaged with logic. [Note: In classical texts, 'usuliyyun' usually refers to jurisprudential theorists; here it is used in a modern historical context.]
المنطق الأرسططاليسي (al-mantiq al-arisṭāṭālīsī): Aristotelian logic, the system of logic originating from Aristotle, as encountered by Islamic scholars. [Note: This term is used anachronistically in modern scholarship; classical Muslim logicians referred to 'al-mantiq al-arisṭī' or simply 'al-mantiq'.]
المتكلمون (al-mutakallimūn): Islamic theologians (theologians) who engaged in kalam, often critical of Aristotelian logic. [Note: In classical texts, 'mutakallimūn' refers to practitioners of 'ilm al-kalam; here it is used in a historical survey.]

Overview of Manahij al-Bahth: The Structure of the Critique of Aristotelian Logic
Table of contents outlining the book's critique of Aristotelian logic in Islamic thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main sections of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic as outlined in Manahij al-Bahth.

Core Idea
The book's structure mirrors Ibn Taymiyya's dual approach: destruction of Aristotelian logic and construction of an Islamic alternative.

This lesson introduces the table of contents of Manahij al-Bahth, a key text critiquing Aristotelian logic from an Islamic perspective. By understanding its structure, you will see how Ibn Taymiyya systematically deconstructs and then rebuilds the theory of definition, propositions, and inference. This overview will prepare you for detailed study of each chapter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three major parts of the book.
The TOC shows three parts: general critique of causality, al-Ghazali's role, and the position of jurists with Ibn Taymiyya's critique. Note that Parts 1 and 2 precede Part 3.
2
Locate the central section on Ibn Taymiyya.
Part Three (الباب الثالث) starts with the position of jurists up to Ibn Taymiyya, then details his critique in Chapters 2-4.
3
Distinguish the destructive and constructive aspects.
For each topic (definition, propositions, inferences), Ibn Taymiyya has a destructive critique (الجانب الهدمي) and a constructive proposal (الجانب الإنشائي). Find examples in the TOC.
4
Note the alternative inference methods.
In Chapter Four, the constructive aspect lists 'آيات' (verses) and 'قياس الأولى' (syllogism of the more appropriate) as Taymiyyan inference forms.

Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents from the book Manahij Bahth (Methods of Investigation), providing an overview of its structure. The book focuses on the critique of Aristotelian logic within Islamic thought, particularly highlighting the contributions of Ibn Taymiyya. The table is divided into chapters and sections. The first part covers general critiques of Aristotelian causality and al-Ghazali's integration of logic. The second part, which begins with 'Part Three', details the positions of jurists and Ibn Taymiyya's extensive critique. Key sections include the critique of definition (al-hadd), propositions (al-qadaya), and syllogisms (al-qiyas). Ibn Taymiyya's approach is split into a destructive aspect (naqd al-janib al-hadmi) and a constructive aspect (al-janib al-insha'i). The constructive aspect introduces alternative inference methods such as 'ayat' (verses) and 'qiyas al-awla' (syllogism of the more appropriate). Finally, the positions of later jurists like Ibn Qayyim and al-Suyuti are summarized. This structure shows that the book is a comprehensive study of Islamic epistemology in relation to Aristotelian logic.

Navigating Ibn Taymiyya's Critique through Its Table of Contents
Author lists chapter and section titles of the book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the destructive and constructive phases of Ibn Taymiyya's logical critique by analyzing the book's table of contents.

Core Idea
A table of contents reveals the structure of a logical argument before you read a single line.

The table of contents of 'Manahij al-Bahth' is not just a list of pages; it is a blueprint of Ibn Taymiyya's systematic deconstruction of Aristotelian logic and his construction of an Islamic alternative. By reading it carefully, you can trace the flow of his critique before diving into the details. In this lesson, you will learn to extract the main argumentative moves from the table of contents.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three main parts
Identify the sections on definitions (al-hadd), propositions (al-qadaya), and inferences (al-istidlalat). Notice they are treated in separate chapters, showing a systematic progression.
2
Distinguish destructive from constructive
For both definitions and inferences, there are separate 'destructive' (hadmi) and 'constructive' (insha'i) aspects. This indicates a two-phase critique: refuting Aristotle and then building an alternative.
3
Identify the alternatives proposed
Find the 'signs' (ayat) and 'analogy of the superior' (qiyas al-awla) under the constructive aspect. These are Ibn Taymiyya's positive proposals replacing Aristotelian forms.
4
Note the jurists' reception
The final chapters discuss how later jurists like Ibn Qayyim, al-San'ani, al-Suyuti, and al-Subki received Ibn Taymiyya's critique. This shows the impact and continuation of the debate.

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents of Manahij Bahth, outlining the book's structure. The first part covers Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian logic, focusing first on definitions (hadd), then on propositions (qadaya), and finally on syllogism (qiyas). It distinguishes between a 'destructive aspect' (al-janib al-hadmi) that refutes Aristotelian positions, and a 'constructive aspect' (al-janib al-insha'i) that offers Ibn Taymiyya's alternative methods, such as using signs (ayat) and analogy of the superior (qiyas al-awla). The latter part of the book discusses the position of jurists like Ibn Qayyim, al-San'ani, al-Suyuti, and al-Subki, and concludes with the Illuminationist (Ishraqi) school's approach combining logical and intuitive methods. This table of contents indicates that the book systematically deconstructs Aristotelian logic and presents an Islamic alternative rooted in Quranic reasoning and legal practice.

Chapter Four: Illuminationist Epistemology – Logical vs. Mystical Method
Chapter and section headings on Illuminationist epistemology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the logical method (al-manhaj al-mantiqi) and the mystical method (al-manhaj al-dhawqi) as outlined in the chapter heading.

Core Idea
Illuminationist thought contrasts discursive logic with direct spiritual insight.

This brief passage announces a major division in Islamic methodology: the logical approach of the philosophers and theologians versus the intuitive approach of the Illuminationists. As we begin Chapter Four, it is crucial to understand this distinction, as it frames the entire discussion of theoretical research methods.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chapter title
Read the Arabic: الباب الرابع: موقف الإشراقيين من طرق البحث النظرية. What is the chapter about? It is about the position of the Illuminationists on the methods of theoretical research.
2
Identify the first section
الفصل الأول : المنهج المنطقي والمنهج الذوقي. What two methods are contrasted? The logical method and the mystical method.
3
Define each method in your own words
Based on the key terms, the logical method is based on Aristotelian syllogistic reasoning; the mystical method is based on direct spiritual experience (dhawq).
4
Consider why this contrast matters
In Islamic intellectual history, these two methods often represent different epistemic priorities. The Illuminationists prioritize intuitive knowledge over discursive reasoning, which is a key epistemological stance.

Commentary
This passage is a table-of-contents entry from the work Manahij Bahth. The first section specifically contrasts the logical method (al-manhaj al-mantiqi) with the mystical method (al-manhaj al-dhawqi). This indicates that the author is about to engage in a comparative epistemological analysis, likely critiquing or distinguishing between rational deduction and intuitive or spiritual insight.

Key Takeaway
The Illuminationists distinguish between the logical method (deductive reasoning) and the mystical method (direct spiritual insight), favoring the latter for higher knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify in Suhrawardi's Hikmat al-Ishraq an instance where he privileges dhawq over discursive reasoning. Then state whether he would classify the famous proof from contingency (burhan al-imkan) as belonging to the logical or mystical method.

Historical Transmission of Aristotelian Logic to Islam
Historical introduction to the transmission of Aristotelian logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key historical factors that facilitated the transmission of Aristotelian logic to the Islamic world and the role of the Umayyad period.

Core Idea
Logic reached Muslims earlier than commonly thought, through Umayyad-era cultural intermingling.

This lesson traces how Aristotelian logic entered Islamic civilization. The author challenges the standard narrative that translation began only in the Abbasid period, pointing to earlier contact under the Umayyads through the intermingling of conquered Hellenistic peoples and Muslim conquerors. Understanding this history helps you appreciate why Islamic scholars engaged with Greek logic and how it shaped fields like kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard scholarly view
Find where the author states what 'researchers in the history of Islamic thought' typically point to as the starting point. Note that they refer to the Abbasid era and that they agree logic was the first Greek science known.
2
Locate the author's correction
The author says 'we cannot neglect' translation activity before the Abbasid period, specifically under the Umayyads. Underline the phrase 'فيما قبل العهد العباسي أي في عصر بني أمية'.
3
Analyze the causal factors
The author lists three factors: (1) stabilization of conquests in Hellenistic lands, (2) intermingling of peoples, (3) Islamic equality. The passage is cut off after mentioning the first factor in detail. Identify the third factor from the text: 'التسوية التامة بين معتنقيه سواء أكانوا عرباً أم عجماً'.
The third factor is given with its consequence: non-Arabs with knowledge of Greek sciences converted.
4
Connect to Islamic sciences
Consider how this early transmission influenced later Islamic disciplines. For example, the early exposure to logic through translation movements provided tools that later mutakallimun (theologians) used in arguments for divine attributes.
This step is forward-looking; the passage itself does not elaborate, but it sets the stage.

In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. General Introduction: The transmission of Aristotelian logic to the Islamic world. It is established that determining the beginning of Muslims' acquaintance with the Greek philosophical and scientific encyclopedia is extremely difficult, such that researchers in the history of Islamic thought suffice with pointing to the Abbasid era as the starting point of Muslims' knowledge of Greek philosophy and the transmission of that philosophy to the Islamic world. Despite their differences in specifying the reign of the Abbasid caliph in which this translation began in its true sense, they almost agree that the first thing Muslims knew from the Greek philosophical heritage was logic. While accepting this latter idea, we cannot neglect the movement of transmitting Greek philosophical sciences, and logic in particular, before the Abbasid era, i.e., in the Umayyad period (40-132 AH / 661-750 CE) and especially in its early part. As soon as the conquests stabilized in the lands dominated by the Hellenistic spirit – and this spirit dominated not only Egypt and Syria but also Iraq and Persia – the intermingling between the conquered peoples and the conquerors began. Several factors helped this, connected to the nature of Islam itself. The most important of these factors are: Islam's proclamation of complete equality among its adherents, whether Arab or non-Arab. This prompted many of the conquered peoples – who had a prior civilization, different customs, and knowledge of Greek sciences – to embrace
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a historical introduction discussing the transmission of Aristotelian logic to the Islamic world. The author begins by noting the difficulty in pinpointing exactly when Muslims first encountered Greek philosophy. Scholars typically refer to the Abbasid period as the starting point of translation activities. However, there is general agreement that logic was the first Greek philosophical science known to Muslims. The author then argues that translation activities actually began earlier, in the Umayyad period, albeit in a less organized form. The conquests brought Muslims into contact with Hellenistic-influenced regions like Egypt, Syria, Iraq, and Persia. This led to cultural intermingling, facilitated by factors inherent in Islam, such as the principle of equality among believers regardless of ethnicity. This equality motivated many non-Arabs with knowledge of Greek sciences to convert to Islam, thereby bringing their intellectual heritage into the Islamic sphere. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating that further factors were to be discussed.

The First Greek Science: Logic's Priority in Islamic Civilization
Author argues for Umayyad-era transmission of logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the claim that logic was the first Greek philosophical science known to Muslims and evaluate the evidence provided.

Core Idea
Logic entered the Islamic world earlier than the Abbasid translation movement.

This lesson examines a foundational claim in the history of Islamic logic: that Aristotelian logic was the first Greek philosophical science to reach Muslims. We'll explore the author's argument that this happened already in the Umayyad period, challenging the common focus on the Abbasid era.

Guided Path
1
Locate the central claim
Find where the author states that logic was the first Greek science known to Muslims. The passage says: 'the first thing Muslims knew of the Greek philosophical heritage was logic' (أول ما عرف المسلمون من تراث اليونان الفلسفي هو المنطق).
This claim is made twice in the passage.
2
Identify the author's revision
The author challenges the common scholarly narrative that focuses on the Abbasid era. He argues that the transmission began earlier in the Umayyad period. Note the phrase 'we cannot neglect the movement of transmitting Greek philosophical sciences... before the Abbasid era'.
This is a historiographical correction.
3
Find the supporting evidence
The author provides two pieces of evidence: (a) Ibn Kathir's statement that 'the sciences of the ancients entered the lands of the Muslims in the first century' and (b) a passage from al-Shirazi quoting al-Suhrawardi.
The second is mentioned but not quoted fully.
4
Assess the strength of evidence
Consider whether these sources constitute strong proof. Ibn Kathir is a later historian; al-Shirazi is even later. The author himself notes that the transmission was limited and not widely adopted. Evaluate the reliability of the sources.
Historical arguments often rely on later testimonies; direct evidence from the Umayyad period is scarce.

In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. General Introduction. The Transmission of Aristotelian Logic to the Islamic World. It is established that determining the beginning of Muslims' acquaintance with the circle of Greek philosophical and scientific knowledge is extremely difficult, such that researchers in the history of Islamic thought suffice with pointing to the Abbasid era as the starting point of Muslims' knowledge of Greek philosophy and the transmission of that philosophy to the Islamic world. Despite their disagreement in determining which Abbasid caliph's reign this translation began in its true sense, they almost agree that the first thing Muslims knew of the Greek philosophical heritage was logic. While accepting this latter idea, we cannot neglect the movement of transmitting Greek philosophical sciences, and logic specifically, before the Abbasid era, i.e., in the Umayyad period (40-132 AH / 661-750 CE), and especially at its beginning. As soon as the conquests stabilized in the lands dominated by the Hellenistic spirit—and this spirit dominated not only Egypt and Syria but also Iraq and Persia—the intermingling between the conquered nations and the conquerors began. Several factors related to the nature of Islam itself helped this. The most important of these factors are: Islam's declaration of complete equality among its adherents, whether they were Arabs or non-Arabs. This called many of the conquered nations—who had previous civilizations, different customs, and knowledge of Greek sciences—to embrace Islam. Islam was not a closed religion; rather, the Islamic world quickly opened to everyone entering it. We will later see one of the Umayyad caliphs, among the Umayyads having a blind pre-Islamic spirit, becoming distressed when he heard that the majority of the hadith scholars and jurists contemporary to him were mawali, i.e., of Persian origin. Intellectual freedom was a characteristic of Islamic rule in the conquered lands. This freedom called many of the sons of the conquered nations to present their opinions and beliefs, and even to debate Muslims regarding their doctrines. The forms of responses differed between the early Muslims of pure Arab stock and the later Muslims of the first generation who were of mixed lineage and had a composite mentality. Then a number of these latter Muslims turned to this Greek science and attempted to acquaint themselves with it (even though they did not adopt anything from it—as we will see later). It is not surprising that some historians of early kalam mention that the early mutakallimun, like the followers of Wasil ibn 'Ata' (133 AH / 728 CE), read the books of the philosophers (1). In any case, Greek philosophy reached the Islamic world through this intermingling during the Umayyad period. There is evidence to prove this: (a) What Ibn Kathir mentions: 'The sciences of the ancients entered the lands of the Muslims in the first century when they conquered the lands of the non-Arabs, but they were not abundant among them nor did they spread, because the predecessors forbade delving into them' (2). What is meant by 'sciences of the ancients' here is Greek philosophical sciences. (b) Clarifying this text further is a passage from the book 'The Four Journeys' by Sadr al-Din al-Shirazi, which he transmitted from al-Mutarahat by al-Suhrawardi, specifically concerning the sources of doctrines.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage from the introductory chapter of Manahij Bahth presents a historical account of the transmission of Aristotelian logic to the Islamic world, with a focus on the pre-Abbasid period under the Umayyads. The author begins by noting the difficulty in pinpointing the exact beginning of Muslims' acquaintance with Greek philosophy, as scholars typically point to the Abbasid era. However, he emphasizes that there is consensus that logic was the first Greek philosophical science known to Muslims. He then argues that the transmission movement cannot be overlooked even before the Abbasid period, specifically during the Umayyad dynasty (40-132 AH / 661-750 CE). The author explains how the stabilization of Islamic conquests in Hellenistic regions (Egypt, Syria, Iraq, Persia) led to cultural intermingling between the conquered peoples and the Arab conquerors. Several factors stemming from Islam itself facilitated this fusion: first, Islam's principle of equality among all Muslims, Arab and non-Arab alike, which encouraged many from the conquered nations—who had prior knowledge of Greek sciences—to convert. Second, Islam was not a closed religion but open to all. The author notes a later Umayyad caliph who was distressed that most scholars of hadith and fiqh were of Persian origin (mawali). Third, intellectual freedom under Islamic rule allowed the conquered peoples to present their beliefs and debate with Muslims. The early Arab Muslims and later mixed-heritage Muslims responded differently. Consequently, some Muslims of later generations turned to Greek sciences to study them, though they did not adopt them. The author cites that early mutakallimun like the followers of Wasil ibn 'Ata' read philosophers' books. Finally, the author asserts that Greek philosophy did reach the Islamic world during the Umayyad period and provides two pieces of evidence: (a) Ibn Kathir's statement that the sciences of the ancients (Greek philosophy) entered Muslim lands in the first century but were not widespread due to opposition from the early pious predecessors; (b) a passage from Sadr al-Din al-Shirazi's book 'The Four Journeys', quoting al-Suhrawardi's 'al-Mutarahat' regarding sources of doctrines. The passage sets the historical context for the study of logic in the Islamic world, emphasizing that its entry began earlier than often recognized.

Factors of Intellectual Fusion: Islam and Hellenistic Culture
Author identifies factors facilitating Greek knowledge transmission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the factors that facilitated the transmission of Greek logic to the Islamic world, and explain how each contributed.

Core Idea
Islam's principles of equality and openness, combined with intellectual freedom, enabled the absorption of Greek sciences.

The author attributes the early transmission of logic not merely to translation efforts, but to deeper social and religious factors. This lesson unpacks those factors: Islamic equality, openness of the religion, and intellectual freedom under Muslim rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three factors
Read the passage and list the factors the author mentions: (1) Islam's declaration of complete equality among all Muslims, (2) Islam not being a closed religion, (3) intellectual freedom in the conquered lands.
The author considers these the most important factors.
2
Explain how equality helped
Equality encouraged non-Arabs (who had knowledge of Greek sciences) to embrace Islam. The author notes that many mawali (non-Arab converts) became scholars. This brought Greek knowledge into the Muslim community.
The author mentions a later Umayyad caliph who was disturbed by this.
3
Explain how openness helped
Islam was not closed to newcomers; the Islamic world quickly opened to everyone. This allowed the integration of diverse cultures and ideas.
This contrasts with some exclusivist religions.
4
Explain how intellectual freedom helped
Intellectual freedom allowed conquered peoples to present their beliefs and debate with Muslims. This created an environment where Greek ideas could be discussed and evaluated.
The author mentions that early mutakallimun read philosophers' books.

وليس من المستغرب أن يذكر بعض مؤرخي الفكر الكلامي الأول أن المتكلمين الأول أمثال أصحاب واصل بن عطاء ( ١٣٣هـ ـ ٧٢٨م ) طالعوا كتب الفلاسفة(١) .
وعلى أية حال لقد وصل خلال هذا التزاوج إلى العالم الإسلامي وفي عصر بني أمية فلسفة اليونان . وهناك من الدلائل ما يثبت هذا .
( أ ) ما يذكره ابن كثير من « أن علوم الأوائل دخلت إلى بلاد المسلمين في القرن الأول لما فتحوا بلاد الأعاجم لكنها لم تكثر فيهم ولم تنتشر ، لما كان السلف يمنعون من الخوض فيها »(٢) . والمقصود بعلوم الأوائل هنا : العلوم الفلسفية اليونانية .
(ب) ويزيد هذا النص توضيحاً فقرة من كتاب الأسفار الأربعة لصدر الدين الشيرازي نقلها عن المطارحات للسهروردي خاصة بمصادر مذاهب
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. General Introduction. The Transmission of Aristotelian Logic to the Islamic World. It is established that determining the beginning of Muslims' acquaintance with the circle of Greek philosophical and scientific knowledge is extremely difficult, such that researchers in the history of Islamic thought suffice with pointing to the Abbasid era as the starting point of Muslims' knowledge of Greek philosophy and the transmission of that philosophy to the Islamic world. Despite their disagreement in determining which Abbasid caliph's reign this translation began in its true sense, they almost agree that the first thing Muslims knew of the Greek philosophical heritage was logic. While accepting this latter idea, we cannot neglect the movement of transmitting Greek philosophical sciences, and logic specifically, before the Abbasid era, i.e., in the Umayyad period (40-132 AH / 661-750 CE), and especially at its beginning. As soon as the conquests stabilized in the lands dominated by the Hellenistic spirit—and this spirit dominated not only Egypt and Syria but also Iraq and Persia—the intermingling between the conquered nations and the conquerors began. Several factors related to the nature of Islam itself helped this. The most important of these factors are: Islam's declaration of complete equality among its adherents, whether they were Arabs or non-Arabs. This called many of the conquered nations—who had previous civilizations, different customs, and knowledge of Greek sciences—to embrace Islam. Islam was not a closed religion; rather, the Islamic world quickly opened to everyone entering it. We will later see one of the Umayyad caliphs, among the Umayyads having a blind pre-Islamic spirit, becoming distressed when he heard that the majority of the hadith scholars and jurists contemporary to him were mawali, i.e., of Persian origin. Intellectual freedom was a characteristic of Islamic rule in the conquered lands. This freedom called many of the sons of the conquered nations to present their opinions and beliefs, and even to debate Muslims regarding their doctrines. The forms of responses differed between the early Muslims of pure Arab stock and the later Muslims of the first generation who were of mixed lineage and had a composite mentality. Then a number of these latter Muslims turned to this Greek science and attempted to acquaint themselves with it (even though they did not adopt anything from it—as we will see later). It is not surprising that some historians of early kalam mention that the early mutakallimun, like the followers of Wasil ibn 'Ata' (133 AH / 728 CE), read the books of the philosophers (1). In any case, Greek philosophy reached the Islamic world through this intermingling during the Umayyad period. There is evidence to prove this: (a) What Ibn Kathir mentions: 'The sciences of the ancients entered the lands of the Muslims in the first century when they conquered the lands of the non-Arabs, but they were not abundant among them nor did they spread, because the predecessors forbade delving into them' (2). What is meant by 'sciences of the ancients' here is Greek philosophical sciences. (b) Clarifying this text further is a passage from the book 'The Four Journeys' by Sadr al-Din al-Shirazi, which he transmitted from al-Mutarahat by al-Suhrawardi, specifically concerning the sources of doctrines.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage from the introductory chapter of Manahij Bahth presents a historical account of the transmission of Aristotelian logic to the Islamic world, with a focus on the pre-Abbasid period under the Umayyads. The author begins by noting the difficulty in pinpointing the exact beginning of Muslims' acquaintance with Greek philosophy, as scholars typically point to the Abbasid era. However, he emphasizes that there is consensus that logic was the first Greek philosophical science known to Muslims. He then argues that the transmission movement cannot be overlooked even before the Abbasid period, specifically during the Umayyad dynasty (40-132 AH / 661-750 CE). The author explains how the stabilization of Islamic conquests in Hellenistic regions (Egypt, Syria, Iraq, Persia) led to cultural intermingling between the conquered peoples and the Arab conquerors. Several factors stemming from Islam itself facilitated this fusion: first, Islam's principle of equality among all Muslims, Arab and non-Arab alike, which encouraged many from the conquered nations—who had prior knowledge of Greek sciences—to convert. Second, Islam was not a closed religion but open to all. The author notes a later Umayyad caliph who was distressed that most scholars of hadith and fiqh were of Persian origin (mawali). Third, intellectual freedom under Islamic rule allowed the conquered peoples to present their beliefs and debate with Muslims. The early Arab Muslims and later mixed-heritage Muslims responded differently. Consequently, some Muslims of later generations turned to Greek sciences to study them, though they did not adopt them. The author cites that early mutakallimun like the followers of Wasil ibn 'Ata' read philosophers' books. Finally, the author asserts that Greek philosophy did reach the Islamic world during the Umayyad period and provides two pieces of evidence: (a) Ibn Kathir's statement that the sciences of the ancients (Greek philosophy) entered Muslim lands in the first century but were not widespread due to opposition from the early pious predecessors; (b) a passage from Sadr al-Din al-Shirazi's book 'The Four Journeys', quoting al-Suhrawardi's 'al-Mutarahat' regarding sources of doctrines. The passage sets the historical context for the study of logic in the Islamic world, emphasizing that its entry began earlier than often recognized.

Key Takeaway

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
Historical Evidence for Early Transmission of Greek Logic to Islam
Author presents multiple proofs for early entry of Greek logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the three types of evidence the author uses to demonstrate that Greek logic entered the Islamic world during the Umayyad period.

Core Idea
Greek logic reached Muslims via multiple pre-Abbasid channels, including Christian monasteries.

This passage challenges the common narrative that Greek logic was a late addition to Islamic thought. The author presents three proofs from the Umayyad era: historical reports, textual traces in early theological works, and direct contact with Christian academies. Let's examine each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first proof: Ibn Kathir's historical report
Locate the reference to Ibn Kathir and note his statement that 'ulum al-awā'il entered in the first century but were suppressed.
2
Examine the second proof: textual transmission via al-Suhrawardi
Find the quotation from al-Asfar al-Arba'a. It describes early theologians having books with Greek names and adopting philosophical ideas.
3
Analyze the third proof: Christian monasteries as academic centers
The author details that Muslims interacted with Church Fathers in monasteries where Aristotle's logic was taught, evidenced by Syriac manuscripts and John of Damascus.
4
Synthesize the evidence
Consider how these three proofs together establish a strong case for early transmission, including Stoic influences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسلام ولم يكن الإسلام ديناً مغلقاً ، بل سرعان ما انفتح العالم الإسلامي لكل داخل فيه . وسنرى بعد أحد خلفاء الأمويين ، وفي الأمويين روح جاهلية عمياء ، يضيق صدره حين يسمع أن العدد الأكبر من المحدثين والفقهاء المعاصرين له من الموالي أي من أصول فارسية . وقد كانت الحرية الفكرية ميزة الحكم الإسلامي في البلاد المفتوحة . وقد دعت هذه الحرية الكثيرين من أبناء الأمم المغلوبة إلى عرض آرائهم ومعتقداتهم بل إلى مناقشة المسلمين في عقائدهم . وقد اختلفت صيغ الردود بين المسلمين الأوائل من عرب أقحاح ، والمسلمين التاليين للصدر الأول وكانوا مهجنين ، وذوي عقلية مركبة . ومن ثم أقبل عدد من هؤلاء المسلمين الآخرين على هذا العلم اليوناني وحاولوا تعرفه ( ولو أنهم لم يأخذوا بشيء منه ـ كما سنرى ذلك فيما بعد ) . وليس من المستغرب أن يذكر بعض مؤرخي الفكر الكلامي الأول أن المتكلمين الأول أمثال أصحاب واصل بن عطاء ( ١٣٣هـ ـ ٧٢٨م ) طالعوا كتب الفلاسفة(١) . وعلى أية حال لقد وصل خلال هذا التزاوج إلى العالم الإسلامي وفي عصر بني أمية فلسفة اليونان . وهناك من الدلائل ما يثبت هذا . ( أ ) ما يذكره ابن كثير من « أن علوم الأوائل دخلت إلى بلاد المسلمين في القرن الأول لما فتحوا بلاد الأعاجم لكنها لم تكثر فيهم ولم تنتشر ، لما كان السلف يمنعون من الخوض فيها »(٢) . والمقصود بعلوم الأوائل هنا : العلوم الفلسفية اليونانية . (ب) ويزيد هذا النص توضيحاً فقرة من كتاب الأسفار الأربعة لصدر الدين الشيرازي نقلها عن المطارحات للسهروردي خاصة بمصادر مذاهب المتكلمين الأول تقرر أنه « بأيديهم ( أي بأيدي المتكلمين الأول ) مما نقله جماعة في عهد بني أمية من كتبهم من كتب ( قوم ) أساميهم تشبه أسامي الفلاسفة : فظن القوم أن كل إسم يوناني فهو فيلسوف ، ووجدوا فيها كلمات استحسنوها : وذهبوا إليها وفرعوها رغبة في الفلسفة ، وانتشرت في الأرض وهم بها فرحون »(۱) . (جـ) ومما يثبت صلة علم المسلمين بفلسفة اليونان في القرن الأول إحتكام المسلمين العلمي واتصالهم بآباء الكنيسة في الشام وما بين النهرين في أديرتهم وكنائسهم ونقاشهم لعقائد المسيحيين . وقد كفل المسلمون للأديرة والكنائس الحرية الدينية والفكرية ، فكانت كمجامع علمية تدرس فيها فلسفة اليونان وفي مقدمتها منطق أرسطو : حتى آخر الفصل السابع من التحليلات الأولى أي إلى آخر القياسات الحملية(٢) . وذلك فضلاً عن أثر المنطق والفلسفة الرواقية في عقائد الكنيسة(۳) ويثبت اتصال المسلمين بأصحاب هذه الأديرة وجود مخطوطات سوريانية من مخطوطات هذا العهد فيها جانب كبير من نقاش المسلمين لعقائد المسيحيين . ورد هؤلاء الأخيرين على المسلمين كما أن البحث في حياة يوحنا الدمشقي يدعم هذه الفكرة وسيأتي ذلك بعد . وهناك دليلان يثبتان أن المسلمين عرفوا المنطق اليوناني عن طريق احتكاكهم بآباء الكنيسة : أما أولهما : فهو الآثار الرواقية في نقد المتكلمين الأول لمنطق أرسطو(٤) .
Islam was not a closed religion; rather the Islamic world quickly opened to all who entered. We will see later that one of the Umayyad caliphs—and in the Umayyads there was a blind pre-Islamic spirit—was distressed when he heard that the majority of the traditionists and jurists contemporary with him were mawali, i.e., of Persian origin. Intellectual freedom was a characteristic of Islamic rule in conquered lands. This freedom called many of the conquered peoples to present their opinions and beliefs, and even to debate Muslims over their creeds. The forms of responses differed between the early Arabs of pure stock and the later Muslims after the first generation, who were of mixed descent and had a composite mentality. Then a number of these later Muslims turned to this Greek science and tried to familiarize themselves with it (though they did not adopt anything from it, as we shall see later). It is not surprising that some historians of early theological thought mention that the early theologians, such as the companions of Wasil ibn 'Ata' (133 AH/728 CE), read the books of the philosophers. In any case, through this cross-fertilization, Greek philosophy reached the Islamic world in the Umayyad era. There is evidence to prove this: (A) What Ibn Kathir mentions: 'The sciences of the ancients entered the lands of the Muslims in the first century when they conquered the lands of the non-Arabs, but they did not become abundant among them nor spread, because the early generations forbade delving into them.' What is meant by 'sciences of the ancients' here is Greek philosophical sciences. (B) This text is further clarified by a passage from the book 'al-Asfar al-Arba'a' by Sadr al-Din al-Shirazi, transmitted from 'al-Mutarahat' by al-Suhrawardi, specifically concerning the sources of the doctrines of the early theologians, stating: 'In their hands (i.e., the hands of the early theologians) is what a group transmitted in the Umayyad era from their books—from the books of a people whose names resemble the names of philosophers; the people assumed that every Greek name was a philosopher, and they found in them words they approved of, adopted them, and branched out from them out of desire for philosophy, and it spread in the land and they rejoiced in it.' (C) Among the things that prove the connection of Muslim learning with Greek philosophy in the first century is the Muslims' scholarly recourse and contact with the Church Fathers in Syria and Mesopotamia in their monasteries and churches, and their debate over Christian doctrines. The Muslims guaranteed religious and intellectual freedom to the monasteries and churches, so they were like academic institutions where Greek philosophy was taught, foremost among them Aristotle's logic: up to the end of the seventh chapter of the Prior Analytics, i.e., to the end of categorical syllogisms. This is in addition to the influence of Stoic logic and philosophy on church doctrines. The connection of Muslims with the inhabitants of these monasteries is proven by the existence of Syriac manuscripts from that period containing a large portion of Muslim debates on Christian doctrines and the latter's responses to Muslims. Moreover, research into the life of John of Damascus supports this idea, and it will come later. There are two proofs that Muslims knew Greek logic through their contact with the Church Fathers: The first is the Stoic traces in the early theologians' criticism of Aristotle's logic.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a historical introduction that presents evidence for the early transmission of Greek logic to the Islamic world, specifically during the Umayyad period, before the major translation movement of the Abbasid era. The author begins by describing the openness of Islamic society and the role of non-Arab converts (mawali) who brought intellectual curiosity. They note that early theologians like Wasil ibn 'Ata' read philosophers' works. Then the author lists three concrete proofs: (1) Ibn Kathir reports that the 'sciences of the ancients' (Greek philosophy) entered in the first century but were suppressed by early scholars. (2) A quotation from al-Shirazi via al-Suhrawardi indicates that early theologians possessed books that they mistook for philosophical works and adopted ideas from them. (3) The Muslims' contact with Christian monasteries, where Aristotle's logic (up to Prior Analytics Book 7) was taught, and the existence of Syriac manuscripts of debates, along with the figure of John of Damascus, demonstrate early familiarity. Finally, the author introduces two additional proofs, partially stating that the first is the Stoic influence in early theological critiques of Aristotle. The overall argument is that Greek logic was known early through multiple channels, contrary to the view that it came only later.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
Evidence for Early Muslim Knowledge of Greek Logic
Author presents historical evidence for early Muslim acquaintance with Greek logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the historical evidence for early Muslim acquaintance with Greek logic presented in this passage.

Core Idea
Muslim access to Greek logic began in the first century Hijri, well before the Abbasid translation movement.

The passage presents multiple lines of evidence for early Muslim familiarity with Greek logic, including interactions with Christian monasteries, early translation efforts, and the influence of Stoic logic on early theological critiques. This lesson will walk through the evidence and its implications for the history of Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author argues that Muslims knew Greek logic in the first century Hijri. Read the opening quote and the concluding sentence to grasp the central thesis.
Look for the explicit statement that translators began in the first century.
2
Examine evidence from church connections
The author cites the freedom given to monasteries, Syriac manuscripts of debates, and the life of John of Damascus. Consider how these show direct intellectual contact.
Pay attention to the role of Stoic logic as a bridge.
3
Analyze the two specific proofs
The first proof is Stoic traces in early mutakallimun's critique of Aristotle; the second is that early Islamic translations followed Christian models, stopping at categorical syllogisms.
These distinguish the early transmission from later comprehensive translations.
4
Evaluate the Khalid ibn Yazid story
The author mentions Khalid's order to translate the Organon but notes scholarly doubts. Assess whether the story is credible given the context of the Alexandrian academy.
The author uses this to push back against the myth of the library's burning.
5
Synthesize the timeline
The author connects Umayyad and Abbasid efforts, highlighting that the Abbasids continued an already existing movement.
Note that the first logical translations were of Aristotle's three works and Porphyry's Isagoge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتكلمين الأول تقرر أنه « بأيديهم ( أي بأيدي المتكلمين الأول ) مما نقله جماعة في عهد بني أمية من كتبهم من كتب ( قوم ) أساميهم تشبه أسامي الفلاسفة : فظن القوم أن كل إسم يوناني فهو فيلسوف ، ووجدوا فيها كلمات استحسنوها : وذهبوا إليها وفرعوها رغبة في الفلسفة ، وانتشرت في الأرض وهم بها فرحون »(۱) . (جـ) ومما يثبت صلة علم المسلمين بفلسفة اليونان في القرن الأول إحتكام المسلمين العلمي واتصالهم بآباء الكنيسة في الشام وما بين النهرين في أديرتهم وكنائسهم ونقاشهم لعقائد المسيحيين . وقد كفل المسلمون للأديرة والكنائس الحرية الدينية والفكرية ، فكانت كمجامع علمية تدرس فيها فلسفة اليونان وفي مقدمتها منطق أرسطو : حتى آخر الفصل السابع من التحليلات الأولى أي إلى آخر القياسات الحملية(٢) . وذلك فضلاً عن أثر المنطق والفلسفة الرواقية في عقائد الكنيسة(۳) ويثبت اتصال المسلمين بأصحاب هذه الأديرة وجود مخطوطات سوريانية من مخطوطات هذا العهد فيها جانب كبير من نقاش المسلمين لعقائد المسيحيين . ورد هؤلاء الأخيرين على المسلمين كما أن البحث في حياة يوحنا الدمشقي يدعم هذه الفكرة وسيأتي ذلك بعد . وهناك دليلان يثبتان أن المسلمين عرفوا المنطق اليوناني عن طريق احتكاكهم بآباء الكنيسة : أما أولهما : فهو الآثار الرواقية في نقد المتكلمين الأول لمنطق أرسطو(٤) . وأما ثانيهما : فهو أن التراجم الإسلامية الأولى للأورجانون التي وصلتنا أو التي وصل إلينا أسماؤها هي على غرار الكتب المنطقية المسيحية ، أي إنها كانت تقف عند آخر الأشكال الوجودية(١) . (د) ومن الدلائل على علم المسلمين بالمنطق اليوناني في عهد بني أمية ما يذكر من أن خالد بن يزيد ( ٩٠هـ ) أمر بعض العلماء اليونانيين الذين كانوا يقيمون في الإسكندرية بترجمة الأورجانون من اليونانية إلى العربية(٢) وإن كان لنا أن نفترض أن هناك شكوكاً تحوط شخصية هذا الأمير الأموي الغريب المدعو بفيلسوف الأول . فقد ثبت أن مجلس التعليم الطبي والفلسفي في مدينة الاسكندرية كان موجوداً حين دخول العرب وأنه لم ينته بدخولهم ولم يعد بعد أبحاث ماكس مايرهوف مجال لقبول الأسطورة المزعومة السخيفة أن مكتبة الاسكندرية أحرقت بأمر الخليفة عمر بن الخطاب . يمكننا إذاً أن نصل - من تلك الدلائل السابقة - إلى أن المسلمين عرفوا الفلسفة اليونانية في القرن الأول الهجري - وابتدأ المترجمون في نقل كتبها إلى اللغة العربية وإذا كان للعباسيين ( ١١٣ - ٦٥٦هـ = ٧٥٠ - ١٢٥٨م ) فضل بعد ذلك فهو أنهم تابعوا تلك الحركة بنشاط عجيب « وكان أول علم اعتنِيَ به من علوم الفلسفة علم المنطق والنجوم »(٣) . ومظهر ذلك إذا ما وصلنا إلى عهد الترجمة الرسمية أن مؤرخي الفكر الفلسفي في العالم الإسلامي يذكرون أن أقدم تراجم الكتب اليونانية «ترجمة كتب أرسططاليس المنطقية الثلاثة في صورة المنطق - وهي كتاب قاطيغورياس ، وكتاب باري أرمنياس ، وكتاب أنالوطيقا الأولى وإيساغوجي لفرفوريوس(٤) . وأن صاحب هذه الترجمة هو عبد الله بن المقفع ( ١٣٩هـ ) كاتب أبي جعفر المنصور(٥)
The early mutakallimun determined that 'in their hands (i.e., the hands of the early mutakallimun) were what a group during the Umayyad era transmitted from their books from the books of a people whose names resemble those of the philosophers: so the people thought that every Greek name is a philosopher, and they found in them words they approved: so they adopted them and branched out from them desiring philosophy, and it spread in the land while they rejoiced in it.' (c) Among what proves the connection of Muslim science to Greek philosophy in the first century is the scientific appeal of Muslims and their connection to the Church Fathers in Syria and Mesopotamia in their monasteries and churches and their discussion of Christian doctrines. The Muslims guaranteed religious and intellectual freedom for monasteries and churches, so they were like scientific academies teaching Greek philosophy, foremost among them Aristotle's logic, up to the end of the seventh chapter of the Prior Analytics, i.e., to the end of the categorical syllogisms. This is in addition to the influence of Stoic logic and philosophy on church doctrines. The connection of Muslims with the owners of these monasteries is proven by the existence of Syriac manuscripts from this period containing a large portion of Muslim discussions of Christian doctrines, and the responses of the latter to the Muslims. Also, research into the life of John of Damascus supports this idea, which will come later. There are two proofs that Muslims knew Greek logic through their contact with the Church Fathers: As for the first: it is the Stoic traces in the critique of the early mutakallimun of Aristotle's logic. As for the second: it is that the early Islamic translations of the Organon that have reached us, or whose names have reached us, are in the style of Christian logical books, i.e., they stopped at the end of the categorical figures. (d) Among the evidence for Muslim knowledge of Greek logic in the Umayyad era is what is mentioned that Khalid ibn Yazid (90 AH) ordered some Greek scholars who were residing in Alexandria to translate the Organon from Greek into Arabic. Although we might assume that there are doubts surrounding the personality of this strange Umayyad prince called the first philosopher, it has been proven that the medical and philosophical teaching council in the city of Alexandria existed when the Arabs entered and did not end with their entry, and after the research of Max Meyerhof there is no room to accept the alleged ridiculous myth that the Library of Alexandria was burned by order of Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab. We can therefore conclude from these previous proofs that Muslims knew Greek philosophy in the first century Hijri – and the translators began to transmit its books into Arabic, and if the Abbasids (113-656 AH = 750-1258 AD) have any credit after that, it is that they continued this movement with remarkable activity, 'and the first science of philosophy to be attended to was the science of logic and astronomy.' The manifestation of this is that when we come to the era of official translation, historians of philosophical thought in the Islamic world mention that the oldest translations of Greek books are 'the translation of Aristotle's three logical books in the form of logic – namely the Categories, On Interpretation, and the Prior Analytics, and Porphyry's Isagoge. And that the author of this translation is Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa' (139 AH), the scribe of Abu Ja'far al-Mansur.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage is a historical argument presenting multiple lines of evidence for early Muslim acquaintance with Greek logic. The author begins by quoting a statement from the early mutakallimun (theologians) that they possessed books from a people (likely Greek philosophers) with names resembling philosophers, and they adopted and expanded upon them. Then, under section (c), the author argues that the connection between Muslim science and Greek philosophy is evidenced by the scientific interactions between Muslims and Christian Church Fathers in the monasteries and churches of Syria and Mesopotamia. Muslims granted these institutions freedom, allowing them to function as academies teaching Greek philosophy, especially Aristotle's logic up to the categorical syllogisms. The author notes the influence of Stoic logic on church doctrines and mentions Syriac manuscripts containing Muslim-Christian debates. The life of John of Damascus is cited as further support. The author then lists two specific proofs: first, the Stoic traces in the early mutakallimun's critique of Aristotle's logic; second, the pattern of early Islamic translations of the Organon, which followed the Christian model by stopping at the categorical figures. Under section (d), the author presents additional evidence: the story of Khalid ibn Yazid ordering a translation of the Organon from Greek to Arabic. Despite doubts about Khalid's story, the author asserts the existence of the Alexandrian academy at the time of the Arab conquest, refuting the myth of the library's burning. From all this, the author concludes that Muslims knew Greek philosophy in the first century Hijri and that translation began early. The Abbasids later continued this movement with vigor, with logic and astronomy being the first philosophical sciences attended to. The author ends by noting that the earliest translations included the three logical books of Aristotle and Porphyry's Isagoge, attributed to Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa'.
