Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
Early Islamic Translations of Greek Logic
Early Islamic translations and the Ibn al-Muqaffa' controversy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the historiographical debate concerning early Islamic translations of Greek logical works and assess the evidence for the role of Ibn al-Muqaffa'.

Core Idea
The earliest Arabic logical texts were likely summaries of commentaries, not direct translations.

The history of how Greek logic entered the Islamic world is itself a subject of scholarly debate. This lesson examines the evidence for early translations, the role of the Umayyad prince Khalid ibn Yazid, and the controversy over whether Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa' or his son Muhammad was the translator of the first Arabic logical works. More importantly, we will see that the earliest works were not direct translations but summaries of commentaries, a critical refinement of earlier historiography.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claims about early translations
The author states that the earliest translations followed Christian models and stopped at the 'last forms of existence.' Look for the names of the books translated (Categories, On Interpretation, Prior Analytics) and the mention of Porphyry's Isagoge.
These form the core logical curriculum in late antiquity.
2
Assess the evidence for Umayyad-era translation
The author mentions Khalid ibn Yazid but expresses doubts, while confirming the existence of the Alexandrian school. Evaluate the strength of this evidence.
The burning of the Library is refuted.
3
Analyze the attribution controversy
The author presents the traditional view that Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa' translated the three books. Then Paul Kraus argues it was his son Muhammad, and that the text was a summary of a commentary. The author adds further doubts based on al-Qifti's mention.
Distinguish between translation and summary.
4
Evaluate the language of the original source
Kraus uses the translation of 'ousia' as 'ayn' to argue for a Greek original. Nallino disagrees. Consider the linguistic evidence.
This shows how philological analysis informs historical conclusions.
5
Reconstruct a more accurate historical narrative
Based on the evidence, Muhammad ibn al-Muqaffa' probably summarized existing translations into easy Arabic. Later, these summaries were mistaken for original translations.
This exemplifies the critical refinement of historical narratives.

As for the second point: the first Islamic translations of the Organon that have reached us, or whose names have reached us, are modeled after Christian logical books, i.e., they stopped at the last forms of existence (1). (d) Among the evidence of Muslims' knowledge of Greek logic in the Umayyad era is what is mentioned that Khalid ibn Yazid (90 AH) ordered some Greek scholars who resided in Alexandria to translate the Organon from Greek into Arabic (2), although we may assume that there are doubts surrounding the personality of this strange Umayyad prince called the first philosopher. It has been established that the medical and philosophical educational council in the city of Alexandria existed at the time of the Arab conquest and that it did not end with their entry, and after the research of Max Meyerhof there is no longer room to accept the alleged stupid legend that the Library of Alexandria was burned by order of Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab. We can therefore conclude - from those previous evidences - that the Muslims knew Greek philosophy in the first century AH, and the translators began to transmit its books into Arabic, and if the Abbasids (113-656 AH = 750-1258 CE) have any credit after that, it is that they continued that movement with amazing activity, and 'the first science from the philosophical sciences that was taken care of was the science of logic and astronomy' (3). The manifestation of that, when we reach the era of official translation, is that historians of philosophical thought in the Islamic world mention that the oldest translations of Greek books are 'the translation of Aristotle's three logical books in the form of logic - namely the book of Categories, the book of Peri Hermeneias, and the book of Prior Analytics, and the Isagoge of Porphyry (4). And that the author of this translation is Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa' (139 AH), the secretary of Abu Ja'far al-Mansur (5) (136-158 AH = 754-775 CE). And the late Paul Kraus proved that the translator of these books was not Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa', but rather his son Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa', and the researcher relied on this on manuscript no. 238 of the Saint Joseph Library in Beirut, which contains these books. It was mentioned at the end of it that its translator was Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa'. Then he proved that these books are not a translation of Aristotle's books but rather a summary of a commentary on these books (1). This statement makes me doubt the attribution of the translation of these books to Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa', and this doubt is justified by what we notice in what has been written about Aristotle's works that were transmitted to the Islamic world in the Abbasid era; we do not find at all any indication that a summary of a commentary on Aristotle's books was translated into Arabic. But we find al-Qifti mentioning a translation of a commentary by Alexander of Aphrodisias on the Categories and stating that Ibn al-Muqaffa' (we care not here about the father nor the son) had a summary of it. Al-Qifti also mentions that Ibn al-Muqaffa' summarized Peri Hermeneias (2). And I lean towards that Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa' did not translate these books but summarized them from commentaries that were translated in his time or during the Umayyad era before, and he put them in an easy, accessible expression as Sa'id mentions (3). Then these translations were lost and only the summary remained, so after a long time it was attributed to Ibn al-Muqaffa' as that he translated it, not that he summarized it. As for the language from which these summaries were translated - whether Greek or Persian - Kraus argues that the internal criticism of these books proves that they were translated from Greek, because the translator used the word 'ayn as a translation for the Greek word 'ousia', while it would have been necessary - if these translations were of Persian origin - for the translator to resort to the Persian word 'jawhar'. And Nallino does not see in this evidence that
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a historical discussion about the early transmission of Greek logic into the Islamic world. It begins by asserting that the earliest Islamic translations of Aristotle's Organon followed the pattern of Christian logical works, meaning they stopped at the 'last forms of existence' (likely referring to the Categories, On Interpretation, and Prior Analytics, i.e., the first three books of the Organon). The author then provides evidence that Muslims were acquainted with Greek logic during the Umayyad period, citing Khalid ibn Yazid's alleged instruction to translate the Organon. The author expresses skepticism about Khalid's role but confirms that the medical-philosophical school in Alexandria continued after the Muslim conquest, and refutes the legend of the burning of the Library of Alexandria by Umar. Moving to the Abbasid period, the author quotes historians who state that the earliest translations included Aristotle's three logical books and Porphyry's Isagoge, attributed to Abdullah ibn al-Muqaffa'. However, Paul Kraus argued that the translator was actually Abdullah's son Muhammad, based on a manuscript, and that the text was not a direct translation but a summary of a commentary. The author expresses doubt about this attribution, noting that no other source mentions a summary of a commentary being translated into Arabic, but al-Qifti does mention that Ibn al-Muqaffa' (either father or son) made a summary of Alexander's commentary on the Categories and summarized Peri Hermeneias. The author concludes that Muhammad ibn al-Muqaffa' likely summarized existing translations into easy Arabic, and later this summary was mistaken for a translation. Finally, the passage discusses the language of the original: Kraus argues for Greek because the translator used 'ayn for 'ousia', while Nallino disputes this reasoning.

Debating the Sources of al-Ghazali's Tahafut
Author presents competing accounts of Tahafut's source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the two competing accounts of the source of al-Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa using the textual evidence provided.

Core Idea
The origins of al-Ghazali's critique are contested between Greek skepticism and a Christian philosopher's refutation.

This passage presents a historiographical debate: did al-Ghazali derive his Tahafut from Sextus Empiricus, as Asin Palacios claimed, or from the Christian philosopher Yahya al-Nahwi al-Daylami, as al-Bayhaqi asserted? Understanding this debate trains you to critically evaluate claims about intellectual transmission in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing claims
Find the claim by Asin Palacios linking Tahafut to Sextus Empiricus, and the counterclaim by al-Bayhaqi attributing it to Yahya al-Nahwi al-Daylami. Note the specific wording used.
2
Examine the evidence for each claim
Al-Bayhaqi quotes a statement from Abu Ali saying most of Tahafut is a restatement of Yahya's speech. Al-Shahrazuri adds that Yahya al-Nahwi al-Daylami is distinct from another Yahya. Assess the strength of this evidence compared to Palacios' assertion.
Also consider the passage's note that Yahya may have derived from Sextus, creating a chain.
3
Evaluate the reliability of the sources
Consider the authority of al-Bayhaqi and al-Shahrazuri as historians. The passage treats their claims seriously but also raises the possibility of indirect transmission via Sextus. Decide which account is better supported.
4
Formulate your own conclusion
Based on the evidence, write a one-paragraph assessment of the likely source of Tahafut, referencing the specific points from the text.
Your conclusion should engage with both the direct claim and the chain of transmission.

ليست هناك أية إشارة إلى هذا . ويرى أسين بلاسيوس Asin Palacios في بحث كتبه عن معنى كلمة « تهافت » أن كتاب « التهافت للغزالي ليس معظمه إلا ترديداً لكتب سكستوس امبريقوس ». غير أن البيهقي يشير إلى أن التهافت مستمد من كتاب ألفه يحيى النحوي الديلمي الملقب بالبطريق يرد فيه على أرسطو وأفلاطون . يقول البيهقي « يحيى النحوي الملقب بالبطريق ـ كان يحيى الديلمي من قدماء الحكماء . وكان فيلسوفاً نصرانياً . ويحيى النحوي البطريق هو الذي صنف كتباً رد بها وفيها على أفلاطون وأرسطو حين همت النصارى بقتله . وقال في شأنه أبو علي : « هو يحيى النحوي المموه على النصارى وأكثر ما أورده الإمام حجة الإسلام الغزالي رحمة الله عليه في تهافت الفلاسفة تقرير كلام يحيى النحوي » ويقول الشهرزوري « يحيى النحوي الديلمي وهو غير يحيى النحوي الإسكندري » ثم لا يخرج كلام الشهرزوري بعد هذا عما ذكره البيهقي . ولكن من المحتمل أن يكون يحيى النحوي هذا استمد نقده من كتاب سكستوس ، هذا من ناحية ، ومن ناحية أخرى من المؤكد أن كثيراً من آراء الشكاك نقل عن طريق شراح الأورجانون وخلال كتابات جالينوس على الخصوص . ويذكر أيضاً عن جالينوس أنه نقد الشكاك. كما يقول الشهرزوري « أنه اشتغل بالتجربة وحكاية أصحاب التجارب » فهل معنى هذا أنه نقل أيضاً الجانب الإنشائي من آراء الشكاك إلى العالم الإسلامي ، وخاصة أن هذا الأخير كان تجريبياً ؟ يحتمل هذا كثيراً .

أما عن منطق الشراح اليونان متقدميهم ومتأخريهم فقد وصل إلى العالم الإسلامي . عرف المسلمون ثيوفرسطس وأوديموس وعرفوا القضايا والأقيسة الشرطية وكانا أول من أشار إليها . أما عن الشراح المتأخرين فوصلت إلى المسلمين أبحاثهم وفيها أثر كبير للأفلاطونية الحديثة .

أما عن شروح اللاتين للمنطق الأرسططاليسي فلا يذكر مؤرخو الفلسفة الإسلامية شيئاً عن ترجمتها للغة العربية ، غير أنني أستطيع أن أجزم بأن المسلمين ترجموا بعض هذه الشروح ، وأستند في هذا إلى مخطوطة نادرة لأسعد بن علي بن عثمان البانيوي « رسالة في المنطق » يقول المؤلف في مقدمة كتابه أنه أراد دراسة العلم اليوناني في مصادره فطلب الكتب الفلسفية يونانية كانت أو لاتينية حتى وجدها ، ثم أخذ في تعلم اللغتين على يد رجل رومي في القسطنطينية حتى أتقنها . ثم أراد بعد ذلك ترجمة الكتب الآلهة والطبيعية الأرسططاليسية - ولكن لما كانت هذه الكتب مستندة على القانون العقلي وهو المنطق - اضطر إلى البحث في المنطق أولاً - ثم أراد ترجمته وترجم بالفعل إيساغوجي « ليوفوريوس الصوري » وقاتغوريا « وأكثر « باري أرمنياس » . ولكنه رأى أن يشرح قبل القيام بترجمة الأورجانون - ما صعب فهمه من هذه الكتب ، وأن يترك شرح الكتب السهلة منها ، وأن يختصر ما فصل المؤلف من تلك الكتب السهلة . ويذكر في فقرة أخرى أن كتابه هذا سيشتمل على خلاصة آراء حكماء اليونان المتأخرين ثم على خلاصة آراء اللاتين - وأنه سيضمن كتابه على الخصوص الشرح الأنور للحكيم الكامل يوانس فوتنيوس . ويقتبس المؤلف كثيراً من شرح « توما أقوناس » أي توما الأكويني ، ويعرض كثيراً من أقواله . ولهذا الكتاب عندي قيمة كبيرة - أولاً : لأنه يثبت معرفة الإسلاميين
There is no indication of this. And Asin Palacios sees in a study he wrote about the meaning of the word 'Tahafut' that the book 'Tahafut' by al-Ghazali is mostly nothing but a repetition of the books of Sextus Empiricus. However, al-Bayhaqi indicates that the Tahafut is derived from a book authored by Yahya al-Nahwi al-Daylami, nicknamed al-Batriq, in which he refutes Aristotle and Plato. Al-Bayhaqi says: 'Yahya al-Nahwi, nicknamed al-Batriq — Yahya al-Daylami was among the ancient sages. He was a Christian philosopher. And Yahya al-Nahwi al-Batriq is the one who composed books in which he refuted Plato and Aristotle when the Christians intended to kill him. And Abu Ali said about him: 'He is Yahya al-Nahwi who deceived the Christians, and most of what the Imam, the Proof of Islam al-Ghazali, may God have mercy on him, presented in Tahafut al-Falasifa is a restatement of the speech of Yahya al-Nahwi.' And al-Shahrazuri says: 'Yahya al-Nahwi al-Daylami, and he is not Yahya al-Nahwi al-Iskandari.' Then al-Shahrazuri's statement after this does not depart from what al-Bayhaqi mentioned. But it is probable that this Yahya al-Nahwi derived his criticism from the book of Sextus, on the one hand, and on the other hand, it is certain that many of the opinions of the skeptics were transmitted via the commentators of the Organon and especially through the writings of Galen. It is also mentioned about Galen that he criticized the skeptics. As al-Shahrazuri says: 'He engaged in experimentation and the narration of the people of experiments.' So does this mean that he also transmitted the constructive aspect of the opinions of the skeptics to the Islamic world, especially since this latter was experimental? This is highly probable. As for the logic of the Greek commentators, early and late, it reached the Islamic world. The Muslims knew Theophrastus and Eudemus, and they knew conditional propositions and syllogisms, and they were the first to refer to them. As for the later commentators, their researches reached the Muslims, and in them there is a great influence of Neoplatonism. As for the Latin commentaries on Aristotelian logic, historians of Islamic philosophy do not mention anything about their translation into Arabic. However, I can assert that the Muslims translated some of these commentaries, and I base this on a rare manuscript by As'ad ibn Ali ibn Uthman al-Banuyi, 'A Treatise on Logic.' The author says in the introduction of his book that he wanted to study Greek science in its sources, so he sought philosophical books, whether Greek or Latin, until he found them. Then he began learning the two languages under a Roman man in Constantinople until he mastered them. Then he wanted after that to translate the metaphysical and physical books of Aristotle — but since these books were based on the intellectual law which is logic — he was compelled to research logic first — then he wanted to translate it and actually translated Isagogi 'of Porphyry the Tyrian' and Categories 'and most of On Interpretation.' But he saw fit to explain, before undertaking the translation of the Organon, what was difficult to understand from these books, and to leave the explanation of the easy books, and to abridge what the author detailed from those easy books. He mentions in another paragraph that this book of his will include a summary of the opinions of the late Greek sages and then a summary of the opinions of the Latins — and that he will include in his book especially the brighter commentary of the perfect sage Johannes Fotonius. And the author quotes extensively from the commentary of 'Thomas Aquinus,' i.e., Thomas Aquinas, and presents many of his statements. And this book has for me great value — firstly: because it proves the knowledge of the Muslims
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a piece of historical front matter from Manahij Bahth. The author surveys the various sources that influenced Islamic logic. He begins by noting the claim of Asin Palacios that al-Ghazali's Tahafut is largely a repetition of Sextus Empiricus, but then presents al-Bayhaqi's alternative view that al-Ghazali derived his critique from Yahya al-Nahwi al-Daylami, a Christian philosopher who wrote refutations of Plato and Aristotle. The author then considers the possibility that Yahya himself drew from Sextus, and notes the role of Galen in transmitting skeptical ideas. The discussion moves to Greek commentators: the Muslims knew Theophrastus and Eudemus, who first discussed conditional propositions and syllogisms, and later commentators brought Neoplatonic elements. Finally, the author argues that Latin commentaries were also translated, citing a manuscript by al-Banuyi who translated Porphyry's Isagoge, Aristotle's Categories, and most of On Interpretation, and who drew on Thomas Aquinas. The passage is descriptive and historiographical, not analytical of logical content.

يحيى النحوي
البيهقي

Key Takeaway
The source of al-Ghazali's Tahafut is disputed, with al-Bayhaqi providing evidence linking it to the Christian philosopher Yahya al-Nahwi rather than directly to Greek skeptics.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
12 min
Transmission of Greek and Latin Logic to the Islamic World
Author surveys Greek and Latin sources of Islamic logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key Greek and Latin sources of Islamic logic as described in the passage and assess the evidence for their transmission.

Core Idea
Islamic logic integrated Greek commentators, conditional syllogisms, and even Latin commentaries by Aquinas.

Beyond the Tahafut debate, the passage catalogues the broader transmission of logical works: the early Peripatetics Theophrastus and Eudemus introduced conditional logic, Galen transmitted skeptical ideas, and a rare manuscript shows that Latin works including Thomas Aquinas were translated. This lesson helps you trace the multi-layered heritage of Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
List the Greek sources mentioned
Identify Theophrastus, Eudemus, and Galen. Note their specific contributions: Theophrastus and Eudemus first discussed conditional propositions and syllogisms; Galen transmitted skeptical ideas and was himself a critic of skepticism.
Also note the later Greek commentators influenced by Neoplatonism.
2
Examine the evidence for Latin translations
Find the reference to al-Banuyi's manuscript. Note what he translated: Porphyry's Isagoge, Aristotle's Categories, and most of On Interpretation. Observe that he also used a commentary by 'Johannes Fotonius' and quoted Thomas Aquinas.
The author considers this manuscript proof that Muslims translated Latin commentaries.
3
Assess the significance of each source
Consider how each source contributed to Islamic logic: conditional logic from Theophrastus/Eudemus enriched syllogistic theory; skepticism via Galen added critical tools; Latin commentaries brought later developments.
Think about how these layers combine in works like those of al-Ghazali or Ibn Rushd.
4
Construct a timeline of transmission
Order the sources chronologically: early Peripatetics, later commentators, Latin scholastics. The passage suggests a continuous flow of logical material into the Islamic world.
Use the manuscript evidence to argue that the translation movement extended beyond Greek to Latin.

ليست هناك أية إشارة إلى هذا . ويرى أسين بلاسيوس Asin Palacios في بحث كتبه عن معنى كلمة « تهافت » أن كتاب « التهافت للغزالي ليس معظمه إلا ترديداً لكتب سكستوس امبريقوس ». غير أن البيهقي يشير إلى أن التهافت مستمد من كتاب ألفه يحيى النحوي الديلمي الملقب بالبطريق يرد فيه على أرسطو وأفلاطون . يقول البيهقي « يحيى النحوي الملقب بالبطريق ـ كان يحيى الديلمي من قدماء الحكماء . وكان فيلسوفاً نصرانياً . ويحيى النحوي البطريق هو الذي صنف كتباً رد بها وفيها على أفلاطون وأرسطو حين همت النصارى بقتله . وقال في شأنه أبو علي : « هو يحيى النحوي المموه على النصارى وأكثر ما أورده الإمام حجة الإسلام الغزالي رحمة الله عليه في تهافت الفلاسفة تقرير كلام يحيى النحوي » ويقول الشهرزوري « يحيى النحوي الديلمي وهو غير يحيى النحوي الإسكندري » ثم لا يخرج كلام الشهرزوري بعد هذا عما ذكره البيهقي . ولكن من المحتمل أن يكون يحيى النحوي هذا استمد نقده من كتاب سكستوس ، هذا من ناحية ، ومن ناحية أخرى من المؤكد أن كثيراً من آراء الشكاك نقل عن طريق شراح الأورجانون وخلال كتابات جالينوس على الخصوص . ويذكر أيضاً عن جالينوس أنه نقد الشكاك. كما يقول الشهرزوري « أنه اشتغل بالتجربة وحكاية أصحاب التجارب » فهل معنى هذا أنه نقل أيضاً الجانب الإنشائي من آراء الشكاك إلى العالم الإسلامي ، وخاصة أن هذا الأخير كان تجريبياً ؟ يحتمل هذا كثيراً .

أما عن منطق الشراح اليونان متقدميهم ومتأخريهم فقد وصل إلى العالم الإسلامي . عرف المسلمون ثيوفرسطس وأوديموس وعرفوا القضايا والأقيسة الشرطية وكانا أول من أشار إليها . أما عن الشراح المتأخرين فوصلت إلى المسلمين أبحاثهم وفيها أثر كبير للأفلاطونية الحديثة .

أما عن شروح اللاتين للمنطق الأرسططاليسي فلا يذكر مؤرخو الفلسفة الإسلامية شيئاً عن ترجمتها للغة العربية ، غير أنني أستطيع أن أجزم بأن المسلمين ترجموا بعض هذه الشروح ، وأستند في هذا إلى مخطوطة نادرة لأسعد بن علي بن عثمان البانيوي « رسالة في المنطق » يقول المؤلف في مقدمة كتابه أنه أراد دراسة العلم اليوناني في مصادره فطلب الكتب الفلسفية يونانية كانت أو لاتينية حتى وجدها ، ثم أخذ في تعلم اللغتين على يد رجل رومي في القسطنطينية حتى أتقنها . ثم أراد بعد ذلك ترجمة الكتب الآلهة والطبيعية الأرسططاليسية - ولكن لما كانت هذه الكتب مستندة على القانون العقلي وهو المنطق - اضطر إلى البحث في المنطق أولاً - ثم أراد ترجمته وترجم بالفعل إيساغوجي « ليوفوريوس الصوري » وقاتغوريا « وأكثر « باري أرمنياس » . ولكنه رأى أن يشرح قبل القيام بترجمة الأورجانون - ما صعب فهمه من هذه الكتب ، وأن يترك شرح الكتب السهلة منها ، وأن يختصر ما فصل المؤلف من تلك الكتب السهلة . ويذكر في فقرة أخرى أن كتابه هذا سيشتمل على خلاصة آراء حكماء اليونان المتأخرين ثم على خلاصة آراء اللاتين - وأنه سيضمن كتابه على الخصوص الشرح الأنور للحكيم الكامل يوانس فوتنيوس . ويقتبس المؤلف كثيراً من شرح « توما أقوناس » أي توما الأكويني ، ويعرض كثيراً من أقواله . ولهذا الكتاب عندي قيمة كبيرة - أولاً : لأنه يثبت معرفة الإسلاميين
There is no indication of this. Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Transmission of Aristotelian Logic to Islam
Author details the transmission of Aristotelian logic to the Islamic world and diverse Muslim responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key stages and figures in the transmission of Aristotelian logic to the Islamic world.

Core Idea
Logic reached Islam through Greek commentators, Latin translations, and diverse Muslim responses.

This lesson traces how Aristotelian logic entered the Islamic world through early Greek commentators, Neoplatonic influences, and later Latin translations. Understanding this history is crucial for contextualizing the logical debates among Islamic scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the early Greek commentators
Recognize that Theophrastus and Eudemus were among the first to discuss conditional propositions and syllogisms, and their works were known to Muslims.
These commentators expanded Aristotle's logic beyond categorical syllogisms.
2
Examine the role of Neoplatonism
Note that later Greek commentators integrated Neoplatonic ideas, which heavily influenced the transmitted commentaries received by Muslims.
Neoplatonism blended Platonic and Aristotelian thought, affecting Islamic philosophers like al-Farabi.
3
Analyze evidence of Latin translations
Study the manuscript of al-Banayawi, who learned Greek and Latin to access and translate logical texts, including Porphyry's Isagoge and Aristotle's Categories.
This demonstrates that Muslims translated Latin as well as Greek commentaries.
4
Summarize the main channels of transmission
Conclude that Aristotelian logic entered early via Greek commentaries, then later via Latin translations, with both streams influencing Islamic scholarship.
The passage emphasizes multiple channels and stages of transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسلامي . عرف المسلمون ثيوفرسطس وأوديموس وعرفوا القضايا والأقيسة الشرطية وكانا أول من أشار إليها . أما عن الشراح المتأخرين فوصلت إلى المسلمين أبحاثهم وفيها أثر كبير للأفلاطونية الحديثة . أما عن شروح اللاتين للمنطق الأرسططاليسي فلا يذكر مؤرخو الفلسفة الإسلامية شيئاً عن ترجمتها للغة العربية ، غير أنني أستطيع أن أجزم بأن المسلمين ترجموا بعض هذه الشروح ، وأستند في هذا إلى مخطوطة نادرة لأسعد بن علي بن عثمان البانيوي « رسالة في المنطق » (١) يقول المؤلف في مقدمة كتابه أنه أراد دراسة العلم اليوناني في مصادره فطلب الكتب الفلسفية يونانية كانت أو لاتينية حتى وجدها ، ثم أخذ في تعلم اللغتين على يد رجل رومي في القسطنطينية حتى أتقنها . ثم أراد بعد ذلك ترجمة الكتب الآلهية والطبيعية الأرسططاليسية - ولكن لما كانت هذه الكتب مستندة على القانون العقلي وهو المنطق - اضطر إلى البحث في المنطق أولاً - ثم أراد ترجمته وترجم بالفعل إيساغوجي « ليوفوريوس الصوري » وقاتغوريا « وأكثر « باري أرمنياس » . ولكنه رأى أن يشرح قبل القيام بترجمة الأورجانون - ما صعب فهمه من هذه الكتب ، وأن يترك شرح الكتب السهلة منها ، وأن يختصر ما فصل المؤلف من تلك الكتب السهلة . ويذكر في فقرة أخرى أن كتابه هذا سيشتمل على خلاصة آراء حكماء اليونان المتأخرين ثم على خلاصة آراء اللاتين - وأنه سيضمن كتابه على الخصوص الشرح الأنور للحكيم الكامل يوانس فوتنيوس . ويقتبس المؤلف كثيراً من شرح « توما أقوناس » أي توما الأكويني ، ويعرض كثيراً من أقواله . ولهذا الكتاب عندي قيمة كبيرة - أولاً : لأنه يثبت معرفة الإسلاميين لبعض الكتب اللاتينية وترجمتها - ثانياً : تزداد هذه القيمة في أن مؤلف الكتاب نفسه استند في تأليفه إلى الكتب اليونانية واللاتينية مباشرة - ثالثاً : أنه يحاول في كتابه أن يبين ما أضيف إلى الأبحاث الأرسططاليسية من عناصر غير أرسططاليسية . وأخيراً يمكننا أن نقول أن المنطق الأرسططاليسي دخل إلى العالم الإسلامي منذ زمن مبكر ، ثم توالت تراجمه وتكررت . ووصل بجانبه وممتزجاً به أحياناً أبحاث أخرى غير أرسططاليسية أضافها الشراح اليونانيون من بعده من مصادر متعددة . ثم انتقل إلى العالم الإسلامي في وقت متأخر جداً التراث المنطقي اللاتيني ووقف مفكرو الإسلام أمام التراث الأرسططاليسي مواقف متعارضة : أما الهلينيون الإسلاميون - فانقسموا أمامه قسمين ، القسم الأول : فلاسفة الإسلام ، وهم ليسوا في الواقع إلا شراحاً للتراث اليوناني وامتداداً للشراح الاسكندريين المتأخرين ، فقبلوه كوحدة فكرية كاملة واعتبروه قانون العقل الذي لا يتزعزع وحاولوا التوفيق بين العناصر غير الأرسططاليسية وبين منطق أرسطو : وهؤلاء هم الشراح الإسلاميون المشاؤون أو الأفلاطونيون المحدثون . القسم الثاني : فريق من الأصوليين والمتكلمين والمناطقة المتأخرين مالوا إلى الرواقية ورفضوا كثيراً من عناصر منطق أرسطو وهؤلاء هم الشراح الإسلاميون الرواقيون - ولكنهم لم يقفوا عند هذا الحد فحسب بل أضافوا أبحاثاً خاصة بهم - أما المتكلمون والأصوليون الأولون فلم يقبلوا المنطق الأرسططاليسي على الاطلاق وحاولوا إقامة منطق جديد بالكلية في جوهره . ووقف فقهاء أهل السنة والجماعة من المنطق الأرسططاليسي بل من المنطق اليوناني على العموم موقف العداوة التامة واصطنع بعضهم حجج الشكاك اليونانيين وأضاف إليها حججاً ابتدعوها ، ولكن كان لهم بجانب موقفهم الهادم موقف آخر إنشائي . وحارب الصوفية المنطق على العموم ، فهاجم السهروردي المنطق
The Muslims knew Theophrastus and Eudemus and knew conditional propositions and syllogisms, and they were the first to point to them. As for the later commentators, their researches reached the Muslims, and in them there is a great influence of Neoplatonism. As for the Latin commentaries on Aristotelian logic, the historians of Islamic philosophy do not mention anything about their translation into Arabic, but I can certainly assert that the Muslims translated some of these commentaries, and I rely on this to a rare manuscript by As'ad ibn 'Ali ibn 'Uthman al-Banayawi: 'A Treatise on Logic' (1). The author says in the introduction of his book that he wanted to study Greek science from its sources, so he sought philosophical books, Greek or Latin, until he found them, then he began learning the two languages from a Roman man in Constantinople until he mastered them. Then he wanted to translate the Aristotelian theological and natural books - but since these books are based on the intellectual law, which is logic - he was forced to research logic first - then he wanted to translate it and actually translated Isagoge 'of Porphyry' and Categories and most of 'De Interpretatione'. But he saw that before undertaking the translation of the Organon, he should explain what was difficult to understand from these books, and leave the explanation of the easy books, and summarize what the author had detailed from those easy books. And he mentions in another paragraph that this book of his will contain a summary of the opinions of the late Greek sages then a summary of the opinions of the Latins - and that he will especially include in his book the clearest explanation of the perfect sage Yuhanna Fwtnys. And the author quotes extensively from the commentary of 'Thomas Aquinas', i.e. Thomas Aquinas, and presents many of his sayings. And this book has great value for me - firstly: because it proves the Muslims' knowledge of some Latin books and their translation - secondly: this value increases because the author of the book himself relied in his composition on Greek and Latin books directly - thirdly: because he tries in his book to show what was added to Aristotelian researches from non-Aristotelian elements. Finally, we can say that Aristotelian logic entered the Islamic world from an early time, then its translations followed and recurred. And alongside it, and sometimes mixed with it, other non-Aristotelian researches arrived, added by the Greek commentators after him from multiple sources. Then, very late, the Latin logical heritage transferred to the Islamic world, and the thinkers of Islam stood before the Aristotelian heritage with opposing stances: as for the Islamic Hellenists - they divided before it into two groups: the first group: the philosophers of Islam, who are in reality only commentators on the Greek heritage and an extension of the late Alexandrian commentators, so they accepted it as a complete intellectual unit and considered it the unshakable law of reason and tried to reconcile between the non-Aristotelian elements and Aristotle's logic: these are the Islamic Peripatetic or Neoplatonist commentators. The second group: a group of usul scholars, theologians, and later logicians who inclined to Stoicism and rejected many elements of Aristotle's logic, and these are the Islamic Stoic commentators - but they did not stop at this limit only, but added their own researches. As for the early theologians and usul scholars, they did not accept Aristotelian logic at all and tried to establish an entirely new logic in its essence. And the jurists of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a stood against Aristotelian logic, indeed against Greek logic in general, with a stance of complete hostility, and some of them adopted the arguments of the Greek Skeptics and added to them arguments they invented, but besides their destructive stance they had another constructive stance. And the Sufis fought logic in general, so al-Suhrawardi attacked logic...
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview by the author of 'Manahij Bahth', detailing the transmission of Aristotelian logic into the Islamic world and the varied responses of Islamic scholars. The author begins by noting that early Muslims knew Theophrastus and Eudemus, who were the first to discuss conditional propositions and syllogisms. He then mentions that later Greek commentators' works reached Muslims, heavily influenced by Neoplatonism. Regarding Latin commentaries on Aristotle, the author claims they were also translated, citing a rare manuscript by al-Banayawi as evidence. Al-Banayawi's work involved learning Greek and Latin, translating key texts like Porphyry's Isagoge and Aristotle's Categories and De Interpretatione, and he planned to explain difficult parts before translating the entire Organon. The author values al-Banayawi's book for proving Muslim engagement with Latin texts, its direct use of Greek and Latin sources, and its attempt to identify non-Aristotelian additions. Finally, the author categorizes Islamic responses to Aristotelian logic: Hellenistic philosophers (Peripatetics/Neoplatonists) accepted it fully; a group of usul scholars, theologians, and logicians adopted a Stoic-influenced approach, rejecting parts and adding their own; early theologians completely rejected it and sought a new logic; Sunni jurists were hostile, sometimes using Skeptics' arguments but also building constructively; and Sufis generally opposed logic, exemplified by al-Suhrawardi's attacks.

Aristotelian logic entered the Islamic world through multiple channels: early Greek commentators, Neoplatonic influences, and later Latin translations, each shaping Islamic responses.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Classifying Islamic Responses to Aristotelian Logic
Author details the transmission of Aristotelian logic to the Islamic world and diverse Muslim responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize the different Muslim stances towards Aristotelian logic as described in the passage.

Core Idea
Muslims responded to Aristotle's logic in four main ways: full acceptance, selective adaptation, complete rejection, and constructive hostility.

This lesson examines the diverse stances of Islamic scholars toward Aristotelian logic. By learning to classify these responses, you will understand how logic was integrated, modified, or opposed within different Islamic intellectual traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hellenists (Peripatetics/Neoplatonists)
Recognize that the first group of Islamic Hellenists accepted Aristotelian logic as a complete intellectual unit and sought to reconcile non-Aristotelian elements with it.
These commentators considered logic the unshakable law of reason.
2
Distinguish the Stoic-influenced logicians
Note that a group of usul scholars, theologians, and later logicians inclined to Stoicism, rejecting many elements of Aristotle's logic while adding their own researches.
They did not fully reject but adapted and supplemented.
3
Contrast the early theologians who rejected logic entirely
Understand that early theologians and usul scholars completely rejected Aristotelian logic and attempted to build an entirely new logic.
This represents a total rejection and constructive alternative.
4
Examine the hostile but constructive stance of jurists and Sufis
Recognize that Sunni jurists showed hostility, sometimes using Skeptic arguments, but also had a constructive side. Sufis like al-Suhrawardi attacked logic in general.
Hostility did not preclude building; some jurists developed alternative methods.

الإسلامي . عرف المسلمون ثيوفرسطس وأوديموس وعرفوا القضايا والأقيسة الشرطية وكانا أول من أشار إليها . أما عن الشراح المتأخرين فوصلت إلى المسلمين أبحاثهم وفيها أثر كبير للأفلاطونية الحديثة . أما عن شروح اللاتين للمنطق الأرسططاليسي فلا يذكر مؤرخو الفلسفة الإسلامية شيئاً عن ترجمتها للغة العربية ، غير أنني أستطيع أن أجزم بأن المسلمين ترجموا بعض هذه الشروح ، وأستند في هذا إلى مخطوطة نادرة لأسعد بن علي بن عثمان البانيوي « رسالة في المنطق » (١) يقول المؤلف في مقدمة كتابه أنه أراد دراسة العلم اليوناني في مصادره فطلب الكتب الفلسفية يونانية كانت أو لاتينية حتى وجدها ، ثم أخذ في تعلم اللغتين على يد رجل رومي في القسطنطينية حتى أتقنها . ثم أراد بعد ذلك ترجمة الكتب الآلهية والطبيعية الأرسططاليسية - ولكن لما كانت هذه الكتب مستندة على القانون العقلي وهو المنطق - اضطر إلى البحث في المنطق أولاً - ثم أراد ترجمته وترجم بالفعل إيساغوجي « ليوفوريوس الصوري » وقاتغوريا « وأكثر « باري أرمنياس » . ولكنه رأى أن يشرح قبل القيام بترجمة الأورجانون - ما صعب فهمه من هذه الكتب ، وأن يترك شرح الكتب السهلة منها ، وأن يختصر ما فصل المؤلف من تلك الكتب السهلة . ويذكر في فقرة أخرى أن كتابه هذا سيشتمل على خلاصة آراء حكماء اليونان المتأخرين ثم على خلاصة آراء اللاتين - وأنه سيضمن كتابه على الخصوص الشرح الأنور للحكيم الكامل يوانس فوتنيوس . ويقتبس المؤلف كثيراً من شرح « توما أقوناس » أي توما الأكويني ، ويعرض كثيراً من أقواله . ولهذا الكتاب عندي قيمة كبيرة - أولاً : لأنه يثبت معرفة الإسلاميين لبعض الكتب اللاتينية وترجمتها - ثانياً : تزداد هذه القيمة في أن مؤلف الكتاب نفسه استند في تأليفه إلى الكتب اليونانية واللاتينية مباشرة - ثالثاً : أنه يحاول في كتابه أن يبين ما أضيف إلى الأبحاث الأرسططاليسية من عناصر غير أرسططاليسية . وأخيراً يمكننا أن نقول أن المنطق الأرسططاليسي دخل إلى العالم الإسلامي منذ زمن مبكر ، ثم توالت تراجمه وتكررت . ووصل بجانبه وممتزجاً به أحياناً أبحاث أخرى غير أرسططاليسية أضافها الشراح اليونانيون من بعده من مصادر متعددة . ثم انتقل إلى العالم الإسلامي في وقت متأخر جداً التراث المنطقي اللاتيني ووقف مفكرو الإسلام أمام التراث الأرسططاليسي مواقف متعارضة : أما الهلينيون الإسلاميون - فانقسموا أمامه قسمين ، القسم الأول : فلاسفة الإسلام ، وهم ليسوا في الواقع إلا شراحاً للتراث اليوناني وامتداداً للشراح الاسكندريين المتأخرين ، فقبلوه كوحدة فكرية كاملة واعتبروه قانون العقل الذي لا يتزعزع وحاولوا التوفيق بين العناصر غير الأرسططاليسية وبين منطق أرسطو : وهؤلاء هم الشراح الإسلاميون المشاؤون أو الأفلاطونيون المحدثون . القسم الثاني : فريق من الأصوليين والمتكلمين والمناطقة المتأخرين مالوا إلى الرواقية ورفضوا كثيراً من عناصر منطق أرسطو وهؤلاء هم الشراح الإسلاميون الرواقيون - ولكنهم لم يقفوا عند هذا الحد فحسب بل أضافوا أبحاثاً خاصة بهم - أما المتكلمون والأصوليون الأولون فلم يقبلوا المنطق الأرسططاليسي على الاطلاق وحاولوا إقامة منطق جديد بالكلية في جوهره . ووقف فقهاء أهل السنة والجماعة من المنطق الأرسططاليسي بل من المنطق اليوناني على العموم موقف العداوة التامة واصطنع بعضهم حجج الشكاك اليونانيين وأضاف إليها حججاً ابتدعوها ، ولكن كان لهم بجانب موقفهم الهادم موقف آخر إنشائي . وحارب الصوفية المنطق على العموم ، فهاجم السهروردي المنطق
The Muslims knew Theophrastus and Eudemus and knew conditional propositions and syllogisms, and they were the first to point to them. As for the later commentators, their researches reached the Muslims, and in them there is a great influence of Neoplatonism. As for the Latin commentaries on Aristotelian logic, the historians of Islamic philosophy do not mention anything about their translation into Arabic, but I can certainly assert that the Muslims translated some of these commentaries, and I rely on this to a rare manuscript by As'ad ibn 'Ali ibn 'Uthman al-Banayawi: 'A Treatise on Logic' (1). The author says in the introduction of his book that he wanted to study Greek science from its sources, so he sought philosophical books, Greek or Latin, until he found them, then he began learning the two languages from a Roman man in Constantinople until he mastered them. Then he wanted to translate the Aristotelian theological and natural books - but since these books are based on the intellectual law, which is logic - he was forced to research logic first - then he wanted to translate it and actually translated Isagoge 'of Porphyry' and Categories and most of 'De Interpretatione'. But he saw that before undertaking the translation of the Organon, he should explain what was difficult to understand from these books, and leave the explanation of the easy books, and summarize what the author had detailed from those easy books. And he mentions in another paragraph that this book of his will contain a summary of the opinions of the late Greek sages then a summary of the opinions of the Latins - and that he will especially include in his book the clearest explanation of the perfect sage Yuhanna Fwtnys. And the author quotes extensively from the commentary of 'Thomas Aquinas', i.e. Thomas Aquinas, and presents many of his sayings. And this book has great value for me - firstly: because it proves the Muslims' knowledge of some Latin books and their translation - secondly: this value increases because the author of the book himself relied in his composition on Greek and Latin books directly - thirdly: because he tries in his book to show what was added to Aristotelian researches from non-Aristotelian elements. Finally, we can say that Aristotelian logic entered the Islamic world from an early time, then its translations followed and recurred. And alongside it, and sometimes mixed with it, other non-Aristotelian researches arrived, added by the Greek commentators after him from multiple sources. Then, very late, the Latin logical heritage transferred to the Islamic world, and the thinkers of Islam stood before the Aristotelian heritage with opposing stances: as for the Islamic Hellenists - they divided before it into two groups: the first group: the philosophers of Islam, who are in reality only commentators on the Greek heritage and an extension of the late Alexandrian commentators, so they accepted it as a complete intellectual unit and considered it the unshakable law of reason and tried to reconcile between the non-Aristotelian elements and Aristotle's logic: these are the Islamic Peripatetic or Neoplatonist commentators. The second group: a group of usul scholars, theologians, and later logicians who inclined to Stoicism and rejected many elements of Aristotle's logic, and these are the Islamic Stoic commentators - but they did not stop at this limit only, but added their own researches. As for the early theologians and usul scholars, they did not accept Aristotelian logic at all and tried to establish an entirely new logic in its essence. And the jurists of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a stood against Aristotelian logic, indeed against Greek logic in general, with a stance of complete hostility, and some of them adopted the arguments of the Greek Skeptics and added to them arguments they invented, but besides their destructive stance they had another constructive stance. And the Sufis fought logic in general, so al-Suhrawardi attacked logic...

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
8 min
Diverse Islamic Responses to Aristotelian Logic
Author outlines the diverse stances of Islamic thinkers toward Aristotelian logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize the different Islamic responses to Aristotelian logic into five main groups and identify the core position of each group.

Core Idea
Islamic responses to Aristotelian logic ranged from full embrace to total rejection and reconstruction.

The history of Islamic logic is not monolithic. When Aristotelian logic entered the Islamic world, it was met with a spectrum of reactions. This lesson will guide you through the five main stances described by the author, from the Peripatetic philosophers who accepted it wholly to the Sufis who sought to replace it entirely. Understanding this diversity is crucial for appreciating later developments in Islamic logic and its role in theology, law, and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Trace the entry of Aristotelian logic
Read the first part of the passage to understand how Aristotelian logic came into the Islamic world: early translations, mixing with non-Aristotelian elements from Greek commentators, and later Latin influences. Note that the author mentions three valuable aspects of his work.
This sets the stage for the diversity of responses.
2
Identify the Hellenistic Muslim groups
The author divides Hellenistic Muslims into two: Peripatetic/Neoplatonic (accepted logic as the 'unshakable law of reason') and Stoic-leaning (rejected many elements and added their own). Underline or note this division.
These represent two major philosophical approaches.
3
Distinguish the early mutakallimun and usuliyyun
The early theologians and legal theorists rejected Aristotelian logic entirely and attempted to build a completely new logic. How does this differ from the Stoic-leaning group?
They sought a total replacement, not just modification.
4
Analyze the Sunni jurist and Sufi positions
Sunni jurists were hostile, sometimes borrowing from Greek skeptics and inventing their own arguments, while also having a constructive side. Sufis, exemplified by Suhrawardi, attacked logic and attempted to create a compressed alternative. Compare their strategies.
Both had destructive and constructive aspects.
5
Synthesize the map of responses
Create a mental map or list of the five groups: Peripatetic, Stoic, early mutakallimun/usuliyyun, Sunni jurists, and Sufis. For each, recall whether they accepted, modified, rejected, or replaced Aristotelian logic.
This summary will help you see the patterns in the following chapters.

لبعض الكتب اللاتينية وترجمتها - ثانياً : تزداد هذه القيمة في أن مؤلف الكتاب نفسه استند في تأليفه إلى الكتب اليونانية واللاتينية مباشرة - ثالثاً : أنه يحاول في كتابه أن يبين ما أضيف إلى الأبحاث الأرسططاليسية من عناصر غير أرسططاليسية(١) . وأخيراً يمكننا أن نقول أن المنطق الأرسططاليسي دخل إلى العالم الإسلامي منذ زمن مبكر ، ثم توالت تراجمه وتكررت . ووصل بجانبه وممتزجاً به أحياناً أبحاث أخرى غير أرسططاليسية أضافها الشراح اليونانيون من بعده من مصادر متعددة . ثم انتقل إلى العالم الإسلامي في وقت متأخر جداً التراث المنطقي اللاتيني ووقف مفكرو الإسلام أمام التراث الأرسططاليسي مواقف متعارضة : أما الهلينيون الإسلاميون - ولكنهم لم يقفوا عند هذا الحد فحسب بل أضافوا أبحاثاً خاصة بهم - أما المتكلمون والأصوليون الأولون فلم يقبلوا المنطق الأرسططاليسي على الاطلاق وحاولوا إقامة منطق جديد بالكلية في جوهره . ووقف فقهاء أهل السنة والجماعة من المنطق الأرسططاليسي بل من المنطق اليوناني على العموم موقف العداوة التامة واصطنع بعضهم حجج الشكاك اليونانيين وأضاف إليها حججاً ابتدعوها ، ولكن كان لهم بجانب موقفهم الهادم موقف آخر إنشائي . وحارب الصوفية المنطق على العموم ، فهاجم السهروردي المنطق الأرسططاليسي أشد هجوم ، وقام بمحاولة منطقية جديدة لاختصار منطق اليونان . هذا هو موقف مفكري الإسلام من المنطق الأرسططاليسي بل ومن المنطق اليوناني على العموم . وهو موضوع بحثنا . وسنبدأ في الباب القادم في عرض موقف الشراح - مشائين كانوا أو رواقيين منه - ثم نتابع فيما يلي من الأبواب موقف طوائف الإسلام - التي ذكرناها آنفاً - منه ثم نبين آخر الأمر الدافع الحقيقي الذي دفع بالمسلمين إلى هذا النقد ، وأي طائفة من المسلمين يتمثل فيها هذا الدافع .
For some Latin books and their translation - secondly: this value increases in that the author of the book himself relied in his writing on Greek and Latin books directly - thirdly: that he tries in his book to show what was added to Aristotelian research from non-Aristotelian elements(1). And finally we can say that Aristotelian logic entered the Islamic world from an early time, then its translations followed one after another and were repeated. And alongside it and sometimes mixed with it arrived other non-Aristotelian researches added by the Greek commentators after him from multiple sources. Then the Latin logical heritage transferred to the Islamic world at a very late time, and the thinkers of Islam stood before the Aristotelian heritage with contradictory stances: As for the Hellenistic Muslims - they divided before it into two groups. The first group: the philosophers of Islam, who are in reality nothing but commentators on the Greek heritage and an extension of the later Alexandrian commentators, so they accepted it as a complete intellectual unit and considered it the unshakable law of reason and tried to reconcile the non-Aristotelian elements with Aristotle's logic: these are the Islamic Peripatetic or Neoplatonic commentators. The second group: a group of late usuliyyun, mutakallimun, and logicians who leaned toward Stoicism and rejected many elements of Aristotle's logic, and these are the Islamic Stoic commentators - but they did not stop at this limit only, but added their own researches. As for the early mutakallimun and usuliyyun, they did not accept Aristotelian logic at all and tried to establish an entirely new logic in its essence. And the Sunni jurists took a stance of complete hostility toward Aristotelian logic and even toward Greek logic in general, and some of them adopted the arguments of the Greek skeptics and added to them arguments they invented, but alongside their destructive stance they had another constructive stance. And the Sufis fought logic in general, as Suhrawardi attacked Aristotelian logic most severely and undertook a new logical attempt to abbreviate the logic of the Greeks. This is the stance of the thinkers of Islam toward Aristotelian logic and even toward Greek logic in general. And it is the subject of our research. And we will begin in the next chapter in presenting the stance of the commentators - whether Peripatetic or Stoic - toward it, then we will follow in the subsequent chapters the stance of the Islamic groups - which we mentioned earlier - toward it, then we will finally clarify the real motive that drove the Muslims to this criticism, and which group of Muslims embodies this motive.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to the book Manahij Bahth, where the author sets the historical context for his study. He begins by mentioning three values of the work: that it relies directly on Greek and Latin sources, that the author used Latin books, and that he aims to identify non-Aristotelian elements in Aristotelian research. Then he provides an overview of how Aristotelian logic entered the Islamic world early and was mixed with other traditions from Greek commentators. He then presents the contradictory responses of Islamic thinkers. He divides the Hellenistic Muslims into two: the Peripatetic/Neoplatonic philosophers who accepted logic as the undoubted law of reason, and the Stoic-leaning usuliyyun, mutakallimun, and logicians who rejected many elements but added their own. Next, he describes the early mutakallimun and usuliyyun who totally rejected Aristotelian logic and sought a new logic. Sunni jurists were entirely hostile, sometimes borrowing from Greek skeptics, but also had a constructive side. Sufis, exemplified by Suhrawardi, attacked logic vigorously and attempted to create a compressed alternative. The author concludes that this diversity is his research subject and outlines the plan for the following chapters: first the commentators, then other Islamic groups, and finally the real motive behind Muslim criticism.

Key Terms
المنطق الأرسططاليسي
الهلينيون الإسلاميون

The Book's Three-Part Plan on Islamic Responses to Greek Logic
Author outlines the book's plan for studying Islamic responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline the three-part plan the author proposes for studying Islamic responses to Greek logic.

Core Idea
The book systematically moves from commentators to Islamic groups to the underlying motive for criticism.

This passage is the author's roadmap. Before diving into content, he tells us exactly how the book is structured: three main parts. Understanding this plan will help you navigate the rest of the work and see how each chapter connects to the central question of why and how Muslims criticized Greek logic.

Guided Path

2
Identify the second subject
After the commentators, the author will present the position of 'طوائف الإسلام' (Islamic groups). Note that he says they were mentioned earlier.
3
Identify the third subject
Finally, the author will show 'الدافع الحقيقي' (the real motive) behind Muslim criticism and which group embodies it.

This sets up the structure for the subsequent chapters, indicating a systematic study of historical responses.

Introduction: Context of Islamic Logic
Chapter heading introducing Aristotelian logic in Islamic tradition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the focus of this chapter and distinguish between the roles of commentators and revivers in the Islamic logical tradition.

Core Idea
Islamic logicians did not just copy Aristotle; they critically engaged and expanded his system.

This chapter heading sets the stage for understanding how Aristotelian logic was transmitted and transformed in Islamic civilization. The terms 'commentators' and 'revivers' point to a dynamic history of both preservation and innovation.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter heading
Note the Arabic: 'الباب الأول المنطق الأرسططاليسي بين أيدي الشراح والمنهضين الإسلاميين'. This announces the theme of the first chapter.
2
Identify the three key elements
The heading contains three main components: (1) Aristotelian logic, (2) the commentators, (3) the Islamic revivers. Understand each element's significance.
3
Define each term
Look up the key terms: 'المنطق الأرسططاليسي' refers to the logical system attributed to Aristotle. 'الشراح' are those who wrote commentaries. 'المنهضين الإسلاميين' are those who revived the study of logic in Islam.
The revivers often came after periods of decline and sought to re-establish logic within Islamic education.
4
Reflect on the relationship between them
Consider how commentators and revivers interacted: commentators preserved and explained the texts; revivers brought them back into active use and critique.
This sets the stage for later discussions of specific figures like al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and Ibn Taymiyya.

This sets the context for the subsequent material, which likely analyzes contributions of figures like al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and later logicians who both preserved and critiqued Aristotelian logic from an Islamic perspective.

The Multiple Sources of Islamic Logic
Author discusses the multiple sources of Islamic logic beyond Aristotle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the multiple sources of Islamic logic as distinct from pure Aristotelian logic.

Core Idea
Islamic logic is a synthesis of Aristotelian, Stoic, Neoplatonic, and Arabic linguistic traditions.

Many assume Islamic logic is merely a translation of Aristotle's Organon. This passage challenges that assumption by revealing the rich tapestry of influences that shaped the Islamic logical tradition. Understanding these sources equips you to recognize the unique contributions of Islamic scholars and to trace the origins of specific logical tools used in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Main Claim
Read the author's opening statement: Islamic scholars did not simply adopt Aristotelian logic passively. They interpreted, completed, and opposed it. This sets the stage for a broader view of sources.
2
List the Modes of Engagement
The author mentions three modes: tafsir (interpretation), takmil (completion), and mu'arada (opposition). Note that each mode implies a creative interaction, not mere copying.
3
Extract the Non-Aristotelian Sources
The author explicitly lists Peripatetic (successors of Aristotle), Stoic, Neoplatonic elements, and Arabic linguistic studies. Write down each source.
Stoic logic is known indirectly; Neoplatonism influenced definitions.
4
Evaluate the Evidence
The author cites the loss of Stoic texts and the need for indirect study. Consider why this makes the reconstruction of sources challenging.
5
Apply the General Principle
The author concludes that no philosophy has a single source. Reflect on how this principle applies to other Islamic sciences like fiqh or kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَحَثَ الإِسْلَامِيُّونَ إِذَنْ المَنْطِقَ الأَرِسْطَاطَالِيسِيَّ مُنْذُ وَقْتٍ مُبَكِّرٍ - وَلَكِنَّهُمْ لَمْ يَبْحَثُوهُ، كَمَا تَرَكَهُ مُؤَلِّفُ الأُورْغَانُونِ نَفْسُهُ، بَلْ خِلَالَ أَبْحَاثٍ مُفَسِّرَةٍ لَهُ أَحْيَانًا، وَمُكَمِّلَةٍ لَهُ تَارَةً، وَمُعَارِضَةٍ لَهُ طَوْرًا، بِحَيْثُ يَتَحَتَّمُ عَلَى بَاحِثِ مَنْطِقِ الشُّرَّاحِ الإِسْلَامِيِّينَ أَنْ يَعُودَ إِلَى التُّرَاثِ المَنْطِقِيِّ الهِيلِينِيِّ بَعْدَ أَرِسْطُو جَمِيعِهِ، وَلِكَيْ يَتَبَيَّنَ مَصَادِرَ هَذَا المَنْطِقِ الإِسْلَامِيِّ - عَلَى مَا فِي هَذِهِ الفَتْرَةِ الأَخِيرَةِ، مِنْ تَارِيخِ اليُونَانِ الفِكْرِيِّ مِنْ غُمُوضٍ. فَقَدْ كَثُرَ كُتُبُ هَذَا العَهْدِ الأَخِيرِ، وَخَاصَّةً لَدَى المَدْرَسَةِ الفَلْسَفِيَّةِ الحَقِيقِيَّةِ الرُّوَاقِيَّةِ ( - الَّتِي ظَهَرَتْ بَعْدَ أَرِسْطُو وَالَّتِي كَانَ لَهَا فِي مَيْدَانِ المَنْطِقِ، كَمَا كَانَ لَهَا فِي بَقِيَّةِ فُرُوعِ الفَلْسَفَةِ الأُخْرَى - تَفْكِيرٌ خَاصٌّ يَرَى بَعْضُ المُؤَرِّخِينَ أَنَّهُ يُخَالِفُ الفِكْرَ الأَرِسْطَاطَالِيسِيَّ) بِحَيْثُ لَا نَسْتَطِيعُ بَحْثَ فَلْسَفَةِ الرُّوَاقِيِّينَ وَمَنْطِقِهِمْ إِلَّا فِي ضَوْءِ دِرَاسَةٍ غَيْرِ مُبَاشِرَةٍ لِنُصُوصِ غَيْرِهِمْ أَوْ أَعْدَائِهِمْ مِنْ فَلَاسِفَةِ اليُونَانِ. وَعَلَى العُمُومِ إِنَّنَا لَا نَسْتَطِيعُ أَنْ نَرُدَّ مَنْطِقَ الشُّرَّاحِ الإِسْلَامِيِّينَ - إِلَى مَصْدَرٍ وَاحِدٍ - هُوَ المَنْطِقُ الأَرِسْطَاطَالِيسِيُّ. بَلْ دَخَلَتْ فِي هَذَا المَنْطِقِ عَنَاصِرُ مَشَّائِيَّةٌ وَضَعَهَا تَلَامِذَةُ أَرِسْطُو مِنْ بَعْدِهِ. وَدَخَلَتْ فِيهِ عَنَاصِرُ رُوَاقِيَّةٌ وَأَفْلَاطُونِيَّةٌ مُحْدَثَةٌ وَأَثَّرَتْ عَلَيْهِ الدِّرَاسَاتُ اللُّغَوِيَّةُ العَرَبِيَّةُ أَشَدَّ تَأْثِيرٍ. وَمِنَ الوَاضِحِ أَنَّنَا لَا نَسْتَطِيعُ أَنْ نَرُدَّ أُصُولَ فَيْلَسُوفٍ مِنْ فَلَاسِفَةٍ أَوْ مَذْهَبٍ مِنَ المَذَاهِبِ الفَلْسَفِيَّةِ إِلَى مَصْدَرٍ فِكْرِيٍّ وَاحِدٍ، بَلْ مِنَ المُحْتَمِ أَنْ يَتَأَثَّرَ كُلُّ فِكْرٍ إِنْسَانِيٍّ بِتِيَّارَاتٍ مُتَعَدِّدَةٍ وَفِكَرٍ مُخْتَلِفَةٍ.
Islamic scholars examined Aristotelian logic from an early time. However, they did not examine it as the author of the Organon himself left it, but through researches that sometimes interpreted it, sometimes completed it, and sometimes opposed it, such that a researcher of the logic of the Islamic commentators must return to the entire Hellenistic logical tradition after Aristotle, in order to discern the sources of this Islamic logic — despite the obscurity in this later period of Greek intellectual history. For many books of this later era have been lost, especially in the true Stoic philosophical school (which appeared after Aristotle and had, in the field of logic as in other branches of philosophy, a particular thought that some historians consider to contradict Aristotelian thought), such that we cannot study the philosophy and logic of the Stoics except in light of an indirect study of the texts of others or their enemies among Greek philosophers. In general, we cannot trace the logic of the Islamic commentators to a single source — Aristotelian logic. Rather, into this logic entered Peripatetic elements placed by Aristotle's successors. Stoic and Neoplatonic elements entered it, and Arabic linguistic studies influenced it profoundly. It is clear that we cannot trace the origins of any philosopher or philosophical school to a single intellectual source; rather, it is inevitable that every human thought is influenced by multiple currents and different ideas.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is an introductory methodological discussion from the author of Manahij Bahth, setting forth the historical background of Islamic logic. The author begins by asserting that Islamic scholars engaged with Aristotelian logic from an early period, but not in a passive or purely faithful manner. Instead, their engagement took three forms: interpretation (tafsīr), completion (takmīl), and opposition (muʿāraḍa). Consequently, the author argues, anyone studying the logic of the Islamic commentators must examine the entire Hellenistic logical tradition after Aristotle, because the Islamic commentators drew on multiple sources. The author acknowledges the difficulty of this task due to the obscurity of later Greek intellectual history and the loss of many texts, especially those of the Stoic school. The Stoics, who emerged after Aristotle, developed an independent logical system that some historians view as opposing Aristotelian logic; however, our knowledge of Stoic logic is indirect, based on texts of their opponents. The main thesis of this passage is that Islamic logic is not reducible to a single Aristotelian source. Rather, it incorporated Peripatetic elements from Aristotle's immediate successors, Stoic elements, Neoplatonic elements, and was deeply impacted by Arabic linguistic studies. The author concludes with a general principle: no philosophical thought can be traced to a single intellectual source; every human thought is influenced by multiple currents and different ideas.

Key Terms
المنطق الأرسططاليسي
المنطق الإسلامي
المدرسة الرواقية
أفلاطونية محدثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Islamic logic is a composite tradition shaped by multiple Greek sources and Arabic linguistic studies, not a mere copy of Aristotle.

The Synthetic Nature of Islamic Logic
Author argues Islamic logic derives from multiple Hellenistic sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the multiple sources of Islamic logic as outlined by the author and explain why it cannot be reduced to Aristotelian logic alone.

Core Idea
Islamic logic is a mosaic of Greek schools and Arabic linguistics, not a copy of Aristotle.

This lesson examines a key passage from the author's introduction, which argues that Islamic logic is not a mere repetition of Aristotle but a creative synthesis. The author highlights Peripatetic, Stoic, Neoplatonic elements, and strong Arabic linguistic influences. Understanding this helps us approach Islamic logical texts with the right historical perspective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim
Read the first sentence: the author states that Islamic logic is not a single-source tradition. Highlight the phrase 'لا نستطيع أن نرد منطق الشراح الإسلاميين إلى مصدر واحد'.
2
List the sources mentioned
Scan for explicit mentions: Aristotelian logic, Peripatetic elements, Stoic elements, Neoplatonic elements, and Arabic linguistic studies. Note each in your own words.
The author uses 'عناصر مشائية' for Peripatetic and 'عناصر رواقية' for Stoic.
3
Understand the author's justification
The author explains why: Muslims interpreted, supplemented, and opposed Aristotle; many Hellenistic texts, especially Stoic, are lost so we know them indirectly. This justifies the complexity.
The loss of Stoic texts is a key historical point.
4
Reflect on the historiographical implication
Consider: if Islamic logic were purely Aristotelian, we could ignore other schools. But the author insists we must return to 'التراث المنطقي الهليني بعد أرسطو جميعه'.
This guides how we read Islamic logical works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بحث الإسلاميون إذن المنطق الأرسططاليسي منذ وقت مبكر ـ ولكنهم لم يبحثوه، كما تركه مؤلف الأورجانون نفسه، بل خلال أبحاث مفسرة له أحياناً، ومكملة له تارة، ومعارضة له طوراً، بحيث يتحتم على باحث منطق الشراح الإسلاميين أن يعود إلى التراث المنطقي الهليني بعد أرسطو جميعه، ولكي يتبين مصادر هذا المنطق الإسلامي ـ على ما في هذه الفترة الأخيرة، من تاريخ اليونان الفكري من غموض. فقد كثير من كتب هذا العهد الأخير، وخاصة لدى المدرسة الفلسفية الحقيقية الرواقية (ـ التي ظهرت بعد أرسطو والتي كان لها في ميدان المنطق، كما كان لها في بقية فروع الفلسفة الأخرى ـ تفكير خاص يرى بعض المؤرخون أنه يخالف الفكر الارسططاليسي) بحيث لا نستطيع بحث فلسفة الرواقيين ومنطقهم إلا في ضوء دراسة غير مباشرة لنصوص غيرهم أو أعدائهم من فلاسفة اليونان. وعلى العموم إننا لا نستطيع أن نرد منطق الشراح الإسلاميين ـ إلى مصدر واحد ـ هو المنطق الأرسططاليسي. بل دخلت في هذا المنطق عناصر مشائية وضعها تلامذة أرسطو من بعده. ودخلت فيه عناصر رواقية وأفلاطونية محدثة وأثرت عليه الدراسات اللغوية العربية أشد تأثير. ومن الواضح اننا لا نستطيع أن نرد أصول فيلسوف من فلاسفة أو مذهب من المذاهب الفلسفية إلى مصدر فكري واحد، بل من المحتم أن يتأثر كل فكر إنساني بتيارات متعددة وفكر مختلفة.
Indeed, the Muslims studied Aristotelian logic from an early time, but they did not study it as its author, the author of the Organon, left it; rather, through researches that sometimes interpreted it, sometimes supplemented it, and sometimes opposed it, such that a researcher of the logic of the Islamic commentators must return to the entire Hellenistic logical heritage after Aristotle. And in order to identify the sources of this Islamic logic, given the obscurity in this last period of Greek intellectual history, many books of this last era have been lost, especially from the true philosophical school, the Stoic school, which appeared after Aristotle and had in the field of logic, as in the rest of the branches of philosophy, a distinctive thought that some historians see as contradicting Aristotelian thought, so that we cannot research Stoic philosophy and logic except through indirect study of texts of others or their enemies from Greek philosophers. In general, we cannot trace the logic of the Islamic commentators to a single source—Aristotelian logic. Rather, Peripatetic elements placed by Aristotle's students after him entered this logic. Stoic and Neoplatonic elements entered it, and Arabic linguistic studies influenced it most strongly. It is clear that we cannot trace the origins of any philosopher or philosophical school to a single intellectual source; rather, every human thought is necessarily influenced by multiple currents and different thoughts.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is an introductory analysis of the sources of Islamic logic. The author begins by stating that Muslims studied Aristotelian logic early but did not take it as Aristotle left it. Instead, they interpreted, supplemented, and opposed it. Therefore, a researcher must consider the entire Hellenistic tradition after Aristotle. The author notes that many texts from that later period are lost, especially from the Stoic school, which had a distinctive logical theory differing from Aristotle's. Our knowledge of Stoic logic comes indirectly through non-Stoic sources. The author concludes that Islamic logic cannot be reduced to Aristotle alone; it includes Peripatetic elements (from Aristotle's students), Stoic elements, Neoplatonic elements, and was strongly influenced by Arabic linguistic studies. The passage emphasizes the synthetic nature of Islamic logic, a theme that will recur throughout the book.

Key Terms
المنطق الأرسططاليسي
الشراح الإسلاميين
عناصر مشائية
عناصر رواقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Peripatetic
B) Stoic
C) Neoplatonic
D) Arabic linguistic

Key Takeaway
Islamic logic is a synthesis of Aristotelian, Peripatetic, Stoic, Neoplatonic, and Arabic linguistic elements, not a single-source tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the three Hellenistic schools mentioned by the author as sources for Islamic logic, and give one example of a concept from each that appears in later Islamic logic texts.
?

Two Schools of Commentators: Peripatetic and Stoic
Author divides Islamic commentators into Peripatetic and Stoic schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Peripatetic and Stoic approaches among Islamic commentators and identify their differing treatment of the logical tradition.

Core Idea
Islamic logicians split into Peripatetic syncretists and independent Stoic adopters—each shaping logic differently.

This passage introduces a foundational classification for understanding the historical development of Islamic logic. The author divides commentators into two camps: those who tried to harmonize all logic under Aristotle (Peripatetics) and those who adopted Stoic positions without reconciling them with Aristotelianism. Recognizing these schools helps explain why some later logical discussions appear contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Read the first sentence: the commentators split into two teams — the Peripatetic (al-mashshāʾiyyūn) and the Stoic (al-rawāqiyyūn).
2
Analyze the Peripatetic method
Notice how they treated all logical heritage as a unity belonging to Aristotle, even when they knew some topics were not his. They used a 'syncretic method' to force attributions.
They justify this via later Greek commentators and their own imagination.
3
Analyze the Stoic method
The Stoic group took Stoic views where they differed from Aristotle, but did not mention the contradiction, attribute the views to their sources, or attempt reconciliation.
They adopted Stoic doctrines silently.
4
Compare the two approaches
Both groups avoid openly acknowledging conflict with Aristotle: Peripatetics absorb everything into Aristotle; Stoics adopt opposing views without naming them. This sets up a key tension in the history of Islamic logic.
We will see this clearly in the discussion of conditional syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وانقسم الشراح الإسلاميون أمام هذا المنطق إلى فريقين ـ كما قلنا ـ فريق مشائي وفريق رواقي. أما الفريق الأول فلم ير ـ اللهم إلا ابن رشد وأبو البركات البغدادي في بعض الأحيان ـ في هذا التراث أي تعارض أو تناقض، واجهوه بالمنهج التوفيقي La Méthode syncrétiste ـ هذا المنهج الذي كان ميزة الإسلاميين في بحثهم لشتى نواحي المعرفة الإنسانية فجاء مزاجاً غريباً من عناصر شتى. فاعتبر التراث المنطقي كله وحدة نادرة المثال لأرسطو فحسب. وعلى الرغم من أن الشراح المشائيين الإسلاميين وصل اليهم أن بعض هذه المباحث ليست للمعلم الأول، إلا أنهم حاولوا في تعسف وتكلف ظاهرين ـ أن ينسبوها إليه ويعللوا هذه النسبة بما ذكره المتأخرون من شراح أرسطو اليونانيين من ناحية وبما شاء لهم فكرهم أن يتخيلوه من ناحية أخرى وسنرى هذا بوضوح في مبحث القياسات الشرطية. أما الفريق الرواقي فأخذ بآراء المدرسة الرواقية المنطقية فيما اختلفت فيه الرواقية مع الأرسططاليسية، ولكنه لم يذكر أن هذه الآراء تعارض آراء أرسطو، كما أنه لم ينسبها إلى أصحابها الأصليين، ولم يحاول أن يوفق بينها وبين الأرسططاليسية نفسها. كان هناك إذن اتجاهان متعارضان في منطق الشراح يخالف أحدهما الآخر إلى أقصى الحدود. وسنرى هذا في المسائل العامة التي سنعرض لها الآن والتي تكون مقدمات المنطق كعلم.
And the Islamic commentators were divided before this logic into two groups—as we said—a Peripatetic group and a Stoic group. As for the first group, they did not see—except for Ibn Rushd and Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi at times—in this heritage any contradiction or inconsistency; they faced it with the syncretist method—this method which was a characteristic of the Muslims in their investigation of various aspects of human knowledge, so it became a strange mixture of diverse elements. They considered the entire logical heritage as a rare unity belonging only to Aristotle. And despite the fact that the Islamic Peripatetic commentators knew that some of these topics were not from the First Teacher, they tried, with evident arbitrariness and affectation, to attribute them to him and to justify this attribution by what the later Greek commentators of Aristotle mentioned on one hand and by what their minds imagined on the other. We will see this clearly in the discussion of conditional syllogisms. As for the Stoic group, they adopted the views of the Stoic logical school where the Stoics differed from the Aristotelians, but they did not mention that these views contradict Aristotle's views, nor did they attribute them to their original owners, nor did they try to reconcile them with Aristotelianism itself. There were, therefore, two conflicting tendencies in the logic of the commentators, each differing from the other to the extreme. We will see this in the general issues that we will now present, which constitute the preliminaries of logic as a science.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage classifies Islamic commentators into two schools based on their handling of the logical tradition. The Peripatetic group, except for Ibn Rushd and sometimes Abu al-Barakat, adopted a syncretic method, treating all logical heritage as a unified whole attributable solely to Aristotle. Even when they knew some topics were not Aristotle's, they forced attributions to him. The Stoic group, by contrast, adopted Stoic views wherever they differed from Aristotle, but without acknowledging the conflict or attributing the views to their original sources. The author describes these as two opposing tendencies that will manifest in the general issues to be discussed. This sets the stage for understanding the divergent treatments of logical topics by later commentators.

Key Terms
المنهج التوفيقي (al-manhaj al-tawfīqī) - syncretic method
المشائيون (al-mashshāʾiyyūn) - Peripatetic commentators
الرواقيون (al-rawāqiyyūn) - Stoic commentators

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How did the Peripatetic commentators treat logical materials that were not originally from Aristotle?
A) They rejected them as inauthentic
B) They attributed them to Aristotle despite knowing otherwise
C) They openly attributed them to Stoic sources
D) They ignored them entirely

Key Takeaway
Islamic commentators split into two opposing tendencies: Peripatetics who syncretically attributed all logic to Aristotle, and Stoics who adopted Stoic views without reconciliation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following passage from an Islamic commentator: 'The conditional syllogism is a valid form of inference, but it must be reduced to the categorical form.' Determine whether the commentator is likely following the Peripatetic or Stoic school, citing evidence from the passage.
?
Explain in one paragraph why the Peripatetic commentators might have been motivated to attribute non-Aristotelian material to Aristotle, using the concept of 'syncretist method' as described in the source.

Two Schools of Islamic Logicians: Peripatetic and Stoic
Classifies Islamic commentators into Peripatetic and Stoic camps.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Peripatetic and Stoic schools of Islamic logicians based on their approach to the Aristotelian logical heritage.

Core Idea
Islamic logicians split into two schools over how to handle non-Aristotelian elements: Peripatetics syncretized, Stoics adopted without reconciliation.

The Islamic commentators on Aristotelian logic did not all follow the same path. As this passage explains, they divided into two distinct schools: the Peripatetics, who saw all logical works as a unified whole belonging to Aristotle, and the Stoics, who adopted Stoic views where they differed. Understanding these two approaches is crucial for interpreting later logical debates in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two schools
The passage explicitly names the two groups: Peripatetic (mashshā’ī) and Stoic (rawāqī). Write them down.
2
Characterize the Peripatetic approach
Find what the Peripatetics believed about contradictions in the logical heritage. They saw no contradiction and used a syncretic method.
3
Characterize the Stoic approach
Find how the Stoics handled differences with Aristotelianism. They adopted Stoic opinions without reconciliation.
4
Contrast their attitudes toward attribution
The Peripatetics strained to attribute non-Aristotelian ideas to Aristotle; the Stoics did not mention conflict or attribute to original sources.
5
Predict implications
Consider how these different approaches might affect later logical topics like conditional syllogisms, which the author mentions as an example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وانقسم الشراح الإسلاميون أمام هذا المنطق إلى فريقين ـ كما قلنا ـ فريق مشائي وفريق رواقي . أما الفريق الأول فلم ير ـ اللهم إلا ابن رشد وأبو البركات البغدادي في بعض الأحيان ـ في هذا التراث أي تعارض أو تناقض ، واجهوه بالمنهج التوفيقي La Méthode syncrétiste ـ هذا المنهج الذي كان ميزة الإسلاميين في بحثهم لشتى نواحي المعرفة الإنسانية فجاء مزاجاً غريباً من عناصر شتى . فاعتبر التراث المنطقي كله وحدة نادرة المثال لأرسطو فحسب . وعلى الرغم من أن الشراح المشائيين الإسلاميين وصل اليهم أن بعض هذه المباحث ليست للمعلم الأول ، إلا أنهم حاولوا في تعسف وتكلف ظاهرين ـ أن ينسبوها إليه ويعللوا هذه النسبة بما ذكره المتأخرون من شراح أرسطو اليونانيين من ناحية وبما شاء لهم فكرهم أن يتخيلوه من ناحية أخرى وسنرى هذا بوضوح في مبحث القياسات الشرطية . أما الفريق الرواقي فأخذ بآراء المدرسة الرواقية المنطقية فيما اختلفت فيه الرواقية مع الأرسططاليسية ، ولكنه لم يذكر أن هذه الآراء تعارض آراء أرسطو ، كما أنه لم ينسبها إلى أصحابها الأصليين ، ولم يحاول أن يوفق بينها وبين الأرسططاليسية نفسها . كان هناك إذن اتجاهان متعارضان في منطق الشراح يخالف أحدهما الآخر إلى أقصى الحدود . وسنرى هذا في المسائل العامة التي سنعرض لها الآن والتي تكون مقدمات المنطق كعلم .
The Islamic commentators divided before this logic into two groups — as we said — a Peripatetic group and a Stoic group. As for the first group, they did not see — except for Ibn Rushd and Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi on occasion — any contradiction or inconsistency in this heritage; they faced it with the syncretic method (La Méthode syncrétiste) — the method that was the distinguishing feature of the Islamic scholars in their investigation of various aspects of human knowledge, resulting in a strange mixture of diverse elements. So they considered the entire logical heritage a rare unity belonging to Aristotle alone. And although the Islamic Peripatetic commentators understood that some of these investigations were not by the First Teacher (Aristotle), they attempted, with apparent distortion and affectation, to attribute them to him and to justify this attribution by what the later Greek commentators of Aristotle mentioned on the one hand, and by what their thought imagined on the other hand. We will see this clearly in the topic of conditional syllogisms. As for the Stoic group, they adopted the opinions of the Stoic logical school where the Stoics differed from the Aristotelians, but they did not mention that these opinions contradict Aristotle's opinions, nor did they attribute them to their original proponents, nor did they attempt to reconcile them with Aristotelianism itself. There were thus two opposing trends in the logic of the commentators, each conflicting with the other to the utmost. We will see this in the general issues that we will now present, which constitute the introduction to logic as a science.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage classifies the Islamic commentators on Aristotelian logic into two schools: the Peripatetic and the Stoic. The author begins by stating that the commentators split into two groups. The first group, the Peripatetics (except for Ibn Rushd and Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi at times), did not see any contradiction in the logical heritage and approached it with a syncretic method, mixing various elements. They considered all logical works to be a unified whole belonging to Aristotle, and when they encountered doctrines not originally from Aristotle, they strained to attribute them to him, using later Greek commentators and their own imagination. The second group, the Stoics, adopted Stoic logical views where these differed from Aristotelianism, but they did not acknowledge the conflict, did not credit the original sources, and did not reconcile them. Thus there were two opposing trends. The author signals that this will be seen in the general issues that form the introduction to logic. All examples and explanations in this passage are historical and not related to Islamic sciences directly, but the passage itself is about the history of logic.

Key Terms
الشراح الإسلاميون
فريق مشائي
فريق رواقي
المنهج التوفيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
