A) They rejected them outright.
B) They attributed them to Aristotle with forced justifications.
C) They ignored them entirely.
D) They accepted them as Stoic innovations.

Key Takeaway
The two schools differed fundamentally in how they handled non-Aristotelian material: Peripatetics syncretized and attributed everything to Aristotle, while Stoics adopted Stoic views without reconciling them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the following statement from an unnamed Islamic logician: 'The logical works of Aristotle are complete and contain all valid reasoning; any apparent additions are simply clarifications of his original thought.' Which school (Peripatetic or Stoic) does this statement likely belong to? Justify your answer using the criteria from the passage.
?
Consider the author's mention of conditional syllogisms. In a later lesson, you will learn that the Stoics developed a detailed theory of conditional syllogisms. Explain how a Peripatetic commentator might treat these syllogisms based on the syncretic method described.

Three Views on Logic's Relationship to Philosophy
Author surveys the historical dispute on logic's relationship to philosophy among Islamic scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three main views on logic's relationship to philosophy as presented by Islamic historians of science.

Core Idea
The debate over logic's status reflects deeper epistemological divisions about the nature of philosophical inquiry.

In this passage, the author surveys the historical debate among Islamic scholars about whether logic is an integral part of philosophy, a mere tool for it, or both. By examining the accounts of al-Khwarizmi and al-Tahanawi, you will learn to recognize the three positions and understand their implications for how logic is studied and valued.

Guided Path
1
Identify the views listed by al-Khwarizmi
Al-Khwarizmi enumerates four opinions: some make logic a third part after theoretical and practical, some a part of theoretical science, some a tool, and some both part and tool.
2
Analyze al-Tahanawi's refinement
Al-Tahanawi asks first whether logic is a science; if it is not, it cannot be part of wisdom. If it is a science, it may be entirely theoretical or partly practical.
He introduces the criterion of secondary intelligibles to exclude logic from wisdom defined as knowledge of external objects.
3
Compare the two accounts
Al-Tahanawi omits the view that logic is both a preface and a part, while al-Khwarizmi includes it. This omission may reflect a later hardening of positions.
The author highlights this difference explicitly.
4
Consider the definitional consequence
Those who define wisdom as knowledge of external objects (al-a'yan) exclude logic because its subject is mental existents (secondary intelligibles). This shows how definitions of wisdom shape the inclusion or exclusion of logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرواقية فقد اعتبرت المنطق جزءاً من الحكمة . فالحكمة عندها تنقسم إلى العلم الطبيعي والجدل والأخلاق ، والجدل هو المنطق . لم يقف الشراح الإسكندريون أمام هذين الاتجاهين المختلفين موقف الحيرة والشك ، بل سرعان ما قاموا بالتوفيق بينهما متطابقين في ذلك مع منهجهم التنسيقي . فاعتبروا المنطق مقدمة للفلسفة وجزءاً منها في الوقت عينه . ويصور الخوارزمي (١) ( ٣٨٧هـ - ٩٧٧م ) في كتابه مفاتيح العلوم هذا النزاع « الفلسفة مشتقة من كلمة يونانية وهي « فيلاسوفيا » ومعناها محبة الحكمة ، فلما عربت قيل فيلسوف ، ثم اشتقت الفلسفة منه ـ ومعنى الفلسفة علم حقائق الأشياء والعمل بما هو أصلح . وتنقسم قسمين أحدهما الجزء النظري والآخر الجزء العملي . ومنهم من جعل المنطق جزءاً ثالثاً غير هذين ، ومنهم من جعله جزءاً من أجزاء العلم النظري ، ومنهم من جعله آلة للفلسفة ومنهم من جعله جزءاً منها وآلة لها » (٢) . ويذكر هذا النزاع التهانوي في صورة تقرب من الخوارزمي فيقول « إعلم أنهم اختلفوا في أن المنطق من العلم أم لا : فمن قال إنه ليس بعلم فليس بحكمة عنده إذ الحكمة علم . ومن قال بأنه علم اختلفوا في أنه من الحكمة أم لا . والقائلون بأنه من الحكمة يمكن الاختلاف بينهم بأنه من الحكمة النظرية جميعاً أم لا . بل بعضه منها ـ وبعضه من العملية . إذ الموجود الذهني قد يكون بقدرتنا واختيارنا وقد لا يكون كذلك . والقائلون بأنه من الحكمة النظرية يمكن الاختلاف بينهم بأنه من أقسامها الثلاثة أم قسم آخر . فمن أخذ في تعريفها قيد الأعيان كما في التعريفات المذكورة لم يعده من الحكمة لأن موضوعه المعقولات الثانية التي هي من الموجودات الذهنية »(١) . ووجه الاختلاف بين كلام التهانوي وكلام الخوارزمي أن التهانوي لا يذكر الرأي القائل بأن المنطق مقدمة للفلسفة وجزء منها في الوقت عينه . ويورد صاحب كتاب جامع العلم الملقب بدستور العلماء(٢) كلام التهانوي بنصه . ثم يردده صاحب كشف الظنون ، ولكن في أسلوب مختلف قليلاً . ويعرض أسعد بن علي بن عثمان البانيوي في رسالة له عن المنطق الآراء الثلاثة عرضاً مفصلاً (٣) . فإذا كان يمكننا أن نستنتج من هذا أن مؤرخي العلم في العالم الإسلامي صوروا المشكلة تصويراً دقيقاً وعرضوا الآراء الثلاثة ، فإنه يتحتم علينا الآن أن نذكر أثر تلك الآراء في مدرسة الشراح الإسلاميين . أما عن الشراح المشائين - أمثال الفارابي ( ٣٥٣هـ ـ ٩٥٠م ) وابن سينا ( ٤٢٨هـ ـ ١٠٨٧م ) والساوي وغيرهم فلا نستطيع - وبخاصة عند الأولين - أن نحدد فكرة ثابتة لهم عن طبيعة المنطق . فبينما يعتبر الفارابي المنطق جزءاً من الفلسفة في كتاب الجمع بين رأي الحكيمين فيقول « إن موضوعات العلوم وموادها لا تخلو من أن تكون إما إلاهية وإما طبيعية وإما منطقية وإما رياضية أو سياسية »(٤) . ويردد الفارابي أيضاً القول بأن المنطق جزء من الفلسفة في كتابه « تحصيل السعادة » (٥) . ولكن الفارابي يعود في كتاب آخر إلى القول بأن المنطق آلة للفلسفة « وأقول لما كانت الفلسفة إنما تحصل بجودة التمييز وكانت جودة التمييز إنما تحصل بقوة الذهن عن إدراك الصواب ، كانت قوة الذهن حاصلة لنا قبل
The Stoics considered logic a part of wisdom. For them, wisdom is divided into natural science, dialectic, and ethics, and dialectic is logic. The Alexandrian commentators did not stand before these two different tendencies with bewilderment and doubt, but rather quickly reconciled them, agreeing with their harmonizing method. So they considered logic a preface to philosophy and a part of it at the same time. Al-Khwarizmi (d. 387 AH / 977 CE) depicts this dispute in his book Mafatih al-'Ulum: 'Philosophy is derived from a Greek word, “philosophia,” meaning love of wisdom. When Arabized, it is said “faylasuf,” then philosophy is derived from it. The meaning of philosophy is the knowledge of the realities of things and acting upon what is most proper. It is divided into two parts: the theoretical part and the practical part. Some made logic a third part besides these two; some made it a part of theoretical science; some made it a tool for philosophy; and some made it a part of it and a tool for it.' Al-Tahanawi mentions this dispute in a form close to al-Khwarizmi, saying: 'Know that they differed on whether logic is a science or not. Whoever said it is not a science does not consider it wisdom, since wisdom is science. Whoever said it is a science differed on whether it is from wisdom. Those who said it is from wisdom can differ on whether it is entirely from theoretical wisdom or not. Rather, some of it is from theoretical and some from practical, since mental existence may be within our power and choice or not. Those who said it is from theoretical wisdom can differ on whether it is from its three divisions or another division. Whoever includes the restriction of external objects in its definition, as in the mentioned definitions, does not consider it from wisdom because its subject is the secondary intelligibles which are among mental existents.' The difference between al-Tahanawi's statement and al-Khwarizmi's is that al-Tahanawi does not mention the view that logic is a preface to philosophy and a part of it at the same time. The author of the book Jami' al-'Ilm, known as Dastur al-'Ulama', quotes al-Tahanawi's text verbatim. Then the author of Kashf al-Zunun repeats it, but in a slightly different style. As'ad bin Ali bin Uthman al-Baniyawi, in a treatise on logic, presents the three views in detail. If we can conclude from this that historians of science in the Islamic world accurately depicted the problem and presented the three views, it is now necessary for us to mention the impact of those views on the school of Islamic commentators. As for the Peripatetic commentators – such as al-Farabi (d. 353 AH / 950 CE), Ibn Sina (d. 428 AH / 1087 CE), al-Sawi, and others – we cannot, especially with the first two, determine a fixed idea for them about the nature of logic. While al-Farabi considers logic a part of philosophy in the book al-Jam' bayn Ra'y al-Hakimayn, saying: 'The subjects and materials of the sciences cannot but be either divine, natural, logical, mathematical, or political.' Al-Farabi also repeats the statement that logic is part of philosophy in his book Tahsil al-Sa'adah. But al-Farabi returns in another book to say that logic is a tool for philosophy: 'And I say: since philosophy is only attained by good discernment, and good discernment is only attained by the strength of the mind in perceiving what is correct, the strength of the mind is present in us before...'
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a historical survey of the debate among Islamic philosophers and historians of science regarding the nature of logic: is it a part of philosophy or merely a tool (instrument) for it? The author begins by noting the Stoic view that logic (dialectic) is a part of wisdom, alongside natural science and ethics. Then the Alexandrian commentators are presented as reconciling two opposing tendencies: they considered logic both a preface (tool) to philosophy and a part of it simultaneously. Next, al-Khwarizmi is quoted from his 'Mafatih al-'Ulum', where he lists the various opinions: some make logic a third part, some a part of theoretical science, some a tool, and some both part and tool. Al-Tahanawi is then cited, offering a more detailed analysis focusing on whether logic is a science, and if so, whether it belongs to wisdom, and specifically to theoretical or practical wisdom. He notes that those who define wisdom with a restriction to external objects exclude logic because it deals with secondary intelligibles (mental existents). The author highlights a difference: al-Tahanawi omits the view that logic is both a preface and a part. The passage then notes that later authors like the writer of 'Jami' al-'Ilm' (Dastur al-'Ulama') and the author of 'Kashf al-Zunun' reproduce al-Tahanawi, and al-Baniyawi details the three views. The author concludes that Islamic historians accurately depicted the problem and that we must now consider the impact of these views on the commentators. Finally, the passage focuses on the Peripatetics, especially al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, noting that their stance is not fixed: al-Farabi sometimes says logic is part of philosophy (e.g., in 'al-Jam' bayn Ra'y al-Hakimayn' and 'Tahsil al-Sa'adah'), and sometimes says it is a tool (as in another book quoted). The passage thus presents a rich historical narrative of the evolving discourse on the status of logic.

Key Terms
المنطق
الحكمة
الجدل
المعقولات الثانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Tahanawi, why do some scholars exclude logic from wisdom (al-hikma)?
A) Because logic is not a science.
B) Because wisdom is limited to external objects, and logic deals with mental existents (secondary intelligibles).
C) Because logic is only a tool, not a science.
D) Because logic is a part of theoretical wisdom only.

Key Takeaway
The status of logic as part or tool of philosophy was a contested issue in Islamic thought, with implications for how logic is defined and valued.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
List the three main views on logic's relationship to philosophy as presented by al-Khwarizmi and al-Tahanawi. For each view, state one implication for how logic would be taught in an Islamic curriculum.
?
Al-Tahanawi mentions that those who define wisdom with the restriction to external objects exclude logic. Explain, in your own words, why this exclusion occurs based on the concept of secondary intelligibles.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
8 min
The Alexandrian Reconciliation: Logic as Both Tool and Part
Author describes the Stoic view and the Alexandrian reconciliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Alexandrian commentators reconciled the two opposing views on logic's relationship to philosophy and how this compromise is reflected in later Islamic historiography.

Core Idea
The Alexandrian compromise of logic as both tool and part became a key reference point for Islamic scholars.

The passage begins with the Stoic view that logic is a part of wisdom, then describes the Alexandrian commentators' reconciliation: they considered logic both a preface (tool) and a part of philosophy. This compromise influenced later Islamic historians, who recorded three distinct views. You will learn to trace this development.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Stoic view
The Stoics divided wisdom into natural science, dialectic (logic), and ethics. Dialectic is explicitly identified with logic.
This makes logic a part of wisdom by definition.
2
Understand the Alexandrian reaction
The Alexandrian commentators, known for harmonizing, did not simply choose between the two tendencies (logic as part vs tool). Instead, they considered logic both a preface (tool) and a part of philosophy simultaneously.
This is presented as an act of reconciliation (tawfiq).
3
Connect to Islamic historiography
The passage then notes that Islamic historians like al-Khwarizmi and al-Tahanawi recorded three views: part, tool, and both. The 'both' view echoes the Alexandrian compromise.
Al-Tahanawi's omission of the 'both' view suggests a later shift.
4
Assess the impact
The Alexandrian compromise allowed logic to be taught both as an introductory tool (like a manual) and as a substantive part of philosophical inquiry. This dual status persisted in Islamic education.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرواقية فقد اعتبرت المنطق جزءاً من الحكمة . فالحكمة عندها تنقسم إلى العلم الطبيعي والجدل والأخلاق ، والجدل هو المنطق . لم يقف الشراح الإسكندريون أمام هذين الاتجاهين المختلفين موقف الحيرة والشك ، بل سرعان ما قاموا بالتوفيق بينهما متطابقين في ذلك مع منهجهم التنسيقي . فاعتبروا المنطق مقدمة للفلسفة وجزءاً منها في الوقت عينه . ويصور الخوارزمي (١) ( ٣٨٧هـ - ٩٧٧م ) في كتابه مفاتيح العلوم هذا النزاع « الفلسفة مشتقة من كلمة يونانية وهي « فيلاسوفيا » ومعناها محبة الحكمة ، فلما عربت قيل فيلسوف ، ثم اشتقت الفلسفة منه ـ ومعنى الفلسفة علم حقائق الأشياء والعمل بما هو أصلح . وتنقسم قسمين أحدهما الجزء النظري والآخر الجزء العملي . ومنهم من جعل المنطق جزءاً ثالثاً غير هذين ، ومنهم من جعله جزءاً من أجزاء العلم النظري ، ومنهم من جعله آلة للفلسفة ومنهم من جعله جزءاً منها وآلة لها » (٢) . ويذكر هذا النزاع التهانوي في صورة تقرب من الخوارزمي فيقول « إعلم أنهم اختلفوا في أن المنطق من العلم أم لا : فمن قال إنه ليس بعلم فليس بحكمة عنده إذ الحكمة علم . ومن قال بأنه علم اختلفوا في أنه من الحكمة أم لا . والقائلون بأنه من الحكمة يمكن الاختلاف بينهم بأنه من الحكمة النظرية جميعاً أم لا . بل بعضه منها ـ وبعضه من العملية . إذ الموجود الذهني قد يكون بقدرتنا واختيارنا وقد لا يكون كذلك . والقائلون بأنه من الحكمة النظرية يمكن الاختلاف بينهم بأنه من أقسامها الثلاثة أم قسم آخر . فمن أخذ في تعريفها قيد الأعيان كما في التعريفات المذكورة لم يعده من الحكمة لأن موضوعه المعقولات الثانية التي هي من الموجودات الذهنية »(١) . ووجه الاختلاف بين كلام التهانوي وكلام الخوارزمي أن التهانوي لا يذكر الرأي القائل بأن المنطق مقدمة للفلسفة وجزء منها في الوقت عينه . ويورد صاحب كتاب جامع العلم الملقب بدستور العلماء(٢) كلام التهانوي بنصه . ثم يردده صاحب كشف الظنون ، ولكن في أسلوب مختلف قليلاً . ويعرض أسعد بن علي بن عثمان البانيوي في رسالة له عن المنطق الآراء الثلاثة عرضاً مفصلاً (٣) . فإذا كان يمكننا أن نستنتج من هذا أن مؤرخي العلم في العالم الإسلامي صوروا المشكلة تصويراً دقيقاً وعرضوا الآراء الثلاثة ، فإنه يتحتم علينا الآن أن نذكر أثر تلك الآراء في مدرسة الشراح الإسلاميين . أما عن الشراح المشائين - أمثال الفارابي ( ٣٥٣هـ ـ ٩٥٠م ) وابن سينا ( ٤٢٨هـ ـ ١٠٨٧م ) والساوي وغيرهم فلا نستطيع - وبخاصة عند الأولين - أن نحدد فكرة ثابتة لهم عن طبيعة المنطق . فبينما يعتبر الفارابي المنطق جزءاً من الفلسفة في كتاب الجمع بين رأي الحكيمين فيقول « إن موضوعات العلوم وموادها لا تخلو من أن تكون إما إلاهية وإما طبيعية وإما منطقية وإما رياضية أو سياسية »(٤) . ويردد الفارابي أيضاً القول بأن المنطق جزء من الفلسفة في كتابه « تحصيل السعادة » (٥) . ولكن الفارابي يعود في كتاب آخر إلى القول بأن المنطق آلة للفلسفة « وأقول لما كانت الفلسفة إنما تحصل بجودة التمييز وكانت جودة التمييز إنما تحصل بقوة الذهن عن إدراك الصواب ، كانت قوة الذهن حاصلة لنا قبل
The Stoics considered logic a part of wisdom. For them, wisdom is divided into natural science, dialectic, and ethics, and dialectic is logic. The Alexandrian commentators did not stand before these two different tendencies with bewilderment and doubt, but rather quickly reconciled them, agreeing with their harmonizing method. So they considered logic a preface to philosophy and a part of it at the same time. Al-Khwarizmi (d. 387 AH / 977 CE) depicts this dispute in his book Mafatih al-'Ulum: 'Philosophy is derived from a Greek word, “philosophia,” meaning love of wisdom. When Arabized, it is said “faylasuf,” then philosophy is derived from it. The meaning of philosophy is the knowledge of the realities of things and acting upon what is most proper. It is divided into two parts: the theoretical part and the practical part. Some made logic a third part besides these two; some made it a part of theoretical science; some made it a tool for philosophy; and some made it a part of it and a tool for it.' Al-Tahanawi mentions this dispute in a form close to al-Khwarizmi, saying: 'Know that they differed on whether logic is a science or not. Whoever said it is not a science does not consider it wisdom, since wisdom is science. Whoever said it is a science differed on whether it is from wisdom. Those who said it is from wisdom can differ on whether it is entirely from theoretical wisdom or not. Rather, some of it is from theoretical and some from practical, since mental existence may be within our power and choice or not. Those who said it is from theoretical wisdom can differ on whether it is from its three divisions or another division. Whoever includes the restriction of external objects in its definition, as in the mentioned definitions, does not consider it from wisdom because its subject is the secondary intelligibles which are among mental existents.' The difference between al-Tahanawi's statement and al-Khwarizmi's is that al-Tahanawi does not mention the view that logic is a preface to philosophy and a part of it at the same time. The author of the book Jami' al-'Ilm, known as Dastur al-'Ulama', quotes al-Tahanawi's text verbatim. Then the author of Kashf al-Zunun repeats it, but in a slightly different style. As'ad bin Ali bin Uthman al-Baniyawi, in a treatise on logic, presents the three views in detail. If we can conclude from this that historians of science in the Islamic world accurately depicted the problem and presented the three views, it is now necessary for us to mention the impact of those views on the school of Islamic commentators. As for the Peripatetic commentators – such as al-Farabi (d. 353 AH / 950 CE), Ibn Sina (d. 428 AH / 1087 CE), al-Sawi, and others – we cannot, especially with the first two, determine a fixed idea for them about the nature of logic. While al-Farabi considers logic a part of philosophy in the book al-Jam' bayn Ra'y al-Hakimayn, saying: 'The subjects and materials of the sciences cannot but be either divine, natural, logical, mathematical, or political.' Al-Farabi also repeats the statement that logic is part of philosophy in his book Tahsil al-Sa'adah. But al-Farabi returns in another book to say that logic is a tool for philosophy: 'And I say: since philosophy is only attained by good discernment, and good discernment is only attained by the strength of the mind in perceiving what is correct, the strength of the mind is present in us before...'
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a historical survey of the debate among Islamic philosophers and historians of science regarding the nature of logic: is it a part of philosophy or merely a tool (instrument) for it? The author begins by noting the Stoic view that logic (dialectic) is a part of wisdom, alongside natural science and ethics. Then the Alexandrian commentators are presented as reconciling two opposing tendencies: they considered logic both a preface (tool) to philosophy and a part of it simultaneously. Next, al-Khwarizmi is quoted from his 'Mafatih al-'Ulum', where he lists the various opinions: some make logic a third part, some a part of theoretical science, some a tool, and some both part and tool. Al-Tahanawi is then cited, offering a more detailed analysis focusing on whether logic is a science, and if so, whether it belongs to wisdom, and specifically to theoretical or practical wisdom. He notes that those who define wisdom with a restriction to external objects exclude logic because it deals with secondary intelligibles (mental existents). The author highlights a difference: al-Tahanawi omits the view that logic is both a preface and a part. The passage then notes that later authors like the writer of 'Jami' al-'Ilm' (Dastur al-'Ulama') and the author of 'Kashf al-Zunun' reproduce al-Tahanawi, and al-Baniyawi details the three views. The author concludes that Islamic historians accurately depicted the problem and that we must now consider the impact of these views on the commentators. Finally, the passage focuses on the Peripatetics, especially al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, noting that their stance is not fixed: al-Farabi sometimes says logic is part of philosophy (e.g., in 'al-Jam' bayn Ra'y al-Hakimayn' and 'Tahsil al-Sa'adah'), and sometimes says it is a tool (as in another book quoted). The passage thus presents a rich historical narrative of the evolving discourse on the status of logic.

The Two Pillars of Logic: Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author links the two divisions of logic to their key tools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to map the division of logic into tasawwur and tasdiq and identify the primary means to each.

Core Idea
Definition yields concepts; syllogism yields assent.

This passage opens Chapter Two by reiterating the fundamental division of logic into tasawwur (conceptualization) and tasdiq (assent). The author immediately links each to its primary tool: definition (hadd) for tasawwur and syllogism (qiyas) for tasdiq. Understanding this division is essential before diving into the chapter's structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main parts of logic
Read the first line: 'The logic of the commentators is divided into two parts: tasawwur and tasdiq.' Note that this is the overarching framework.
2
Link each part to its tool
The text states: 'Through definition we attain tasawwur, and through syllogism we attain tasdiq.' This establishes that hadd and qiyas are the primary methods.
Remember: hadd is for concepts, qiyas is for judgments.
3
Recognize the chapter's focus
The chapter will investigate tasawwurat. The author emphasizes that hadd is the most important part of this investigation.
The rest of the chapter elaborates how definitions are constructed from words and meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفَصْلُ الثَّاني
مبحث التصورات
منطق الشراح ينقسم ـ كما قلنا ـ إلى قسمين : التصور والتصديق .
وبواسطة التعريف نتوصل إلى التصور ، وبواسطة القياس نتوصل إلى التصديق ، فأهم مباحث التصورات إذن هو الحد ، كما أن أهم مباحث التصديقات هو القياس . وسنبحث في هذا الفصل مبحث التصورات ـ ومبحث التصورات يشمل أقساماً ثلاثة : أولاً ـ البحث في الألفاظ من حيث صلتها بالمعاني ـ وثانياً ويلي هذا البحث في المعنى ذاته . فاذا ما تكون لدينا هذان القسمان ـ البحث في اللفظ والبحث في المعنى ـ تكامل لنا عنصرا الحد . فنصل بذلك إلى القسم الثالث الجوهري من التصورات . ونحن في بحثنا لهذه الأقسام سنحاول على الخصوص توضيح المسائل الآتية .
أولاً ـ العناصر التي أضافها الشراح الإسلاميون . فقد أضاف الإسلاميون بعض الأبحاث وخاصة اللغوية منها ـ والتي لا نجد لها شبيهاً لا في منطق أرسطو ولا في منطق الشراح اليونانيين .
ثانياً ـ سنعرض العناصر الرواقية المنطقية التي دخلت في منطق الشراح الإسلاميين والتي تكون هي والعناصر الخاصة والأرسططاليسية منطقهم .
ثالثاً ـ سنحاول أن نبين أثر بعض مباحث التصورات في مباحث اللغة والأصول ، ولا تظهر هذه المسائل بوضوح بقدر ما تظهر في أول أقسام
Chapter Two: The Investigation of Tasawwurat. The logic of the commentators is divided – as we said – into two parts: tasawwur and tasdiq. And through definition we attain tasawwur, and through syllogism we attain tasdiq. Thus the most important investigation of tasawwurat is the definition (hadd), just as the most important investigation of tasdiqat is the syllogism (qiyas). And we will investigate in this chapter the investigation of tasawwurat – and the investigation of tasawwurat includes three sections: First – the investigation of words in relation to their meanings; Second – following this, the investigation of meaning itself. So when we have these two sections – the investigation of words and the investigation of meaning – the two elements of definition are completed for us. Thus we arrive at the third essential section of tasawwurat. And in our investigation of these sections, we will especially attempt to clarify the following issues: First – the elements that the Muslim commentators added. For the Muslims added some investigations, especially linguistic ones, which we find no counterpart to either in Aristotle's logic or in the logic of the Greek commentators. Second – we will present the logical Stoic elements that entered into the logic of the Muslim commentators and which, together with the specific elements and the Aristotelian ones, constitute their logic. Third – we will try to show the influence of some investigations of tasawwurat on the investigations of language and usul (principles of jurisprudence), and these issues appear nowhere as clearly as they do in the first sections...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is the opening of Chapter Two, serving as a methodological introduction. The author first reminds that the logic of the commentators is divided into two parts: tasawwur and tasdiq. He then explains the means to each: definition (hadd) leads to tasawwur, and syllogism (qiyas) leads to tasdiq. Hence the core of tasawwurat is definition, and the core of tasdiqat is syllogism. The chapter will focus on tasawwurat, which the author subdivides into three sections: (1) the relationship of words to meanings, (2) the investigation of meaning itself, and (3) the essential element of definition that emerges from combining the first two. The author then lists three specific aims for his discussion: first, to highlight the novel contributions of Muslim commentators, especially linguistic investigations absent in Greek logic; second, to identify the Stoic logical elements that merged with Aristotelian and original Muslim elements; and third, to demonstrate the impact of tasawwurat studies on linguistic and usul al-fiqh investigations, particularly in the first sections of the chapter.

Key Terms

قياس

B) Tasawwur by hadd, tasdiq by qiyas
C) Both by hadd
D) Both by qiyas

Key Takeaway
Logic is fundamentally divided into tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent), each with its own primary tool: definition for concepts, syllogism for assent.

The Three Sections and Three Aims of Tasawwurat
Author lists three sections and three aims for the chapter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the three sections of the tasawwurat chapter and the three specific aims the author sets out.

Core Idea
Tasawwurat proceeds from words to meanings to the definition itself.

In the same opening passage, the author outlines the chapter's structure into three sections: (1) words in relation to meanings, (2) meaning itself, and (3) the resulting definition. He also announces three historical and methodological aims: highlighting Muslim contributions, identifying Stoic elements, and showing relevance to usul al-fiqh. This lesson helps you navigate the chapter's roadmap.

Guided Path
1
List the three sections of the chapter
Find the phrases: 'First – the investigation of words in relation to their meanings; Second – following this, the investigation of meaning itself; Third – the essential element of definition.' Write them down in order.
Notice that the third section depends on the first two.
2
Link the sections to the three aims
After the sections, the author lists three aims: (1) to show what Muslim commentators added, (2) to identify Stoic elements, (3) to show influence on language and usul. These aims are not tied to specific sections but guide the entire discussion.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the aims continue onto the next page.
3
Reflect on the historical layers
The author distinguishes between Aristotelian, Stoic, and uniquely Muslim contributions. This reflects the composite nature of Islamic logic.
This shows that Islamic logicians did not merely repeat Aristotle but created a synthesis.

The Two Pillars of Mantiq: Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author divides logic into tasawwur and tasdiq linked to definition and syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur and tasdiq and identify their respective methods in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Logic splits into two domains: concepts (tasawwur) and judgments (tasdiq), each with its own route to knowledge.

This short passage lays the groundwork for the entire study of mantiq. It announces that the subject matter of logic is not monolithic but bifurcated into conceptualization and assent, and that each domain has a characteristic procedure. Understanding this division is like having a map before traversing the territory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of logic
The passage states that logic divides into two sections: tasawwur (conceptualization) and tasdiq (assent). Recognize these as the two fundamental categories of knowledge.
2
Note the method for each part
The author says: 'By means of definition we arrive at tasawwur, and by means of syllogism we arrive at tasdiq.' So definition (hadd) is the tool for concepts, and syllogism (qiyas) is the tool for judgments.
Definition here means a precise description of essence; syllogism means a logical argument.
3
Identify the most important topic in each
The passage asserts that 'the most important topic of tasawwurat is definition, just as the most important topic of tasdiqat is syllogism.' This tells you what the chapter will focus on.
4
Understand the logical progression
Notice the sequence: first you need clear concepts (via definition), then you can form judgments (via syllogism). This mirrors the natural order of reasoning.
In Islamic sciences, defining terms precisely prevents ambiguity in arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفَصْلُ الثَّاني مبحث التصورات منطق الشراح ينقسم ـ كما قلنا ـ إلى قسمين : التصور والتصديق . وبواسطة التعريف نتوصل إلى التصور ، وبواسطة القياس نتوصل إلى التصديق ، فأهم مباحث التصورات إذن هو الحد ، كما أن أهم مباحث التصديقات هو القياس .
The second chapter: The Topic of Perceptions. The logic of the commentators divides, as we said, into two sections: tasawwur and tasdiq. By means of definition we arrive at tasawwur, and by means of syllogism we arrive at tasdiq. So the most important topic of tasawwurat is definition, just as the most important topic of tasdiqat is syllogism.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a methodological introduction to the second chapter on perceptions. The author first reminds the reader that logic is divided into two parts: conceptualization (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Then he connects each part to its primary method: definition (ta'rif) is the means to achieve conceptualization, while syllogism (qiyas) is the means to achieve assent. Consequently, the most critical topic in the study of concepts is definition (hadd), just as the most critical topic in the study of judgments is syllogism. This sets the stage for the chapter's focus on definition and the elements that constitute it.

Key Terms
التصور (tasawwur): Conceptualization; the apprehension of a meaning without judgment of truth or falsehood. [Note: In Ibn Taymiyya's critique, the division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq is challenged, but here it is standard.]
التصديق (tasdiq): Assent; the judgment that something is true or false, forming a proposition. [Note: No significant shift in mainstream mantiq.]
الحد (hadd): Definition; a statement that gives the essence of a thing, composed of genus and differentia. [Note: In later texts, hadd is often contrasted with rasm (description).]
القياس (qiyas): Syllogism; a composed argument that yields certain knowledge if valid. [Note: In some usul works, qiyas refers to analogical reasoning; here it is syllogistic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Qiyas (syllogism)
B) Hadd (definition)
C) Tasdiq (assent)
D) Al-Ilahiyyat (theology)

Key Takeaway
Logic is divided into conceptualization and assent, with definition and syllogism as their respective primary tools.

The Three Sections of Tasawwurat
Author outlines the three sections of tasawwurat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three sections of tasawwurat and explain how the first two sections build toward the third.

Core Idea
Definition (hadd) rests on the prior study of words and meanings.

In this opening plan, the author lays out the structure of conceptual logic (tasawwurat). He tells us that before we can define anything, we must first study words and their relation to meanings, then meanings themselves. These two foundations lead to the essential third section: the definition. This lesson walks you through that structural logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sections mentioned
Look at the Arabic: the author lists first the study of words, then meanings, then definition. What are their Arabic names?
The sections are: مباحث الألفاظ, مبحث المعنى, and القسم الثالث الجوهري من التصورات (which is the definition).
2
Understand the logical order
Why must words and meanings come before definition? Because definition requires understanding terms and their concepts.
3
Note the author's claim about completeness
The author says: 'تكامل لنا عنصرا الحد' — once we have the first two sections, the elements of definition become complete. So definition is not separate but built from them.
4
Connect to the broader course
This is a plan for the chapter. Keep this structure in mind as you study each section.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسنبحث في هذا الفصل مبحث التصورات ـ ومبحث التصورات يشمل أقساماً ثلاثة : أولاً ـ البحث في الألفاظ من حيث صلتها بالمعاني ـ وثانياً ويلي هذا البحث في المعنى ذاته . فاذا ما تكون لدينا هذان القسمان ـ البحث في اللفظ والبحث في المعنى ـ تكامل لنا عنصرا الحد . فنصل بذلك إلى القسم الثالث الجوهري من التصورات . ونحن في بحثنا لهذه الأقسام سنحاول على الخصوص توضيح المسائل الآتية . أولاً ـ العناصر التي أضافها الشراح الإسلاميون . فقد أضاف الإسلاميون بعض الأبحاث وخاصة اللغوية منها ـ والتي لا نجد لها شبيهاً لا في منطق أرسطو ولا في منطق الشراح اليونانيين . ثانياً ـ سنعرض العناصر الرواقية المنطقية التي دخلت في منطق الشراح الإسلاميين والتي تكون هي والعناصر الخاصة والأرسططاليسية منطقهم . ثالثاً ـ سنحاول أن نبين أثر بعض مباحث التصورات في مباحث اللغة والأصول ، ولا تظهر هذه المسائل بوضوح بقدر ما تظهر في أول أقسام التصورات وهي مباحث الألفاظ .
We will discuss in this chapter the topic of tasawwurat. The topic of tasawwurat includes three sections: First — the study of words in terms of their relation to meanings. Second — following this, the study of meaning itself. So once we have these two sections — the study of the word and the study of meaning — the elements of definition become complete for us. Thus we reach the third essential section of tasawwurat. In our study of these sections, we will especially attempt to clarify the following issues: First — the elements that the Islamic commentators added. For the Islamic commentators added some studies, especially linguistic ones, which have no parallel in Aristotle's logic or in the logic of the Greek commentators. Second — we will present the Stoic logical elements that entered the logic of the Islamic commentators and which, together with the specific and Aristotelian elements, constitute their logic. Third — we will attempt to show the influence of some topics of tasawwurat on topics of language and usul (principles of fiqh). These issues do not become clear as much as they appear in the first section of tasawwurat, which is the study of words.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a plan for the chapter on tasawwurat. The author first lists three sections: (1) words in relation to meanings, (2) meaning itself, and then (3) the essential section of definition, which is built from the first two. After this structural outline, he then lists three additional issues he will highlight: (a) the unique linguistic contributions of Islamic commentators not found in Aristotle or Greek commentators, (b) the Stoic logical elements that entered Islamic logic, and (c) the impact of these topics on the fields of Arabic language and usul al-fiqh. The author notes that this influence is most visible in the first section on words.

الألفاظ
المعاني
العناصر
الحد

Key Takeaway
The study of tasawwurat proceeds from words to meanings to definition, a logical progression that underscores the dependence of definition on linguistic and conceptual analysis.

Three Issues in Islamic Logic
Author identifies three issues to highlight regarding tasawwurat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three key issues that the author highlights regarding the development of Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Islamic logic is a synthesis of Aristotelian, Stoic, and original linguistic contributions.

After outlining the three sections of tasawwurat, the author turns to three historiographical issues. He wants to clarify what Islamic logicians added beyond Aristotle and the Greek commentators, what Stoic elements entered their system, and how these logical topics influenced other Islamic sciences like language and usul al-fiqh. This lesson unpacks those three issues.

Guided Path
1

2

The author notes that Stoic logical elements entered Islamic logic. Which specific elements? (author doesn't list, but you can infer from other knowledge: conditionals, disjunctions, etc.)
3
Identify the third issue: influence on language and usul
The author says the influence is most visible in the first section (words). How might logical studies of words affect usul al-fiqh? (e.g., discussions of 'amm, khass, mutlaq, muqayyad)
4
Synthesize
Understand that Islamic logic is not a mere copy of Aristotle; it has its own character from these three sources.

The Unique Composition of Islamic Logic
Author describes the blend of elements in Islamic logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three components that constitute Islamic logic according to the author.

Core Idea
Islamic logic is a synthesis of Aristotelian, Stoic, and original Islamic contributions.

Many assume Islamic logic is simply a translation of Aristotle. However, the author of Manahij al-Bahth explains that it is a unique blend. Understanding this blend is crucial for reading later commentaries and recognizing the diverse intellectual heritage that shaped Islamic logical thought.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about composition
Read the first sentence: 'بل أضافوا أبحاثاً خاصة بهم وأبحاثاً أخرى رواقية، ومزجوا هذين العنصرين بالعنصر الارسططاليسي'. Notice that the Islamic commentators added their own researches and Stoic researches to the Aristotelian element.
The author explicitly states this mixture is what constitutes Islamic logic.
2
Distinguish the three components
List them: 1) Aristotelian element, 2) Stoic elements, 3) Original researches specific to Islamic commentators. The author says 'هذا المزيج دائماً منطق الشراح الإسلاميين' – this mixture is always the logic of the Islamic commentators.
3
Explain why this matters for studying Islamic logic
Islamic logic is not a monolithic copy of Aristotle. It evolved through critical engagement. For example, the theory of conditional syllogisms was enriched by Stoic influences. Recognizing this helps in analyzing arguments in later works like those of al-Razi or al-Taftazani.
This foreshadows non-Aristotelian elements mentioned later in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل أضافوا أبحاثاً خاصة بهم وأبحاثاً أخرى رواقية ، ومزجوا هذين العنصرين بالعنصر الارسططاليسي ، ويكون هذا المزيج دائماً منطق الشراح الإسلاميين . ويتصل بهذا البحث السابق ، مبحث المعاني ، لأن اللفظ كما قلنا لا يشتغل به المنطقي إلا من حيث صلته بالمعنى . فالمعنى من حيث هو ثابت في نفسه هو الغاية من البحث في اللفظ ، مع العلم بأن المعنى تدل عليه الألفاظ ، إذ من البديهي أننا لا نستطيع تعريف المعنى بدون اللفظ . وقد خلا هذا البحث عند الشراح الإسلاميين من الأبحاث اللغوية ، لأن طبيعته نفسها تستلزم هذا الخلو ، إذ هو بحث في المعنى ، في ذاته ودخلت في هذا البحث ، كما هو مقرر ، عناصر غير أرسططاليسية ، وسنحدد مكان تلك العناصر في ثنايا مناقشتنا لهذه المباحث . نسبة المعاني إلى مداركنا : وأول ما نلقاه من تلك المباحث هو نسبة المعاني إلى مداركنا ، أو بمعنى أدق كيفية الحصول على تلك المعاني . إننا نتوصل إلى تلك المعاني عن طريقين : طريق الاحساس وطريق الاستدلال العقلي(١) ، وليس هذا المبحث في الواقع منطقياً في ذاته ، بل هو يتصل بنظرية المعرفة ويعالجه الإسلاميون علاجاً ميتافيزيقياً بحثاً يتصل بأفلاطون من ناحية ، وبالأفلاطونية المحدثة من ناحية أخرى . وليس يهمنا هنا عرض تلك المباحث الميتافيزيقية وبحثها ، ولكن أحب أن أشير إلى أنه يتصل بتقسيم الموجودات إلى محسوسة ومعقولة ، تقسيم آخر يشترك فيه أيضاً المنطق والميتافيزيقا وهو تقسيم الموجودات إلى شخصية معينة ، أي الموجودات الجزئية ، وإلى أمور عامة ، أي الكليات .
Rather, they added researches specific to them and other Stoic researches, and they mixed these two elements with the Aristotelian element, and this mixture always constitutes the logic of the Islamic commentators. It connects to the previous discussion, the topic of meanings, because, as we said, the logician only concerns himself with the utterance in terms of its connection to meaning. Meaning, in itself, is the goal of the investigation of utterance, knowing that meanings are indicated by utterances, since it is obvious that we cannot define meaning without utterance. This investigation among the Islamic commentators was free from linguistic investigations, because its nature necessitates this absence, since it is an investigation of meaning in itself. Non-Aristotelian elements entered into this investigation, as established, and we will determine the place of those elements in the course of our discussion of these topics. The relation of meanings to our faculties: The first thing we encounter of these topics is the relation of meanings to our faculties, or more precisely, how to obtain those meanings. We arrive at those meanings through two ways: the way of sensation and the way of intellectual reasoning (1). This topic is not actually logical in itself, but rather it connects to epistemology, and the Islamic scholars treat it as a purely metaphysical treatment connected to Plato on one hand, and to Neoplatonism on the other. It is not important here to present and discuss those metaphysical topics, but I would like to point out that it connects to the division of existents into sensible and intelligible, another division in which logic and metaphysics also share, which is the division of existents into particular individuals, i.e., particular existents, and into general matters, i.e., universals.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage begins by characterizing Islamic logic as a synthesis: the Islamic commentators added their own original researches and Stoic elements to the Aristotelian foundation. This mixture is what constitutes 'the logic of the Islamic commentators.' The author then transitions to the topic of meaning (ma'na). The logician studies utterances only as they relate to meaning; meaning itself is the ultimate goal. While utterances are necessary to express meaning, the investigation of meaning per se is free from linguistic concerns. The author notes that this investigation includes non-Aristotelian elements, which will be identified later. Next, the author addresses the epistemological question of how meanings are obtained. There are two ways: sensation and intellectual reasoning. This topic is actually metaphysical rather than purely logical, but it leads to the division of existents into sensible/intelligible and particular/universal categories. The author thus sets the stage for the detailed classification of meanings that follows.

Key Terms
المنطق الشراح الإسلاميين
العنصر الارسططاليسي
العنصر الرواقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Islamic logic is a unique synthesis of Aristotelian, Stoic, and original elements, not a mere copy of Aristotle.

The Two Sources of Meaning in Islamic Logic
Author introduces the investigation of meaning and its two sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two ways meanings are obtained according to Islamic logicians and relate them to the division of existents.

Core Idea
Meanings are obtained through sensation and intellectual reasoning, leading to a division of existents into sensible/intelligible and particular/universal.

The investigation of meaning is central to Islamic logic. But how do we acquire meanings? The author explains that there are two pathways: sensation and intellectual reasoning. This epistemological question connects logic to metaphysics, and it prepares the ground for the classification of terms and concepts that follow.

Guided Path
1
Understand why meaning is central to logic
The author says: 'اللفظ لا يشتغل به المنطقي إلا من حيث صلته بالمعنى' – the logician only deals with utterance insofar as it relates to meaning. Meaning is the goal. So the study of how meanings are obtained is foundational.
2
Identify the two ways of obtaining meanings
The text explicitly states: 'إننا نتوصل إلى تلك المعاني عن طريقين: طريق الاحساس وطريق الاستدلال العقلي' – we arrive at meanings through the way of sensation and the way of intellectual reasoning.
These correspond to empirical and rational knowledge.
3
Connect to the division of existents
The author links these two ways to the division of existents into sensible (محسوسة) and intelligible (معقولة), and further to particular (جزئية) and general/universal (عامة/كليات). This shows how meaning acquisition relates to ontology.
This division is shared by logic and metaphysics.
4
Recognize the metaphysical treatment
The author notes that this topic is not purely logical but connects to epistemology and is treated metaphysically by Islamic scholars. This warns us that some discussions in logic textbooks may have deeper philosophical roots.

بل أضافوا أبحاثاً خاصة بهم وأبحاثاً أخرى رواقية ، ومزجوا هذين العنصرين بالعنصر الارسططاليسي ، ويكون هذا المزيج دائماً منطق الشراح الإسلاميين . ويتصل بهذا البحث السابق ، مبحث المعاني ، لأن اللفظ كما قلنا لا يشتغل به المنطقي إلا من حيث صلته بالمعنى . فالمعنى من حيث هو ثابت في نفسه هو الغاية من البحث في اللفظ ، مع العلم بأن المعنى تدل عليه الألفاظ ، إذ من البديهي أننا لا نستطيع تعريف المعنى بدون اللفظ . وقد خلا هذا البحث عند الشراح الإسلاميين من الأبحاث اللغوية ، لأن طبيعته نفسها تستلزم هذا الخلو ، إذ هو بحث في المعنى ، في ذاته ودخلت في هذا البحث ، كما هو مقرر ، عناصر غير أرسططاليسية ، وسنحدد مكان تلك العناصر في ثنايا مناقشتنا لهذه المباحث . نسبة المعاني إلى مداركنا : وأول ما نلقاه من تلك المباحث هو نسبة المعاني إلى مداركنا ، أو بمعنى أدق كيفية الحصول على تلك المعاني . إننا نتوصل إلى تلك المعاني عن طريقين : طريق الاحساس وطريق الاستدلال العقلي(١) ، وليس هذا المبحث في الواقع منطقياً في ذاته ، بل هو يتصل بنظرية المعرفة ويعالجه الإسلاميون علاجاً ميتافيزيقياً بحثاً يتصل بأفلاطون من ناحية ، وبالأفلاطونية المحدثة من ناحية أخرى . وليس يهمنا هنا عرض تلك المباحث الميتافيزيقية وبحثها ، ولكن أحب أن أشير إلى أنه يتصل بتقسيم الموجودات إلى محسوسة ومعقولة ، تقسيم آخر يشترك فيه أيضاً المنطق والميتافيزيقا وهو تقسيم الموجودات إلى شخصية معينة ، أي الموجودات الجزئية ، وإلى أمور عامة ، أي الكليات .
Rather, they added researches specific to them and other Stoic researches, and they mixed these two elements with the Aristotelian element, and this mixture always constitutes the logic of the Islamic commentators. It connects to the previous discussion, the topic of meanings, because, as we said, the logician only concerns himself with the utterance in terms of its connection to meaning. Meaning, in itself, is the goal of the investigation of utterance, knowing that meanings are indicated by utterances, since it is obvious that we cannot define meaning without utterance. This investigation among the Islamic commentators was free from linguistic investigations, because its nature necessitates this absence, since it is an investigation of meaning in itself. Non-Aristotelian elements entered into this investigation, as established, and we will determine the place of those elements in the course of our discussion of these topics. The relation of meanings to our faculties: The first thing we encounter of these topics is the relation of meanings to our faculties, or more precisely, how to obtain those meanings. We arrive at those meanings through two ways: the way of sensation and the way of intellectual reasoning (1). This topic is not actually logical in itself, but rather it connects to epistemology, and the Islamic scholars treat it as a purely metaphysical treatment connected to Plato on one hand, and to Neoplatonism on the other. It is not important here to present and discuss those metaphysical topics, but I would like to point out that it connects to the division of existents into sensible and intelligible, another division in which logic and metaphysics also share, which is the division of existents into particular individuals, i.e., particular existents, and into general matters, i.e., universals.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage begins by characterizing Islamic logic as a synthesis: the Islamic commentators added their own original researches and Stoic elements to the Aristotelian foundation. This mixture is what constitutes 'the logic of the Islamic commentators.' The author then transitions to the topic of meaning (ma'na). The logician studies utterances only as they relate to meaning; meaning itself is the ultimate goal. While utterances are necessary to express meaning, the investigation of meaning per se is free from linguistic concerns. The author notes that this investigation includes non-Aristotelian elements, which will be identified later. Next, the author addresses the epistemological question of how meanings are obtained. There are two ways: sensation and intellectual reasoning. This topic is actually metaphysical rather than purely logical, but it leads to the division of existents into sensible/intelligible and particular/universal categories. The author thus sets the stage for the detailed classification of meanings that follows.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

The Hellenistic Roots of Islamic Logic
Author outlines the two main Hellenistic logical traditions and their synthesis in Islamic thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main Hellenistic logical traditions absorbed by Islamic scholars and explain how the Peripatetic synthesis merged them.

Core Idea
Islamic logic is a synthesis of Aristotelian and Stoic traditions, not a pure Aristotelian import.

This passage introduces the historical background of logic in the Islamic world. It explains that Islamic scholars did not simply adopt Aristotelian logic wholesale; they also engaged with Stoic logic and created a synthetic Peripatetic tradition. Understanding this hybrid origin is essential for appreciating later Islamic critiques and developments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two traditions
From the passage, note the two schools: the Stoic (al-rawāqiyya) and the Aristotelian (al-arisṭāṭālīsiyya). They differ in their treatment of concepts (taṣawwurāt) and assents (taṣdīqāt).
The Stoic influence is often overlooked; the passage highlights its distinctiveness.
2
Recognize the Peripatetic synthesis
The Peripatetic (al-mashshā'ī) direction attempted to unify all Hellenistic logical heritage into a complete, coherent whole. It incorporated non-Aristotelian elements, distancing itself from pure Aristotelianism.
3
Characterize the heritage
The author states that this heritage is fundamentally Hellenistic, not Islamic in spirit, and is distant from Islamic civilization to varying degrees.
This sets up the tension that Muslim scholars had with Greek logic.
4
Connect to subsequent chapters
The passage previews that the stance of Muslim scholars towards Aristotelian logic will be examined in later chapters, making this historical background essential.
You will later see how figures like al-Ghazali and Ibn Taymiyya reacted to this Hellenistic legacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التفصيلية وأجزائه ففي مبحث التصورات وفي مبحث التصديقات تختلف الرواقية عن الأرسططاليسية ويختلف الشراح الإسلاميون الرواقيون عن الفكر الأرسططاليسي. أما الاتجاه الثاني، وهو الاتجاه المشائي فقد حاول أن يجمع بين التراث المنطقي الهيلني كله في وحدة كاملة منسقة، فابتعد الى حد ما عن الفكر الأرسططاليسي، بما أدخله على هذا الفكر من عناصر غير أرسططاليسية. هذا هو تاريخ الهلينية المنطقية في العالم الإسلامي ذكرنا البعض منه وأهملنا البعض كالبرهان وكالخطابة والشعر والجدل والسفسطة . . . ولكن أي تراث هو ؟ إنه في جوهره فكر هليني يبتعد عن روح الحضارة الإسلامية كل الإبتعاد على درجة من التفاوت ـ هذا الروح الذي يتجلى في موقف علماء الإسلام من المنطق الأرسططاليسي، وهو ما سنتابعه في الأبواب القادمة.
Detailed [logic] and its parts: in the topic of concepts and in the topic of assents, the Stoic [school] differs from the Aristotelian, and the Stoic Muslim commentators differ from Aristotelian thought. As for the second direction, which is the Peripatetic direction, it attempted to combine all of the Hellenistic logical heritage into a complete unified whole, thus distancing itself to some extent from Aristotelian thought, by introducing into this thought non-Aristotelian elements. This is the history of Hellenistic logic in the Islamic world; we have mentioned some of it and neglected some, such as demonstration, rhetoric, poetry, dialectic, and sophistry... But what heritage is it? In essence, it is Hellenistic thought, completely distant from the spirit of Islamic civilization, to a varying degree — this spirit that manifests in the stance of Muslim scholars towards Aristotelian logic, which we will follow in the coming chapters.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a historical overview of the Hellenistic logical traditions in the Islamic world. It begins by noting that in the study of concepts (tasawwur) and assents (tasdiq), the Stoic school differs from the Aristotelian, and that the Stoic commentators among Muslims differ from Aristotelian thought. Then it describes the Peripatetic (mashshai) direction, which sought to synthesize all Hellenistic logic into a coherent whole, incorporating non-Aristotelian elements and thus deviating from pure Aristotelianism. The author characterizes this heritage as fundamentally Hellenistic, distant from the Islamic spirit, and promises to explore the Muslim scholars' stance on Aristotelian logic in later chapters. The passage establishes that Islamic logic is not purely Aristotelian but includes Stoic influences, yet remains essentially Hellenistic.

Key Terms
رواقية (rawāqiyya): The Stoic school of logic, distinct from Aristotelian logic in topics of concepts and assents. [Note: In earlier Greek philosophy, it refers to the Stoic school; here it is used specifically in the context of logical methodology.]
أرسططاليسية (arisṭāṭālīsiyya): The Aristotelian logical tradition, considered pure Aristotelianism. [Note: No significant shift; standard reference to Aristotelian logic.]
مشائي (mashshā'ī): The Peripatetic direction that attempted to unify all Hellenistic logic, incorporating non-Aristotelian elements. [Note: In earlier philosophy, it refers to followers of Aristotle; here it denotes a synthetic approach.]
هيلني / هليني (hīlīnī / halīnī): Hellenistic thought, referring to the Greek-influenced intellectual heritage. [Note: No significant shift; standard term for Hellenistic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Preserve pure Aristotelianism

C) Reject Stoic logic entirely
D) Create an Islamic logic independent of Greek thought

Key Takeaway
The logical heritage in the Islamic world is a synthesis of Aristotelian and Stoic traditions, shaped by the Peripatetic effort to unify Hellenistic thought.

Introduction to the Stance of the Usuliyyun on Aristotelian Logic
Chapter heading announcing the topic of the second section.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the main subject and temporal scope of Chapter 2.

Core Idea
The usuliyyun's engagement with Aristotelian logic shaped Islamic legal theory.

This lesson introduces the second chapter of the book, which investigates how Islamic legal theorists (usuliyyun) interacted with Aristotelian logic up to the fifth century AH. Understanding this historical relationship is crucial for appreciating the development of Islamic jurisprudence and its methodological foundations. The chapter sets the stage for examining the varied responses of scholars to Greek logical traditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms: usuliyyun and Aristotelian logic
The usuliyyun are scholars of usul al-fiqh, concerned with legal theory. Aristotelian logic refers to the logical system derived from Aristotle, which was studied and integrated by Muslim philosophers and logicians.
2
Note the temporal boundary: 'until the fifth century AH'
This indicates the historical scope of the chapter, covering the early and formative period of Islamic legal theory.
3
Understand the purpose of this chapter: to survey the positions of usuliyyun
The chapter will explore whether these scholars accepted, rejected, or modified Aristotelian logic in their legal reasoning.

Chapter Roadmap: Usul Scholars vs Aristotelian Logic
Author outlines the plan for discussing the stance of usul scholars on Aristotelian logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to outline the chapter's structure and identify the key components of the usul scholars' own logic as presented by the author.

Core Idea
The author contrasts Hellenistic-influenced commentators with 'pure Islamic logic' of usul scholars, setting up a critical comparison.

This passage is a methodological introduction that sets the stage for the entire chapter. The author first summarizes the previous chapter's findings on Islamic commentators influenced by Hellenistic thought, then announces the shift to the usul scholars—both from usul al-fiqh and usul al-din (theologians). The author promises to present their distinctive logical system and later trace its eventual transition back to Aristotelian logic. This roadmap is essential for understanding the author's overarching argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's goal in this passage
The author is not arguing a point but laying out the chapter's plan. Look for verbs like 'سنبحث' (we will research) and 'نعرض' (we will present) that signal future content.
2
Differentiate the two groups discussed
The first group (previous chapter) are Islamic commentators (Peripatetics and Stoics) who were Hellenistic extensions. The second group (this chapter) are usul scholars (fiqh and kalam) who developed an independent Islamic logic.
3
List the two main components of Islamic logic according to the author
The author mentions 'al-ḥadd al-uṣūlī' (usuli definition) and 'al-istidlālāt al-islāmiyya' (Islamic inferences) as analogies to Aristotelian definition and syllogism.
4
Note the plan for critical discussion and transition
After presenting the Islamic logic, the author will discuss criticisms by mutakallimun against Aristotelian topics, and finally the transition from Islamic logic back to Aristotelian logic.
5
Recognise that this is a preface, not the argument itself
This passage does not contain any logical analysis; it is a structural overview. Do not mistake it for a substantive claim about logic.

بحثنا في الباب السابق موقف الشراح الإسلاميين مشائين ورواقيين من المنطق الأرسططاليسي، وتبين لنا أن هؤلاء الشراح الإسلاميين كانوا امتداداً للعقل الهليني في العالم الإسلامي، بحيث كانت الوشائج التي تربطهم بالتفكير المنطقي الإسلامي واهية تماماً. ولهذا: لفظهم المجتمع الإسلامي لفظاً تاماً وسنبحث في هذا الباب موقف الأصوليين: علماء أصول الفقه. وعلماء أصول الدين - المتكلمين - من المنطق الأرسططاليسي، ثم نعرض بعد ذلك منطقهما الخاص. وهو يتكون من مبحث الحد الأصولي - وهو بمثابة مبحث الحد عند الأرسططاليسيين - ومباحث الإستدلالات الإسلامية - وهي بمثابة مباحث القياس ولواحقه عند المناطقة الأرسططاليسيين ثم نبحث في جملة من الانتقادات التي هاجم بها علماء أصول الدين - المتكلمون - بعض المباحث الأرسططاليسية. ثم نصل آخر الأمر إلى نقطة التحول من هذا المنطق الإسلامي الخالص أي إلى المنطق الأرسططاليسي. وعلى هذا الأساس قسمت مواد هذا الباب إلى الفصول الآتية: الفصل الأول: موقف علماء أصول الفقه من المنطق الأرسططاليسي. الفصل الثاني: موقف علماء أصول الدين - المتكلمين - من المنطق الأرسططاليسي. والسبب الذي يدعونا إلى البحث في موقف علماء أصول الفقه،
We researched in the previous chapter the stance of the Islamic commentators (Peripatetics and Stoics) towards Aristotelian logic, and it became clear to us that these Islamic commentators were an extension of the Hellenistic mind in the Islamic world, such that the ties binding them to Islamic logical thinking were completely weak. For this reason: the Islamic society completely rejected them. And we will research in this chapter the stance of the usuliyyun: scholars of usul al-fiqh, and scholars of usul al-din (the mutakallimun) towards Aristotelian logic. Then after that we will present their own logic. It consists of the topic of usuli definition (hadd), which is like the topic of definition among the Aristotelians, and the topics of Islamic inferences (istidlalat), which are like the topics of syllogism and its appendages among the Aristotelian logicians. Then we will research a number of criticisms with which the scholars of usul al-din (the mutakallimun) attacked some Aristotelian topics. Then finally we will reach the point of transition from this pure Islamic logic to Aristotelian logic. On this basis, the materials of this chapter are divided into the following sections: First Section: The stance of the scholars of usul al-fiqh towards Aristotelian logic. Second Section: The stance of the scholars of usul al-din (the mutakallimun) towards Aristotelian logic. And the reason that calls us to research the stance of the scholars of usul al-fiqh...
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a methodological introduction that sets up the chapter's structure. The author first summarizes the previous chapter, which dealt with Islamic commentators influenced by Hellenistic thought—Peripatetics and Stoics—and notes that they were essentially extensions of the Greek mind, with weak connections to authentic Islamic logic, leading to their rejection by the Muslim community. Now, the author turns to the attitude of the usul scholars, both from usul al-fiqh and usul al-din (theologians), regarding Aristotelian logic. The author promises to present their own logic, which consists of two main parts: a theory of definition (al-hadd al-usuli) analogous to Aristotle's definition, and a theory of inference (al-istidlalat al-Islamiyya) analogous to Aristotle's syllogism and its appendages. Then, the author will discuss the criticisms leveled by the mutakallimun against certain Aristotelian doctrines. Finally, the chapter will trace the transition from this pure Islamic logic back to Aristotelian logic. The chapter is divided into two sections: first on usul al-fiqh scholars, second on usul al-din scholars. The passage ends mid-sentence, presumably continuing on the next page to explain the reason for studying usul al-fiqh scholars specifically.

الحد الأصولي
الإستدلالات الإسلامية
